Governor-General

His Excellency the Right Honourable Sir Ninian Martin Stephen, a Member of Her Majesty's Most Honourable Privy Council, Knight of
the Order of Australia, Knight Grand Cross of the Most Distinguished Order of St Michael and St George, Knight Grand Cross of the Royal Victorian Order, Knight Commander of the Most Excellent Order of the British Empire, Knight of the Most Venerable Order of
the Hospital of St John of Jerusalem, Governor-General of the Commonwealth of Australia and Commander-in-Chief of the Defence Force.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION
(FIRST SESSION: FIFTH PERIOD)
Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 140 of 1982)An Act to amend the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 consequent upon the enactment of the Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982.
Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 70 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Air Accidents (Commonwealth Government Liability) Act 1963.
Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 77 of 1982)An Act to amend the Air Navigation (Charges) Act 1952, and for related purposes.
Air Navigation Legislation (Validation and Interpretation) Act 1982 (Act No. 95 of 1982)An Act to make provision in relation to the exercise of certain powers and the performance of certain functions under regulations and instruments under the Air Navigation Act 1920 and for related purposes.
Appropriation Act (No. I) 1982-83 (Act No. 1160Â£]982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation Act (No.2) 1982-83 (Act No. 117 of 1982)An Act to appropriate a sum out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for certain expenditure in respect of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Appropriation (Parliamentary Departments) Act 1982-83 (Act No. 118 of 1982)An Act to appropriate certain sums out of the Consolidated Revenue Fund for the service, in relation to the Parliamentary Departments, of the year ending on 30 June 1983.
Asian Development Fund Act 1982 (Act No. lSI of 1982)An Act to authorize a further contribution by Australia to the Asian Development Bank for the purposes of the Asian Development Fund.
Australian Bicentennial Road Development Trust Fund Act 1982 (Act No. 133 of 1982)An Act to establish a Trust Fund for the purpose of the grant of financial assistance to the States and to the Northern Territory for expenditure on upgrading Australian roads in celebration of the Bicentenary in 1988, and for related purposes.
Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 126 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Stamp Duty Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 125 of 1982)- An Act to amend section 6 of the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Insurance Business) Act 1969.
Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 124 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Tax (Vehicle Registration) Act 1981.
Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 127 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Australian Capital Territory Taxation (Administration) Act 1969.
Bank Account Debits Tax Act 1982 (Act No. 141 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks.
Bank Account Debits Tax Administration Act 1982 (Act No. 142 of 1982)An Act relating to the collection of a tax in respect of certain debits made to accounts kept with banks, and for related purposes.
Bounty (Berry Fruits) Act 1982 (Act No. 132 of 1982)An Act to provide for the payment of bounty on the production of certain berry fruits.
Bounty (Ships) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 145 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Bounty (Ships) Act 1980.
Broadcasting and Television Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 154 of 1982)An Act to amend the Broadcasting and Television Act 1942, and for related purposes.
Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. ISS of 1982)- An Act to amend the Broadcasting Stations Licence Fees Act 1964.
Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 71 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Civil Aviation (Carriers' Liability) Act 1959.
Conciliation and Arbitration (Management of Organizations) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 143 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904.
Cotton Levy Act 1982 (Act No. 147 of 1982)An Act to impose a levy upon certain cotton produced in Australia.
Cotton Research Act 1982 (Act No. 146 of 1982)An Act to provide for the collection of levy imposed by the Cotton Levy Act 1982 and to establish a Cotton Research Trust Account.
Customs and Excise Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 81 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Excise Act 1901.

THE ACTS OF THE SESSION-continued
Customs Securities (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 137 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Act 1901 and the Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977 in relation to certain Customs securities, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff Act 1982 (Act No. 113 of 1982)- An Act relating to duties of Customs.
Customs Tariff (Miscellaneous Amendments) Act 1982 (Act No. 115 of 1982)An Act to make certain amendments consequent upon the enactment of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 114 of 1982)An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 consequent upon the enactment of the Customs TariffAct 1982 and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 136 of 1982)An Act to repeal section 140fthe Customs Tariff(Anti-Dumping) Act 1975, and for related purposes.
Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Amendment (Countervailing Duties) Act 1982 (Act No. 68 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Customs Tariff (Anti-Dumping) Act 1975 in relation to countervailing duties.
Defence Force (Miscellaneous Provisions) Act 1982 (Act No. 153 of 1982)An Act to amend certain Acts, and to enact transitional provisions, consequent upon the enactment of the Defence Force Discipline Act 1982, and for other purposes.
Defence Force Discipline Act 1982 (Act No. 1520f 1982)An Act relating to the discipline of the Defence Force and for related purposes.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No. I) 1982 (Act No. 109 of 1982)- An Actto amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.1) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Tax Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. II00f 1982)- An Act to amend the Diesel Fuel Tax Act (No.2) 1957.
Diesel Fuel Taxes Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 108 of 1982)- An Act relating to taxes on diesel fuel.
Dried Fruits Export Charges Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. l440f 1982)- An Act to amend the Dried Fruits Export Charges Act 1924.
Drought Assistance (Primary Producers) Act 1982 (Act No. 99 of 1982)An Act to make provison for the grant of financial assistance to primary producers who are affected by drought.
Export Market Development Grants Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 157 of 1982)An Act to amend the Export Market Development Grants Act 1974, and for related purposes.
Fertilizers (Subsidy) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 69 of 1982)An Act to amend section 3 of the Nitrogenous Fertilizers Subsidy Act 1966 and to amend the Phosphate Fertilizers Bounty Act 1963.
Health Legislation Amendment Act (No.2) 1982 (Act No. 1120f 1982)An Act to amend the Health Insurance Act 1973, the National Health Act 1953 and the Health Insurance Commission Act 1973, and for related purposes.
Income Tax (Companies, Corporate Unit Trusts and Superannuation Funds) Act 1982 (Act No. 102 of 1982)- An Act to impose a tax upon incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Individuals) Act 1982 (Act No. 101 of 1982)An Act to impose a tax upon incomes, other than incomes of companies, of corporate unit trusts and of superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Rates) Act 1982 (Act No. 105 of 1982)An Act to declare the rates of income tax payable upon incomes other than incomes of companies, corporate unit trusts and superannuation funds.
Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 103 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Mining Withholding Tax) Act 1979.
Income Tax (Rates) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 1040f 1982)- An Act to amend the Income Tax (Rates) AC11976.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.4) 1982 (Act No. 76 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.5) 1982 (Act No. 106 of 1982)- An Act to amend the law relating to income tax.
Income Tax Assessment Amendment Act (No.6) 1982 (Act No. 123 of 1982)- An Act relating to income tax.
Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 97 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Liquefied Petroleum Gas (Grants) Act 1980.
Live-stock Export Charge Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 83 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Live-slock Export Charge ACI 1977.

Northern Territory (Self-Government) Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 130 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Northern Territory (Self-Government) Act 1978.

Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 78 of 1982)- An Act relating to certain remuneration and allowances.
Repatriation Legislation Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 100 of 1982)- An Act relating to repatriation and related matters.
Royal Commissions Amendment Act 1982 (Act No. 139 of 1982)- An Act to amend the Royal Commissions Act 1902.
Salaries and Wages Pause Act 1982 (Act No. 134 of 1982)An Act to suspend for 12 months the powers of certain authorities in relation to the remuneration of certain persons, and for certain other purposes.

TAX EVASION: PROSECUTIONS
Mrs CHILD-I remind the Prime Minister of the fact that Commissioner Costigan, having expressed the view that new legislation was not necessary to act effectively against the tax evasion industry, concerning the claim that the new legislation has arrested the matter, said:
Even if it is, the past criminal activities cannot be allowed to go unpunished, and the revenue lost to be forgotten. Action is required.
I therefore ask the Prime Minister: Firstly, why have no prosecutions been launched under the Crimes (Taxation Offences) Act, assented to 20 months ago in December 1980? Secondly, how many prosecutions are in the course of preparation? Thirdly, when may we expect the first of these prosecutions to be launched?
Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Treasurer has flu this morning and is not here; otherwise I have no doubt that he would be advised on the detail of the honourable member's question. In relation to the specific question on the Crimes (Taxation Offences) Act, I will get the information for the honourable member as soon as possible.

TIMBER INDUSTRY
Mr PORTER-I refer the Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce to the troubled state of the timber industry at present. I ask: What action is the Government taking in relation to the timber industry's requests firstly, for a reference to the Temporary Assistance Authority; secondly, for anti-dumping action to be taken against imports from New Zealand; and, thirdly, a renegotiation of unfair trading practices imposed under the terms of the proposed agreement with New Zealand for closer economic relations?
Mr HODGMAN-The matter raised by the honourable member is important. Honourable members will be aware that the Minister for Industry and Commerce met with representatives of the Australian Softwood Products Council regarding the position in the industry and the Council's request for a reference to the Temporary Assistance .Authority covering sawn softwood timber as referred to by the honourable member. In addition, Sir Phillip Lynch met with representatives of the South East Trades and Labour Council of South Australia on the very same matter. He is, therefore, fully aware of the serious downturn in the industry's activity.
Mr Hurford-What is he doing about it?
Mr HODGMAN-If the honourable member listens he will find out. After studying a report from the Department on the Council's request the Minister decided that the most appropriate action at this point of time would be for the permanent heads of the relevant Australia and New Zealand departments to discuss the matter. These discussions are now under way. In making these decisions the Minister took into consideration the following factors: Firstly, New Zealand imports are currently the main source of concern; secondly, sawn timber is on schedule A of the New Zealand-Australia Free Trade Agreement; and, thirdly, the advanced stage of negotiations on the closer economic relationship between Australia and New Zealand. One objective of these discussions will be to establish clearly the reasons for the breakdown in our trading relationship with New Zealand in this area. Another objective of the discussions between the two permanent heads will be the resumption of meaningful talks between the two industries leading to a more cooperative and fruitful long term relationship.
Mr Hurford-More talks! What is he doing about it?
Mr SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member for Adelaide will remain silent.

Questions without Notice

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 959

Mr HODGMAN-It is an important matter. The honourable member should listen to the detail of the answer. This approach is regarded as more constructive and capable of achieving a quicker and more lasting solution to the present problems than a formal reference to the TAA at this stage. Imports of sawn softwood from all sources will continue to be carefully monitored so that further action can be considered immediately should increased imports from sources other than New Zealand threaten to disrupt the local industry. I advise the House that in addition to the official discussions there is progress on the softwood timber industry's dumping complaint. A senior Australian Customs officer from the department is in New Zealand at this time pursuing dumping investigations. This follows an examination of pricing and costing data which suggested apparent dumping margins of up to 49 per cent on square dressed structural softwood from New Zealand.
Mr SPEAKER-The honourable gentleman will bring his answer to a conclusion.
Mr HODGMAN-Mr Speaker, the answer would be incomplete without--

Mr HODGMAN-To assist the House, I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a document containing the balance of the information which I wish to give to the House.
Leave granted.
The document read as follows-
The Department will be finalising its investigation as soon as possible and preparing a report for my consideration. With regard to the honourable member's reference to CER and unfair trading practices, I understand that the industries' dissatisfaction with the proposed CER arrangements largely relates to the transition period during which export incentives and import licensing will continue to apply. The industries acknowledge that, when fully implemented, CER will benefit the forest products industries by removing the main causes of imbalance in New Zealand's favour in the current trading relationship.
Following a series of consultations and discussions with the Australian forest products industries, the Department facilitated a meeting between the industries of the two countries. At this meeting in Auckland on 21 and 22 July, the Australian industry delegation sought the early removal of export incentives and import licensing. While it is still too early to predict confidently the outcome of this meeting, since talks between industry sectors in the two countries are continuing, the New

Zealand industry is well aware of the depth of the Australian concern. I believe that throuol, continued industry-to-industry consultations, supported wilcre necessary by -contact between officials, it should be possible to arrive at a position acceptable to both sides.

INTERNATIONAL LABOUR ORGANISATION
Mr ROCHER-My question is directed to the Minister for Employment and Industrial Relations. Has Australia's delegation to the International Labour Organisation requested application of ILO conventions No. 87, dealing with the protection of the right to organise, and No. 98, protecting the right of workers to organise without employer interference and to bargain collectively, following the suppression of Poland's free trade union organisation, Solidarity? Will the Government seek an investigation by the ILO governing body's Committee on Freedom of Association? Will the credentials of future Polish delegates to the ILO be challenged by Australia's delegates? Will Australia draft a resolution condemning the suppression of Solidarity and actively pursue its introduction and adoption by thelLO?
Mr MACPHEE-The Australian Government ratified and supports the Conventions to which the honourable member referred. We have in fact been concerned about the matters raised in

960 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Questions without Notice

the question. Convention No. 87, concerning freedom of association and protection of the right to organise, and Convention No. 98, concerning the right to organise and bargain collectively, involve basic human rights and are central to the International Labour Organisation. The right of individuals to make their own decisions about the organisations they wish to join or not to join is a fundamental one. In December 1981, complaints were lodged with the ILO against the Polish Government by the International Confederation of Trade Unions and the World Council of Labour regarding breaches by Poland of the principles of freedom of association and, in particular, Convention No. 87, which Poland had ratified.
Following several communications between the ILO and the Polish Government, the governing body of the ILO, of which the Australian Government, employers and workers are members, in its February and March session considered an interim report prepared by the Freedom of Association Committee on matters raised in the complaint. It stressed the extreme gravity of the allegations and it pointed to the detention of unionists for reasons connected with their activities to defend the interests of workers with a serious interference with civil liberties and trade union rights. All Australian members in the governing body-worker, employer and government-supported the adoption of the report of the Committee and the governing body further considered the matter in its May meeting and again urged the Polish Government to respect Convention No. 87 which has been ratified by Poland. During the annual convention of the ILO in June of this year, the Committee on the Application of Conventions and Recommendations considered the suspension oftrade union activities in Poland and recognised that this was a particularly serious case. It also noted that the general suspension of trade union activities constituted a serious infringement of Convention No. 87 and called for a continuation of co-operation between the ILO and the Polish Government. The whole of the Australian delegation supported that report and the Australian Government will continue to do all that it can through the ILO, to bring pressure upon the Polish Government.
Mr Scott-I ask that the Minister table the document.
Mr MACPHEEI table the document.

TRADE UNION ACTIVITIES

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Costigan report indicates quite clearly that trade unions in this country, as other organisations of significance within the Australian community, need to have

Questions without Notice

their activities governed and determined to a significant extent by laws which may be directed specifically to the institutions themselves or, if not that, by the normal laws of the Commonwealth or of a State. PARLIAMENTARY ZONE: PROPOSED WORKS
Report and Ministerial Statement
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory)-Pursuant to section 5 of the Parliament Act 1974 I present proposals for the construction of roads, bridges and landscape elements required to complete the setting for the new Parliament House and link it with the balance of the parliamentary zone and Canberra's metropolitan road network.
I seek leave to make a brief statement.
Leave granted.
Mr HODGMAN-The National Capital Development Commission in consultation with the Parliament House Construction Authority has developed a draft program of works to facilitate access to the new Parliament House. Included in the works is the construction of roads and relocation and augmentation of services. Also included is the establishment of the formal land axis along the line between Capital Hill and Camp Hill envisaged by Walter Burley Griffin and reflected in the design of the new Parliament building and its main entrance forecourt. Consideration of the various proposed works is being sought from the Joint Committee on the New Parliament House. When its report is tabled I expect to be in a position to take further action under the Parliament Act to obtain the approval of the Parliament.
Some of the proposed road works require a variation to the Plan of Layout of the City of Canberra and I propose to notify my intention to vary the Plan in accordance with the Seat of Government Administration Act 1910 by special gazette shortly. There will follow a period for the lodgment of public objections to the variations after which the Joint Committee on the Australian Capital Territory will consider the proposals and report to Parliament.

EDUCATION
Mr DAWKINS (Fremantle) (11.07)-1 move:
That this House calls on the Government to re-establish the needs principle in relation to the funding of schools in Australia to ensure that all Australian children have an equal opportunity to the highest standard of education.
The House will be aware that the needs principle was first adopted by the Australian Labor Party as its policy in the middle 1960s and was first implemented by a Federal Government when the Labor Government was elected at the end of 1972. Following that election the Interim Committee for the Australian Schools Commission, under the chairmanship of Professor Karmel, was established to examine the needs of all schools in Australia and to make recommendations about how those needs ought to be addressed and how the Commonwealth might contribute financially to meeting the needs as exposed by that Committee. The recommendations of that Committee were first put into effect early in 1973 and, at the same time, plans were put in place to establish the Schools Commission.
The Schools Commission was designed to be a permanent body which would continually examine the needs of schools in Australia and make recommendations about how the Commonwealth might financially assist all schools in Australia in relation to meeting those needs. We have now seen the Schools Commission operating successfully ever since. However, over recent times, the Schools Commission has been burdened by the redefinition which this Government has employed in relation to its operations and in relation to the funding recommendations which it makes to the Government and the programs which the Government has required the Schools Commission to implement. What we have is a gross distortion of the original needs policy to the point where it is scarcely recognisable any more as a needs principle. What has to be recognised is that the needs principle, as established and defined by the Labor Party and first put into operation by the Labor Party, was based very firmly on the notion of equality of opportunity as far as the education of Australian children was concerned. We took the view that it was necessary to provide Commonwealth assistance to schools in Australia to ensure that all children, regardless of whether they came from wealthy or poor backgrounds, regardless of whether they were in poor communities and regardless of whether they were in government or non-government schools, should have an equal opportunity to receive the highest possible standard of education.

Education

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 969

However, what we now have is this Government's pre-occupation with privilege and its determination to entrench that privilege has meant that the notion of equality has been sacrified. The funding policies of this Government have worked very much to the advantage of the wealthier schools in Australia and very much to the disadvantage of the poorest schools in Australia, whether they be government or non-government. The Commonwealth's contribution to the running costs of government schools throughout Australia has fallen from 10 per cent under the last Labor Government to 7 per cent under this Government. That means that there has been a decline in the Commonwealth's contribution to the funding of government schools throughout Australia. As well as that, if one looks at the total amount of funds for schools throughout Australia, one sees that the share of Commonwealth funds for government schools has slipped from 60 per cent under the last Labor Government to less than 50 per cent now. It is our view that this is a complete distortion of the original intentions of the needs policy. It really overlooks the very serious problems that exist in relation to schooling in Australia and the challenges which lie ahead.
This sort of distortion has taken place and this drift has occurred away from the government school sector largely because of the funding guidelines which the Government has sent to the Schools Commission and because of some deliberate policy decisions which the Government has taken in relation to the funding of nongovernment schools. In the first place, the basis of funding non-government schools is that these schools are entitled to a percentage subsidy based on the standard running costs of government schools in this country; that is, depending on whether the non-government schools are determined to be in group 1, 2 or 3, they receive a subsidy which ranges from 20 per cent for the wealthiest schools to 40 per cent for the poorest schools in Australia. The percentage of the subsidies has been increased under this Government. The most rapid increase in subsidy has gone not to the schools in greatest need, not to the poorest non-government schools in this country, but rather to the wealthiest schools in this country which already enjoy a very high standard of resources and an infinitely higher standard than most non-government schools.
What has happened is that as the running costs of government schools have increased the subsidies to non-government schools have automatically increased. On top of that, as a result of the increasing of the percentage which is applied to the standard running costs, the per capita grants

going to non-government schools have increased. This has had the effect in terms of the rate of increase in those subsidies going to non-government schools of increasing the subsidies to the wealthiest schools by three times the rate for government schools and nearly twice that for the poorest Catholic school in Australia. This has meant that, notwithstanding the fairly substantial increases in subsidies to non-government schools, a serious gap still exists in relation to the resources of the poorest non-government schools and government schools as a whole.
The intention was that the Federal contribution should be used to lift the resources of nongovernment schools so that they approached at least the resources which existed in government schools. But if we look at the Catholic system, which after all accounts for 80 per cent of the non-government schools enrolments in this country, we find that the average Catholic school operates at 75 per cent of the resources which exist in average government schools. That 25 per cent gap which currently exists between government schools and Catholic schools is the same gap which existed 10 years ago when the needs of schools were first examined by the Karme1 Committee. It is to that problem of that gap that the Labor Party would want to direct most of its attention. We believe it is important for the Commonwealth to continue to contribute to nongovernment schools in order that the standards in those schools can be lifted to the level of resources which exists in government schools.
The gap which exists in relation to the poorer schools in this country-the gap between those schools and government schools-has to be set against the other gap which exists between the most wealthy schools in Australia and average government schools. If I can give a few figures to indicate the extent of that gap I think we will be able to see the way in which this Government's education policies have really distorted, in a very gross way, the needs principle which was established in 1973. If we look at the average running costs of secondary State schools we find that there is an expenditure of a little over $2,000 a pupil. About $2,000 is spent on each pupil in an average government secondary school. We can then look at what resources are available to a private secondary school in level I, recognising that level 1 no longer exists as a discreet level any more and that all the level 1 schools are now in an expanded group 1. If we take just those schools which last year would have been in level 1 we find that the level of private income available to those schools, on average, is $2,500 a pupil.

970 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Education

We find that the average expenditure in government schools is about $2,000 a pupil whereas the private resources available to the average of the most wealthy non-government schools amount to $2,500. On top of that, to that private income has to be added a 20 per cent subsidy from the Commonwealth and a further approximately 20 per cent subsidy-it varies from State to State-from the State governments. As a result of those additional subsidies which come from public sources we are beginning to see that the extra resources available to these wealthy schools rarely exceed by a very large factor the resources available to the average government secondary school. That gap now is running in the order of 63 per cent-that is the gap which exists because of the additional resources which are available to the most wealthy non-government secondary schools in Australia.
It seems to us that there is no justification at all for the public to continue to subsidise resources in those wealthy schools when the resources in those schools exceed by such a lavish amount the resources which are available to government schools at a time when there still exists a substantial gap between the resources available to the poorest non-government schools and those available to government schools. What we believe in relation to the pursuit of a decent needs policy is that if a needs policy means anything it means that the resources which the Commonwealth can make available to schools in this country ought to be directed towards those schools in greatest need. The first task should be to assist those low resourced non-government schools to reach the level which obtains in the government system. That is the way in which we would approach this problem.
We believe that it is necessary, in relation to lifting the resources in non-government schools, to reach agreement with the non-government school systems about the appropriate level of resources which ought to exist within those schools and to work with those systems to ensure that those resource targets are achieved over a reasonable period. We also believe that there is a need to reach agreement with State governments about resource targets in their schools. Whilst it is often claimed by this Government that the targets established by the Karmel Committee in fact have been met and in some cases exceeded, and that therefore the Commonwealth contribution is not as necessary as it used to be when those schools did not operate at the level recommended by the Karmel Committee, what has to be remembered in the first instance is that the Karmel Committee recommended those targets as being the minimum

acceptable standards which ought to exist in government schools and certainly not desirable targets. Just to say that we have reached, on average, the minimum acceptable targets does not mean that there can be any decline in the contribution from the Commonwealth towards improving government schools.
Another point that has to be acknowledged is that it is the government sector where the greatest demands are being made in relation to the problems of secondary education. The Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) has been ranting-I suppose that is the only way to describe it-in recent times about the inadequacy of our schools and about the failure of schools to provide adequately for those students who he believes are not academically inclined. He believes that the secondary schools are apparently serving the academically inclined students well but not those who have different and non-academic types of interests or ambitions. I acknowledge that there is a very clear need to concentrate very directly on the problems of school retention rates because we have found that retention rates, after 25 years of increasing gradually, in recent times have turned around. We now find that more and more kids, mainly boys, entirely those in government schools, have been dropping out of school much earlier than was the case a few years ago.
It is important to find the reasons for that increased drop-out rate and to try to encourage those students who would have remained at school in earlier times to continue at school or to move into some other form of full time education, whether it be in secondary schools or in the technical and further education sector. We cannot begin to approach that problem if, at the same time, we are reducing the Commonwealth contribution to the cost of running government schools. It is in the government sector where most of these problems are showing up. It is in the government sector where retention rates are lowest. The retention rate is as low as 10 per cent in some secondary schools. That is to be compared with retention rates of 90 per cent and above in many nongovernment schools. We believe that there is a need to concentrate very precisely on the problems of retention rates in government secondary schools and to provide those schools with the sort of additional assistance which is necessary for them to provide an enriched, diverse curriculum to ensure that they provide the sort of education that secondary students obviously want.
In this context, however, it is important to allay one myth. If one listened to the Prime Minister one would believe that it is only the low achievers who are dropping out of school early. That is

Education

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 971

simply not true. One of the most alarming factors in recent times has been the increase in the dropout rate amongst those students who are in the top achievement quartile. There has been a very substantial increase in the number of bright students dropping out of secondary schools. It is difficult to identify all the reasons for that but one of the important factors, of course, is simply the state of the job market. Many of the brighter kids decide that they will get out of school earlier and get the few jobs which are available at that time rather than stay on in school for an extra couple of years with indifferent employment prospects at the end. That has a number of important implications for this country. In the first place it means that we are losing the contribution that could otherwise be made by the students who are clearly able to benefit from continuing education. It also means that a lot of other students who do not have a large range of job opportunities in front of them are pushed out of the jobs which they normally would have gone to into the dole queues. That is a very important question which really has to be addressed. We cannot do so if at the same time we are starving the government schools of funds. We need to concentrate on the problem of retention rates. That will mean giving extra assistance for those schools, particularly government schools, which are experiencing that problem.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIlar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired. Is the motion seconded?
Mr FREE (Macquarie) (11.26)-1 second this motion with some pleasure because it is a singularly appropriate motion to be discussing in what may be the final weeks of this Parliament. At a time when we are witnessing this elitist Government in its death throes it is important to recognise that it is this Government's commitment to elitist education policies that has frustrated the application of the needs policy that would have provided equality for all Australian children. That policy was frustrated by the Liberal and National Country parties even during the period when the Australian Labor Party was in office. The Interim Committee for the Australian Schools Commission was appointed by the Whitlam Government to make recommendations as to the immediate needs of schools, priorities within those needs and appropriate measures to assist in meeting those needs. The Committee concluded that it should make its assessment of needs on a quantitative basis. It defined need in those days as the need for a minimum quantity of resources in schools and constructed an index to measure the input of such resources. The index

measured recurrent resources in schools by converting expenditure on salaries and other recurrent items to a standard equivalent using weights to make the figures comparable for all government school systems.
There was a separate index for primary and secondary school systems. The base of the index was the average recurrent resources in government schools in 1972, and this was represented as 100. As this was the average level of resources in government schools there were obviously some state systems which were either above or below this level. The Committee then examined the practicalities of overcoming the worst schooling deficiencies in a reasonably short period. It concluded that the minimum acceptable degree of improvement would require an overall average increase of at least 40 per cent in the use of recurrent resources per pupil in government primary schools and 35 per cent in government secondary schools by 1979. The Committee based its funding recommendations for government schools on this analysis. For 1974 and 1975 it recommended funds aimed to go about one-sixth of the way in each year towards reaching the 1979 targets. The Whitlam Government accepted these recommendations.
The Committee adopted the same approach towards the recurrent funding of non-government schools. It divided non-government schools into two categories-the Catholic systemic schools and the non-systemic schools. It was found that the systemic schools were operating in 1972 at an average standard of about four-fifths that of government primary schools. It then recommended funds for 1974 and 1975 that would bring these schools one-third of the way towards the 1979 target standards it had set for government schools. With regard to non-systemic schools, the Committee classified each school into one of eight categories according to the level of resources used in the schools. This was a direct reflection of the concept of need as defined by the Committee. Schools in the top category had resources well in excess of the 1979 targets. Thus the Committee recommended phasing out assistance for these schools over 1974 and 1975. The other categories had increasing levels of subsidy according to the decreasing levels of resources in each school.
The Labor Government accepted the Committee's recommendations with one modification. It decided quite rightly to cease funding the top category of schools almost immediately rather than to phase out such funding. However, the States Grants Schools Act 1973 which authorised the grants recommended by the Committee was amended by the then Opposition, the Liberal and

972 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Education

National Country parties, in the Senate to ensure that the grants for the top category of schools, the resources of which were already in excess of target, were maintained. With the establishment of the Schools Commission, in December 1973, that Commission endorsed the target resource standards set by the Interim Committee and maintained the quantitive approach to the assessment of needs. It developed the Schools Recurrent Resources Index-SRRI-which was a more refined version of the index created by the Interim Committee.
Although the Labor Government in 1975 did not accept the Commission's financial recommendations, it did accept some of the modifications to the subsidy scheme for non-Bovernment schools recommended by the Commission. The most important of these was the linking of nongovernment school subsidies to average running costs in government schools. This involved the calculation of a national average annual per student cost in government schools in the primary and secondary sectors. The result was referred to as 'standard cost'. Non-government schools were then ranked into six subsidy levels through the use of SRRI. The per capita grants to be received by schools at each subsidy level were expressed as percentages of standard cost. This was to have far reaching consequences because it meant that the need of non-government schools would now be measured in direct relation to actual resources in government schools rather than to target standards.
With the advent of the Fraser Government in 1975, the approach to government school funding changed. The general recurrent grants received by government schools have declined since 1976. The Liberal-National Country Party Government has used the target resource standards established by the Interim Committee as a justification for this policy. By 1977, some States had passed the targets and others had almost reached them. By the financial year 1977-78, on a six-State average, government schools were operating at 7 per cent above the targets. This rapid growth in resources was a result of increased State government funding, as Commonwealth general recurrent grants had not been increased in real terms since 1975. The Fraser Government has tried to justify the lack of increases in government school general recurrent grants on the basis that the needs of these schools as measured by the Interim Committee have been met, although the original targets were, in the Committee's own words, 'the minimum acceptable degree of improvement'.
This Government's policy on non-government school funding is based on two essential elements.

The first is that, as far as this Government is concerned, all non-government schools, irrespective of needs, should receive Commonwealth grants equivalent to at least 20 per cent of average government school running costs. The second element of the policy is that subsidies over and above this level should be provided to nongovernment schools on the basis of needs. Therefore, while this Government endorses the needs principle, it does not consider that that principle should override what it sees as the right of all nongovernment schools, regardless of their resources and the level at which they are operating, to receive support equivalent to 20 per cent of the average government school running cost.
This Government has also maintained the linkage between non-government school subsidies and government school running costs, although the Schools Commission has recommended time and again that the nexus be broken. The effect of maintaining this linkage has been to provide increasing subsidies for non-government schools. Since 1976, the Commonwealth general recurrent grants for government schools have declined. The improvement in government school resources since that year has been achieved, as I mentioned, by the States, increasing their own expenditure. As the Commonwealth recurrent grants to nongovernment schools are based on government running costs, this increased State expenditure has been directly reflected in increases in Commonwealth subsidies for non-government schools. At a time when the Commonwealth Government has been endeavouring to contain total expenditure on schools, the automatic increases in subsidies have been made up by cutting expenditure on other programs, principally capital grants for government schools. The non-government capital program has not suffered a similiar decrease. Increased Commonwealth recurrent and capital grants for non-government schools has enabled these schools to attract more students. In contrast, government school student numbers are declining.
Decreases in student numbers in government schools do not automatically result in the release of recurrent resources, particularly when these decreases in enrolments occur over a wide range of schools. Obviously, when student numbers drop and there is no equivalent fall in expenditure, expenditure per student increases; This entails further increases in non-government subsidies, as they are linked to per student recurrent expenditure in government schools. In short,

Education

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 973

there is a circular progression favouring nongovernment schools created by the current funding policies. As we increase per student expenditure in government schools from the States, that leads to increased subsidies for non-government schools. That then leads to increased enrolments in non-government schools, which in turn leads to decreased enrolments in government schools, bringing us back to the beginning of the cycle.
The Commission's attitude to the linkage has been spelt out on a number of occasions. As a result of this linkage, there has been a substantial shift of funds from the government to the nongovernment sector. In the period 1976 to 1983, grants for government schools have declined by

11.2 per cent. Over the same period, grants for non-government schools have increased by 76.9 per cent. In that period, capital grants to government schools have fallen by 35.8 per cent. The Commission has pointed out the effect of the linkage time and again. In 1977, the Commission urged the Government to reconsider its policy guidelines. In its report for the triennium 1979-81, it raised the matter again. In its report for 1980, the Commission again raised the matter. I seek leave to have incorporated in Hansard Table B3 from the report.
Leave granted.

Mr FREE-I thank the Minister for Immigration and Ethnic Affairs (Mr Hodges) and the House. The report states:
Table 83 shows the significant way in which the Commonwealth's role in school funding is changing. It shows a decline of 14 per cent in total allocations to government schools in 1980 as compared with 1976, a rise of 24 per cent in payments to non-government schools and a fall of 12 per cent in programs shared by government and non-government schools. The Commission believes these trends are a cause for concern.

The Commission raised the matter again in its Report for the Triennium 1982 to 1984. It again recommended a minimum contribution to govern-
ment schools. It recommended in that report that a basket of services, a desirable and realistic level
of resource standards, be made available. Each of these recommendations was rejected by the Government and the linkage has been maintained. The results of present policy are obvious. First of all, there is a continuing squeeze on the

974 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Education

government school sector. Secondly, large numbers of non-government schools, almost exclusively in the Catholic sector, are still badly off, some of them operating at 70 per cent of government schools resource levels. Thirdly, we have an accelerated use of public money to support private affluence in the form of wealthy private schools. I refer to the GPS schools-the greater public schools-the schools which in fact the greater public never gets into.
Mr Fisher-What percentage are they?

Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Mr COLEMAN (Wentworth) (11.42)- When I read the motion moved by the honourable member for Fremantle (Mr Dawkins) today, my first thought was that it was a pointless motion in that it is calling for the re-establishment of something that is already well established and working well. Having listened to him and the honourable member for Macquarie (Mr Free), it seems that the real animus behind the motion is not a dedication to a needs principle but some sort of hatred of the sorts of schools that the Australian Labor Party wants to see cut out of the system, particularly the church schools. The Opposition has not been particularly specific when it comes to naming the schools, but it is fairly clear that it is objecting to the church schools. The Opposition would like to see the system retained but wants to see the small minority who go to some of the church schools deprived of the sort of assistance that the Commonwealth gives. The Opposition certainly does not believe in freedom of choice and equality of treatment.

Education

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 975

The system that the Government is administering is based on several principles. It is based in most detail on the needs principle, but other principles are applied as well. We want freedom of choice in education, we want equality of opportunity and access, we want the disadvantaged to be given particular treatment and assistance-a discrimination in their favour-and we want, through a number of programs, to achieve high standards in excellence in schools. There are a number of principles. As far as funding is concerned the most important of all is the needs principle. That is qualified or supplemented by the other principles that I have mentioned.
The system is one for a basic grant to all children and for treating all children equally. We believe in equality of treatment, equality of opportunity and freedom of choice for parents. If that freedom of choice indicates preference for a school with a church atmosphere, it is extremely important and of fundamental importance, perhaps of the greatest importance. Nevertheless, the basic grant of 20 per cent of the standard costthat is, of the average recurrent cost of educating a child in a government school-goes to everyone. That is basic and in many cases it stops there. Applying the needs principle, further assistance is given according to whether the school is, to whatever extent, rich or poor. The school which can most meet its commitments out of its independent revenue or its private revenue gets least assistance. The school which has the most difficulty in meeting its commitments out of its own revenue gets the most.
There are three groups of schools. Group 1 looks after the pupils in independent schools, who are in a very small minority-some 10 per cent. The remaining 90 per cent of the pupils are divided into group 2 and group 3. Approximately 85 per cent of those pupils are in the group 3 category-the most needy schools category. The ones who get the most assistance are the 85 per cent of children at non-government schools in group 3. It is worth mentioning in passing that government schools would all be, in terms of their resources, in group 1. They are far ahead of the non-government group 3 schools, which are some 30 per cent below the government schools in terms of resources. That is why they call out for and get the most assistance in terms of the needs principle and the equity principle.
It is worth noting that the assistance given is 1 Iso accountable. A school receiving Commonwealth money as assistance must provide each year a statement that the subsidy has been used for the purposes intended. If it is not so used, it simply would not be continued and the school

would have to return the money. Beyond that statement of proper use, the schools now must account for the way in which they use private funds-the funds they do not get from the Government. I will quote from page 5 of the Commonwealth Schools Commission's program guidelines for 1982 as I think the question of accountability is of some importance. It is worth quoting it because it is not always understood. In paragraph 2.9, which relates to the income assessment procedure, the guidelines state:
The Commission-
That is, the Commonwealth Schools Commission-
will consider school income as well as expenditure in assessing each school for additional subsidies beyond its basic grant of 20 per cent.
This expenditure is the expenditure on resources, but the guidelines state that the Commission will also consider income. The guidelines continue:
However, assessment will continue to be based primarily on the level of recurrent expenditure from private resources. The Commission, following consultations with non-government school authorities, has developed a mechanism for the inclusion of income as a secondary consideration in the assessment procedure. State Planning and Finance Committees will be substantially responsible for its application.
Details of the procedure are said to be available from the Commission's State offices. As well as a system of funding based on the principle of varying needs-different subsidies for the different groups of schools, depending on how much each group can meet from its own resources of its total necessary expenditures-there is a great degree of accountability on the expenditure by these schools of the Commonwealth's money.
The honourable member for Fremantle and the honourable member for Macquarie, in supporting this motion, placed a great deal of emphasis on the question of government schools. They spoke as if the Commonwealth is not thoroughly committed to government schools whereas in fact it is thoroughly committed to government schools. Indeed, government schools enrol 77 per cent of total school students. For that 77 per cent of the enrolments they get 89 per cent of the government moneys spent on schools-government moneys meaning both Commonwealth and State moneys-whereas the 23 per cent of students in the non-government schools receive orrly 11 per cent of the government moneys. In the whole system of funding there is a very strong priority for government schools. Honourable members opposite can argue about the desirability of that if they want to. They can talk about deprivatisation of education, as some people do. Leaving aside abstract and philosophic questions, the fact remains that the government schools sector gets the giant

976 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Education

share of the government moneys. Indeed a pupil in a non-government school now gets from governments $850 a year towards the cost of his education whereas the pupil in the government school gets $1,950 a year towards the cost of his education. So an enormous priority is given to government schools. To imply, as do the speakers supporting the honourable member for Fremantie, that there is some downgrading of the government school sector is misleading and, in many cases, mischievous. The emphasis is on the government school sector. That emphasis is overwhelming in terms of the amount of money going there for each child.
Nevertheless, in addition to acknowledging that some 77 per cent of Australian school students are being educated in government schools, we have to acknowledge that the other 23 per cent in the exercise of freedom of choice decide that they want to go to church schools or, in some cases, nonchurch, non-government schools. Most of the 23 per cent of students are in church schools or in that position. This Government believes in helping them to exercise that freedom of choice. The Government notes, in addition, that a large number of them, particularly those attending the group 3 schools, are going to schools which are inferior to the State schools but which have appealed to the parents of those children and which in the exercise of their freedom of choice outweighs the inferior resources.
Acknowledging that position, it is obvious that a government with any sense of equity has to adopt policies which will assist those group 3 schools to reach the level of government schools. The basic consideration in the total survey of the school situation in Australia now is that government schools remain significantly ahead of the group 3 schools in terms of their resources. These schools are overwhelmingly Catholic systemic schools. It is only reasonable, if we respect this freedom of choice, that major steps be taken to help those group 3 schools to reach a higher level of resources. Of course that is what the purpose of the Government's policy is about, based very largely on the needs principle. While the nongovernment schools remain some 30 per cent below government schools in their resources this needs policy obviously must be maintained and strengthened. That is why this Government has increased the amount to be given to these group 3 schools to 40 per cent of the standard cost of educating a child in the government schools.
In discussing the funding of schools I am afraid tnat Opposition members either make a mistake or take a wrong direction almost every time that they discuss this matter. One of the mistakes that

they have made or one of the wrong directions that they have taken is to refer only to Commonwealth funding at a particular time ignoring total funding. Obviously the Commonwealth in making its decision as to how much it will spend on whatever category of school has to take into account how much is already being spent by the State governments. Of course, the State governments spend far more on government schools than does the Commonwealth. There was a time when the State governments were responsible for 100 per cent of the funding. The Commonwealth has now come in increasingly but obviously it does not turn a blind eye to what the States are doing in running their schools. The Commonwealth must adjust its spending to the amounts sperit by the State governments in their attempts to get the schools to a certain level.
If there is a big increase in funding by the States then that must be taken into account when deciding on the best use of Commonwealth money as distinct, of course, from that other category of Commonwealth money-that is, moneys returned to the States under the revenue sharing arrangements. I mention that because if honourable members wanted to include that in the amount of money spent we could easily say that the Commonwealth is responsible for the lot. But that would be an unconstitutional way of putting it. Clearly, we must take into account what the States are doing.
In any case, even allowing for that, Commonwealth total recurrent grants have greatly increased. There is not only the general recurrent grant but also the English as a second language grant to government schools, the disadvantaged category and the special education category. Commonwealth funding of State schools has gone up some 20 per cent since 1976. Even this year, in terms of ordinary general recurrent grants as opposed to total recurrent grants, it will go up again by some 2 per cent or $6m this financial year. Honourable gentlemen opposite also ignore the fact that a third of capital funding in government schools is Commonwealth money. If they want to play around with figures they could pick figures out of the triennium report which shows that capital funding to non-government schools is falling. As I have said the basic thing is to take into account not Commonwealth or State money but the total taxpayer assistance.
The real motive behind this motion seems to be not to defend freedom of choice but to undermine it. This motion is not really interested in the needs

Education

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 977

system but it delivers an attack on that small minority of people who, in the exercise of their freedom of choice at whatever personal financial sacrifice they have to make, decide to go to the sorts of schools that the Opposoition is determined to destroy or in any case, greatly weaken-that is, the group I schools. It seems to me to be a very petty and short sighted way of approaching the question of education in Australia today. We have a needs system. We are determined to strengthen it, build upon it and succeed with it. This motion seems to me to be absolutely superfluous.
Mr FISHER (Mallee) (11.56)-1 believe that this Government has shown its support for and concern about the education of our nation's youth in all our schools, whether government or nongovernment, in this year's appropriations. This responsibility has been acknowledged in the charter of the Commonwealth Schools Commission since its inception under the previous government in 1973. I see that the honourable member for Macquarie (Mr Free) has left the House. I hope that he has gone to read his 1973 Hansard because he will find there that our party did not frustrate the Australian Labor Party Government in its efforts to establish the Schools Commission. In fact we supported its establishment and development and voted on it in this Parliament.
In 1982-83 the Commonwealth will spend some $3.792m directly on education, which is an increase of some 13.5 per cent. This appropriation, in these times of economic restraint, is in the long term interests of the future well being of the country and indicates a commitment and a determination to ensure both the preservation and a continuing improvement of the government school system in this country. The Budget figures show that total recurrent funding for government schools is greater this year than last year and will be greater next year in real dollar terms. Therefore, it is not correct-as the honourable member for Fremantle (Mr Dawkins) said-that we have abandoned the needs principle. We have not abandoned the government school system.
This year we have adopted the Schools Commission recommendation that recurrent funds for government schools be increased by 2 per cent in real terms or by $6m. The Government has recognised that government school systems are experiencing cost pressures from rising salaries, superannuation and fluctuating enrolments. The Schools Commission recommendation that government schools capital be maintained in real terms has been accepted. The Commonwealth is to contribute a third of the total capital expenditure in government schools. Government support has increased in real terms at a time when government

school enrolments have declined by some 2 per cent.
I am seriously concerned at the misconceptions and misrepresentations that the Commonwealth has abandoned the government school system as opposed to the non-government school system and that the Commonwealth has favoured these schools by diverting funds from the government sector. Neither of these statements is true. In fact the truth can easily be e!;>tablished by the application of Government policy and by the Budget figures. Government policy quite clearly and intentionally does a number of things: It promotes and encourages equality of opportunity; it ensures that access to education is available to all; it assists in achieving high standards of excellence at all levels; it promotes the freedom of choice in education; and it addresses the problems of the disadvantaged, and the disadvantage, in the school system.
