Mr FISHER-I shall refer to some of these policy items and indicate the nonsense of the Opposition's general business motion here today. In pursuing the aims of equality of educational opportunity Commonwealth funding policies must take account of the pattern of resources reflected in State government expenditures which are many times greater than the Commonwealth's direct funding role involves. All of us in this Parliament should know that State governments have the constitutional responsibility for the administration and delivery of educational services. Despite this I am pleased that the present and previous Commonwealth governments have assumed a funding role for our schools.
While it can be seen that Commonwealth programs discriminate in favour of certain disadvantaged groups, it is misleading to view the Commonwealth effort in isolation. Commonwealth direct funding complements State effort with the overall objective of directing total public expenditure to maximum educational effect in terms of resource need. State educational expenditure in most cases represents up to 50 per cent of State Budgets, and the greatest proportion quite rightly goes to government schools, both primary and

978 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Education

secondary. I shall give an example of this in quoting some of the figures from the last report of the Schools Commission which indicates that government school sector recurrent funding by State governments reached $3,304.4m in 1979-80. Commonwealth recurrent funding towards government schools was $315.2m. In the capital area-this is for government schools-the State governments provided some $306.6m whereas the Commonwealth provided $129.3m. We see now how the division of responsibility changes in the funding of non-government schools. In the nongovernment school sector recurrent funding by Stllte governments was $165m whereas the Commonwealth funding was $293m. In the capital area-this is the important point which I makeState governments provided no capital funds for non-government schools. It was left to the Commonwealth Government to provide some $33.6m in 1979-80 towards capital programs in nongovernment schools.
I think the complete absence of honesty in the debate on this motion this morning is indicated by the failure to recognise the very clear areas of responsibility that exist for the State and Commonwealth governments. I also believe there has been a very real misunderstanding of the Government's role in the funding program for non-government and government schools. When the Commonwealth Schools Commission refers to standard cost it uses a figure based on the average recurrent cost per student in a government school. In 1982, for example, that standard cost was based on the recurrent cost per student of services funded from State government sources in 1979-80. This was adjusted for price movements to the time of the payment of those grants together with Commonwealth general recurrent per capita grants to the States for government schools in 1982. Standard costs for other years are calculated in a similar fashion.
In 1983, as a result of commitments made in relation to the 1980 Federal election, grants to group 3 non-government schools will rise to 40 per cent of the standard cost. The intention behind this increase is to assist those schools to move their relatively low resource standards towards more acceptable levels of resource use. For Catholic systemic schools funds are paid initially on a block grant basis to the systemic authority which then distributes the grants to individual schools on the basis of need. In each State duly constituted State planning and finance committees-this demolishes the argument of the Opposition that

there is no accountability-scrutinise the distribution of those funds to each systemic school. Systems authorities provide details of fund distribution in advance to the planning and finance committees which must be satisfied that funds will be distributed amongst systemic schools in accordance with their respective needs. Once this is done the system authority is required to account for the way in which the money is applied. State governments, as I have already said-I emphasise this point-have the constitutional and primary responsibility for government schools.
Commonwealth policy on school funding has as its central objective the provision of educational services of the highest standard for all Australians whether they be in government or nongovernment schools. In addressing this objective the Government necessarily takes into account the total resources available to both State and Commonwealth areas. State governments direct less than 5 per cent of their total school budgets to the non-government school area. Commonwealth funding in redressing this imbalance to some extent must therefore provide relatively greater assistance to non-government schools. This simple fact of basic justice seems to have been overlooked by the Opposition-perhaps deliberately. At present non-government schools, with 23 per cent of all enrolments, receive II per cent of total funds for all schools. The average resource levels of government schools have now reached the stage at which they would be classified under the Commonwealth subsidy system for nongovernment schools as being in group I, the highest resource category. Despite this each student at a government school effectively receives from taxpayers-that is, from all government sources-more than twice the amount, about $1,902, received by a student at a nongovernment school, about $824. This is as it should be.
The Commission has reported that the great majority of non-government schools have resources, on average, some 30 per cent below those of government schools. The Commonwealth's concern for these low resource schools has led to continued improvements in their subsidies, from about 30 per cent of the average government school standard cost in 1977 to 38 per cent in 1982. As I said earlier, this will increase to 40 per cent in 1983. This represents an increase in real terms from about $364 to $580 per primary and secondary student in the most needy nongovernment schools in 1977 to $562 and $913 in 1983.
Mr Deputy Speaker, I understand the very widespread concern in the Australian electorate

Education

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 979

at the significant expansion in enrolment levels in non-government schools, necessitating increased capital facilities and also the development of many new schools. This is occurring in my electorate, and I have no doubt that it is occurring in many other electorates throughout Australia. However it is wrong to suggest that this results from Commonwealth Government policy. I disagree entirely with the honourable member for Macquarie, who suggested that as we put more capital into the schools we will have increased enrolments. I believe the trend is the reverse. Increased enrolments in non-government schools are because of a variety of factors which necessitate the Commonwealth's applying more capital to them. As I have already pointed out, there are very severe income assessment procedures to define categories of and appropriate funds for non-government schools, and I reject the accusation that accountability is not applied.
I recognise the divisions that the expansion of the non-government school sector can cause. It is not good socially and it is not good for the efficient use of our resources. Several towns in my electorate, with a little over 1,000 people, have three primary schools. That cannot be good for the long term future of education. We must look at the reasons for this enormous expansion in recent years in the non-government school sector. Some teachers and some administrators by their negative attitude have convinced this nation, parents and students that our government schools are not good. This is a very negative attitude. Our government schools are good; many are outstanding. Most of our teachers and most of our administrators are dedicated and professional people. The parents involved with our government schools have a genuine involvement and a desire to see those schools and their resources improved.
I reject this motion because I believe that all it does is reinforces the belief, once again, in the community that our good government schools are lacking in some resources. I re-emphasise that the great majority of non-government schools have resources about 30 percent below the average cost of educating a student at a government school. This Government remains committed to developing side by side the non-government and government school systems. We support the fine work of government schools and the high standards set by their staff, and we will continue to do so.
Dr JENKINS (Scullin) (12.11 )-1 want to fill out the few minutes remaining in this debate on this General Business item to return to the track of re-establishing the needs principle in relation to the funding of schools in Australia. One of the

most refreshing things that happened to education in the 1970s was the adoption of the needs principle. At that time no one quite knew what it meant. The Schools Commission had to look at it and see what measures could be used to ensure that children in Australian schools had some equality of opportunity to a high standard of education. The Commission saw the needs principle as necessary to assess an appropriate level of resources; it then aimed to bring schools up to this level of resources. In looking at areas and the factors that could have some influence there, it had to look at a number of social and economic factors which played their role. It was a very important base on which to build this needs principle for the improvement of schools. Unfortunately what has happened is that there has been no reassessment of this needs principle. As has been said by my colleagues there has been a skew distribution of Federal Government financing going into particularly the wealthier, independent non-government schools.
If we have a look at the resource level that is available now we see that many schools have been brought up to and beyond the appropriate resource level. But still a large number of government and parish schools are below the level of necessary resources. They are hidden by the averaging concept. Their resources are being delayed by the government guidelines, which have been given to the Schools Commission to decide what will be done with the finance, to really effect a redistribution not just to non-government schools per se but also to what appears to be a privileged section. I think there is a strong argument that we look at the needs principle and re-establish it on a reassessment of what the minimum resources should be. That must be updated and those resources may in many cases be new.
With new technology questions arise concerning the training of people in computers and the necessity of having personal computers in our school system. Is this now seen as a necessary resource? That must be reassessed. There must be a reassessment of our co-operation with the States so that we learn what they see as the present level of needs and the degree of accountability that they are prepared, to accept in their budgets.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The time allotted for precedence of General Business has expired. The honourable member for Scullin will have leave to continue his speech when the debate is resumed.

980 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence

JOINT COMMITTEE ON FOREIGN AFFAIRS AND DEFENCE
Mr KATTER (Kennedy)-On behalf of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence, I present the Committee's report entitled 'An Aircraft Carrier for the Australian Defence Force', together with the transcript of evidence.
Ordered that the report be printed.
Mr KATTER-by leave-I thank the House and I particularly thank the Chairman of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence, the honourable member for Higgins (Mr Shipton), for the privilege of being able to table this report. Honourable members may be aware that the aircraft carrier reference was given to the Subcommittee on Defence Matters on 20 April this year. Since then the sub-committee has worked pretty intensively to meet the rather tight deadline of I September. Hearings were held at which both official and non-official witnesses gave evidence regarding acquisition of an aircraft carrier. I wish to express the sub-committee's appreciation to all those people who were involved in providing submissions.
Honourable members will appreciate just how dependent parliamentary committees are on the goodwill and assistance of their witnesses, and those citizens who, out of a sense of public duty, came forward to share their special knowledge deserve recognition for assisting an important and serious work of the Parliament, and what is more serious, the defence of this country. While not wishing by any means to make invidious comparisons I would like to record our appreciation of the assistance we have received over many years-not just with this inquiry-from the Strategic and Defence Study Centre at the Australian National University. In particular I would like to thank Dr Bob O'Neill who, regrettably, has resigned as head of the Centre in order to take up a position in London with the International Institute for Strategic Studies.
As with our previous inquiry into defence procurement, the sub-committee, during this inquiry, enjoyed the services of Commodore Ken Gray as consultant. Our thanks go to him and to Robert King, the secretary, Mr John Needham, the research officer and Mrs Maureen Rockey, the steno-secretary. I would also like to thank other staff, such as Miss Linda Rutter, Miss Robyn Graham, Mrs Brenda Jackson, Mrs Carol Casey, Mrs Barbara Johns and Mrs Gloria Stewart.
The Sub-committee on Defence Matters was diverted from its inquiry into the structure of the Australian Defence Force to inquire into the question of an aircraft carrier. It was something

different. We were given an assignment which was based on a political decision which was made in the Senate. As I indicated when I tabled the report 'Threats to Australia's Security-Their
Nature and Probability' on 18 November 1982, that report was intended as a forerunner to further reports covering particular aspects of the terms of reference to monitor the implementation of the Australian Government's announced defence programs. An inquiry into force structure was the next logical stage in this process. As a result of this background the sub-committee was singularly well prepared to conduct an inquiry into the acquisition of an aircraft carrier over a limited period. I think this helps'to explain why the Committee is able to present a unanimous report to the Parliament on what has been a most controversial subject.
The Committee developed the report in direct relationship to the three clauses of the terms of reference. Chapter I examines, in terms of the various levels of contingencies, the relevance of an aircraft carrier to Australia's current and perceived strategic environment. Chapter 2 establishes the role of an aircraft carrier in the Defence Force structure of Australia, examines its effectiveness in that role and looks at optional capabilities which could perform that role. Chapter 3 considers the effects of the purchase of an aircraft carrier on the future procurement program for the Australian defence forces.
The Committee has acknowledged that an aircraft carrier could provide many functions which would be valuable in the Australian Defence Force. The Committee is of the view also that many of the functions performed by an aircraft carrier can be performed as effectively, or at least as acceptably, by other elements of our air and maritime forces. Though it could be argued that alternative naval vessels and aircraft could perform their individual functions, an aircraft carrier would combine many of them. For example, the aircraft carrier does offer a platform which combines an anti-submarine warfare and command and control function. The report reflects also the view of the Committee that many elements of our air and maritime forces perform functions more closely related to the immediate priority of Australia's defence planning.
The Australian Government's offer, made during the Falklands conflict, to permit the British Government to withdraw from the sale of HMS Invincible. which has been accepted by the British Government, provides a fresh opportunity to review the value of an aircraft carrier to Australia's Defence Force structure and'the priority such a purchase should be accorded. The Committee has

Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 981

noted recent statements by the Minister for Defence (Mr Sinclair) that all options, including a decision not to purchase a carrier, will be con- .sidered. At the price at which HMS Invincible was to be made available, a good case could be made for its purchase. However, at the full project cost which would be involved in the acquisition of a specially designed ship, in the context of present defence spending levels, it is harder to justify such a purchase. Other desirable acquisitions would have to be curtailed or delayed unacceptably for the Australian Government to purchase such an aircraft carrier.
We have recently been reminded of the stringent economic circumstances in which Australia
is currently placed. Very hard decisions will have to be made on priorities for defence expenditure. If future defence expenditure of a much higher order than hitherto could be contemplated, an aircraft carrier could be included in the capital expenditure program without necessarily distorting the shape and balance of the Defence Force. The Committee did not anticipate that defence expenditure would rise to such a level in the foreseeable future.
The Committee identified a number of roles in which an aircraft carrier would be effective: The role in which it would be most effective is antisubmarine warfare. The Committee reached the conclusion, based on evidence received, that the most substantial requirement for anti-submarine warfare operations would be associated with the consequences of global war. However, the Committee received evidence that the contingency of anti-submarine warfare during prolonged global war, which is seen as remote, has not been a factor in recent or current planning of Australian Defence Force structure. The contingency of global conventional war is not, therefore, seen as a specific factor determining the characteristics of the forces. An aircraft carrier would be effective also in the lower level contingency situations, which are identified in the report. It is then a matter of judgment what priority should be accorded. It is a matter of fact, however, that, except in its command and control functions, an aircraft carrier is a platform rather than a weapons system.
The Committee was presented with varying project costs for the aircraft complement of a carrier. It seems likely that this will cost about $500m. The projected cost of a platform required to make these weapons systems effective would range, now that Invincible is no longer available, up to $1,OOOm or more. A major shortcoming of the anti-submarine warfare STOVL carrier is its current inability to provide airborne early warning. Unless this could be provided, a naval force

would be restricted in the information and warning that it had in respect of the approach of many aircraft or surface units, even when airborne early warning was provided by land-based aircraft. Its availability for the support of naval operations would be limited.
Regrettably, I am confined to a very strict time slot. Other matters are mentioned in my tabling report that I would like to have stressed. I think it is very regrettable that a time slot which is strictly limited is applied in a matter of such vital importance to this nation. I will be discussing the matter at another level at a future date. I am very disappointed that I was confined to five or six minutes. I went over that limit by a couple of minutes. I now have much pleasure in tabling this report. I hope that it meets with the approval of the Parliament generally and the people of Australia.
Mr MORRISON (St George)-by leaveThe report of the Joint Parliamentary Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence comes to the House in spite of the Government's tactics to prevent the Joint Parliamentary Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence from considering the matter. The Government refused to consider an amendment that I moved in this House earlier this year to refer to the Joint Parliamentary Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence the very matter that has now come back into this House. This report is before us now only because the Opposition was able to arrange for a similar motion to the one proposed in this House and not considered by it to be moved in the Senate. The Senate, despite the opposition of the Government, referred the matter to the Joint Parliamentary Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence. This is the result of that consideration.
I think that in the discussion that the Committee undertook on this report we could discern the real reasons for the unaccustomed display of courtesy and generosity by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) in allowing the United Kingdom to retain HMS Invincible. The answer for this action can be found in the Budget. The Prime Minister set out to construct an electiontype Budget. To achieve this, the defence expenditure has been slashed. It has been slashed to no more than an increase of one per cent in real expenditure. This is despite all the promises made during the Afghanistan hysteria of a real increase of 7 per cent. So, last year the Government saved $85m on the aircraft carrier. It no longer has a commitment to roughly $200m this year and a further $200m nert year. So, the result was that the Government decided that it could not afford an aircraft carrier.

982 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence

I point out that it should be not just a question of finance that is considered in the acquisition of equipment for our Services. The Committee's re-
port underlines the proposition that appears foreign to this Government. I want to make that proposition clear. It is not enough to say that a particular platform or weapon would be useful; what is essential to be analysed is the opportunity cost. By that I mean that what has to be shown in any proposed purchase is that, in terms of Australia's national security, the options opened
up by that purchase are better than those that the purchase effectively closes off. It is a matter of opportunity cost. On that criterion, the Committee came down with the conclusion that the purchase of the aircraft carrier could not be justified.
Mr Baume-A wrong conclusion.
Mr MORRISON-The conclusion, I must confess, took a lot of study. It is unfortunate that the honourable member for Macarthur does not apply himself to the same sort of study. I want to bring up a couple of points very quickly because my colleague the honourable member for Swan (Mr Beazley) who played a very important part in the deliberations will follow me with only five minutes to speak. I proposed also on many occasions in this House going back over a number of years the establishment of a maritime command comprising units of the Navy and Air Force. I have
proposed also in this House that the Tracker aircraft-this was before the Invincible purchase but when we knew that the HMAS Melbourne was going out-should be dedicated to coastal surveillance. I am delighted to say that the Committee has recognised in its conclusions the need for a maritime command structure. It has put forward the very sensible proposition that the Tracker aircraft for the remainder of their service life should be dedicated to coastal surveillance. That dedication will bring with it the highly skilled professionals of the Fleet Air Arm.
The Committee has recommended also-I want the Government to take particular notice of this-that we should consider transferring responsibility for some or all of the P3C long-range maritime patrol aircraft to the Fleet Air Arm. The P3Cs in their surveillance task work have to work closely with the Navy. I think the background and training of the Fleet Air Arm personnel mesh in well with that concept. I recommend

further that the east coast surveillance tasks that some of these P3Cs undertake be relocated to the Nowra air base. Under this Government-I notice the honourable member for Macarthur (Mr Baume) is here; he has contributed to the Government's policy-the Nowra air base is withering on the vine.
I think the recommendations put forward in the Committee's report will bring about further life for the Fleet Air Arm, which can play an important role. I regret that the Government did not see fit to foster and encourage this report from this Committee. I am delighted that the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence was able to consider the report and I believe the Government should act on that report.
Mr SHIPTON (Higgins)-by leave-The report of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence on an aircraft carrier for the Australian Defence Force is a unanimous report. As I have said on previous occasions, during my time on the Committee I have been impressed by the efforts of members on both sides of the Parliament to approach issues with open minds and to judge issues irrespective of party attitudes. It is a credit to Committee members on both sides of the House and the Senate that on a subject as controversial as this one we have a report with no dissent. I was disappointed, however, in the comments made by the honourable member for St George (Mr Morrison) at the beginning of his speech when he introduced a partisan attitude by raising matters of Budget expenditure and criticising the Government. I point out to this House that when the Senate was considering whether a reference should be made to the Committee the Government in the Senate made no objection to the reference going to the Committee. I think the reference by the Senate to the Joint Committee is a welcome expression of faith in and good will towards the Joint Committee, which comprises members of the Senate and the House of Representatives.
This report is the fourth report on defence matters presented by the Joint Committee since 1977, following a report on threats to Australia's security which was tabled in November last year. That report was praised in part at least by the former Minister for Defence, the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen), who is in the chamber today. The report on the aircraft carrier took last year's report on threats to Australia's security as the basis for its consideration of the relevance of an aircraft carrier to Australia's current and perceived environment. I think it was a timely reference to the Committee because the Committee

Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 983

had, in fact, considered the threats and the nature of threats to the Australian environment.
On the subject of threats, given the current strategic environment, the Committee's convention is that the primary function of the Australian Defence Force should be to deter all levels of threat from emerging. Whilst the current environment is seen as being relatively favourable, the Falkland Islands war has shown us that threats can emerge at short notice from unexpected areas. The general contention of the Committee was reinforced by witnesses who appeared before the Sub-Committee. The Committee further contended that, wherever possible, capabilities acquired by Australia should be relevant to deterrents and that they should have a multiplier effect. This multiplier effect would have a compound effect on any potential aggressor.
Multiplier effects are very important. One is that the existing Fill force and the projected FA 18 force have a potent strike-including maritime strike-potential. However, this potential would be considerably enhanced if they could carry out strikes to greater ranges than can be achieved with their present fuel capabilities. This could be attained by the acquisition of an in-flight refuelling capability. I have long advocated the acquisition of an in-flight, mid-air refuelling capability and it could be provided by converting Boeing 707 aircraft at a relatively small cost. I thank the House for its indulgence.
Mr BEAZLEY (Swan)-by leave-I think this report of the Joint Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence on an aircraft carrier for the Australian Defence Force is an important contribution to our understanding of this issue. In a disciplined manner it considers the value of an aircraft carrier to a comprehensive series of wartime contingencies Australia might confront. In so doing it abandons the vague romanticism that has bedevilled much of the discussion on this matter to this point. As an aspect of the force structure necessary to deal with the threats arising from global contingencies through to the threat of invasion, raids and low level terrorist-type problems, a carrier has been systematically considered by the Committee in a bipartisan fashion and found wanting or, if not found wanting, other weapons systems have been found adequate to perform the carrier's functions.
It is important to remember that it is not just an argument about the economies of defence. Economic questions certainly influence the implementation of priorities but the list of priorities established by the Committee, using largely Department of Defence sources, would not rate

the purchase of a carrier very highly. The Committee has had a cold hard look at STOVL aircraft and has not found them sufficiently valuable to rate their purchase and a platform for them above improving the effectiveness of our land-based maritime strike and surveillance capability. That includes all aspects of its air bases, airborne early
warning and in-flight refuelling. The Committee has gone into some considerable detail on that. I draw attention to the Committee's noted concern about delays in the development of air bases in the north of Western Australia and, for that matter, in the north of Australia generally, from which such aircraft could be deployed.
I also draw the attention of the House to one other feature of the Committee's report and that is the question of the future of the Fleet Air Arm. It is my belief and the belief of a number of members of this Committee that there has been an absence of systematic attention given to the future role of that Air Arm within Australia's defence forces irrespective of whether there is a carrier in the Australian naval force structure. We visited the Fleet Air Arm base at Nowra and had some substantial discussions with the officers there. I rather think that it is time for politicians with a direct interest in this matter, as well as those with an indirect interest, to cease chasing after shadows. The Government has quite clearly excluded from this Budget the possibility of the purchase of an aircraft carrier in the immediate or not too distant future. Thus we should start to come to grips with the question of what the future of the Fleet Air Arm should be.
The Committee makes some specific suggestions. It suggests the transfer of the Orions-some or all of them-to the Fleet Air Arm as part of a maritime command structure. It suggests the usage for coastal surveillance of the Grumman Trackers that are in storage and it mentions the valuable functions they performed in the mid-1970s in the Broome area. The Committee seeks to take out of cold storage the A4 Skyhawks because of the substantial life they still have left in them and because of their valuable role for both training and combat purposes in the rest of the Fleet Air Arm and for training with naval ships practising co-operation with air power in a maritime context. We believe that this can be done only in a appropriately structured maritime command structure. The Committee advocates that in its recommendations.
I thank the staff of the Committee who have co-operated in producing this report very rapidly. The Chairman and the Government members of the Committee took this matter very seriously. The Committee produced a unanimous report

TAXATION
Discussion of Matter of Public Importance
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr MiIIar)-Mr Speaker has received a letter from the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) proposing that a definite matter of public importance be submitted to the House for discussion, namely:
The maldistribution of the tax burden between wage and income earners and others.
I call upon those members who approve of the proposed discussion to rise in their places.
More than the number o/members required by the Standing Orders having risen in their places.
Mr LIONEL BOWEN (Kingsford-Smith) (12.38)-The matter which is before the House relates to the tax burden now being foisted on the majority of Australians who are classed as pay-asyou-earn taxpayers. I emphasise that this relates directly to the Government's failure in respect of matters which have been highlighted in the last 48 hours in this House by what is known as the Costigan report-the report of the Royal Commission on the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union. What it means is that, because of the Government's failure to prosecute people who are guilty of criminal conduct in the sense that they are not paying their taxes, we now have a tax evasion-avoidance industry which is deemed to be in the range of billions of dollars. In fact, it could be to such an extent that if we were to give a refund to every pay-as-you-earn taxpayer in Australia we could probably give them about $3,000 each. That shows the magnitude of the problem.
Today I do not want to address my remarks to the question of guilt, which is obvious, but to the statistics which should have been obvious. This question arises in Costigan's report where he talks about the enormous growth in the taxable incomes of individuals and the very slight increase in the taxable incomes of private companies. The Labor Party's economic committee, to which I give credit, made the point that in 1965-66 nonPAYE taxpayers paid one third of total personal income tax. In 1981-82 their contribution had dropped to 18 per cent. Those figures indicate the change in the tax share which has taken place.

There was a reduction in the burden for nonPAYE taxpayers and an enormous increase in the burden of every average Australian who has to pay tax as he earns his wages. This is completely unfair. The Government should have done something about it. It has avoided the issue and, because of bungling and ineptitude in the ministerial area, it is now faced with trying to prosecute people and recover that money.
It is obvious why the Government did not do anything about it when it had the statistics in front of it. Obviously, it did not want to do anything. Mr Costigan, in his report, was able to identify immediately a major increase in the number of companies in State corporate affairs registries. He produced the Victorian figures. There has been an enormous increase in company registration in all States. I have been able to get hold of some figures for New South Wales. They show that in New South Wales between 1976 and 198071,350 new companies were registered. However, the number of annual returns lodged by companies incorporated in New South Wales, which is the normal requirement under State law, rose by only 22,000. So, nearly 50,000 companies were not performing at all. In other words, they were probably the bottom-of-the-harbour group. In a period of four years nearly 50,000 such companies seem to have been established in New South Wales alone. If that is the extent of tax evasion and avoidance in New South Wales-the only real inquiry we have had has been into 923 companies in Victoria-is it not fair to assume that billions of dollars are being lost in tax revenue? Mr Eric Risstrom was quoted as saying, before the Costigan report came to light, that about $7 billion a year in tax revenue was being lost. The graphs in the Costigan report indicate that the increase in the tax burden on PAYE taxpayers, in an endeavour to make up the loss, has been very substantial. They confirm the figures which I have given.
In the few minutes available to me I want to make it clear what the Government has to do. It should immediately try to make the people who have obtained these ill-gotten gains disgorge what they are not entitled to. Untold wealth of massive magnitude is owing to the Commonwealth. The rest of the community has to make it up. Let me refer to other statistics which are available for resident and non-resident taxable private companies. The number of such companies dropped to 88,652. 63,720 in the period 1973-74 to 1978-79. Because the number decreased, the taxable income of those companies obviously did not go up much. The taxation they paid increased from $685m to $745m over a period of about six years. Let us look at what happened in the same

Taxation

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 985

period to individuals who had their tax deducted. Their taxable income increased. The companies' taxable income did not increase, partly because the number of companies decreased which is extraordinary. But PAYE taxation increased from $5 billion to $14 billion, making up the leeway. The statistics should have been obvious to the Government. If it had wanted to it could have looked at the records of the Australian Taxation Office or any of the State corporate affairs statistics.
The number of resident and non-resident nontaxable private companies increased from 98,000 to 130,000. The companies that were not paying tax mushroomed by 32 per cent over this period. It is extraordinary to think that there was a 32 per cent increase in non-taxable private companies, whereas the number of taxable private companies declined by 28 per cent. People can get lost in trying to remember those figures. The most simple aspect is revealed in the 1981-82 Budget. It stated that PAYE taxes would increase by 21.9 per cent. In fact the Government increased them by 23.2 per cent. It thought that company tax would increase by 10 per cent but it increased by only 7.6
per cent. Surely it was obvious to the Taxation Office and the Treasury that they were not getting sufficient taxation revenue from companies over the years. In the end they were virtually preparing Budgets on the basis that the only way to get it was from the poor, unfortunate weekly wage or salary earner whose tax is deducted in advance.
In an earlier report of some months ago Mr Costigan said that he had had discussions with the Deputy Commissioner of Taxation in New South Wales and the Commissioner of Taxation and the two First Assistant Commissioners of Taxation in Canberra. The Taxation Office informed him that it had a great deal of information which caused it to conclude that criminals were involved in tax schemes. It is obvious that the Commissioner of Taxation, the Government and the Treasury knew that revenue was not coming in and that this was related to criminal activity. We will not go back to that argument now, but surely one would have thought that a prudent government and a reasonably active Treasury would have known that the reason they were not able to collect the revenue over a period of years was massive tax evasion and avoidance. The Australian Labor Party was able to ascertain that before it could get the figures which I have cited.
I ask the Government what it will do to stop the continuation of this activity. It will not stop it by merely lodging a prosecution. These hardened white-collar people will hang on to that money and fight through the courts. We expect to get the

money. In fact if retrospective legislation comes in full blast we hope to get double. These people ought to be fined as well as sent to gaol. They still have the money. We are not too worried'-ttbout their personal freedom. From the taxpayers' point of view the issue is what will be done by this Government. Will it just ignore the matter and hope that something turns up? It will not do that. Where is the Government's policy? It talks about legislation which is basically unnecessary in many ways, particularly from the criminal point of view. There is ample evidence to suggest that the existing law is adequate. If the Government wants retrospectivity that will be just an added string to its bow but it should not let it become a shield or a method of hiding from the public the fact that something should have been done earlier. We submit that it should have and there is evidence to prove that.
Is it not time that this Government developed a strategy for collecting the tax as and when it becomes due from those who are getting ill-gotten gains? Is it not important to devise the same method of collection as that which now applies to PAYE taxpayers? In other words, why not tax the income at the source of payment? That should not be beyond the wit of normal people. Whether the Government can find time to introduce ap-
propriate legislation to do that I am not too certain but it would not be very hard. Because of the massive tax evasion which has gone on and the fact that PAYE taxpayers have been burdened and will continue to be burdened by white collar crime which is not being effectively dealt with it is about time the Government developed new procedures so that when the payer makes the payment the appropriate amount of taxation is deducted from it and remitted forthwith to the Taxation Office. That would guarantee at least the collection of some money when it was due and payable, whether it be from the banks, from stockbrokers or in some other way. Also, transfers of shares should bear a charge in respect of tax that mayor may not be payable. The principle which always applied in the days of what was called federal estate duty was that one could not transfer assets after a death if the federal estate duty had not been paid because there was a charge on the transfer of the assets. I think it appropriate that we do something about this.
It is no good blaming the increase in wages for the increase in this amount of PAYE tax revenue. In the year 1980-81 gross pay-as-you-earn taxation payments increased by 23.2 per cent, but average weekly earnings increased by only 14.8 per cent. This indicates that PAYE tax payments increased by \0 per cent more than average

986 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Taxation

weekly earnings. It must be remembered that 60 per cent of wage and salary earners receive less than average weekly earnings. In 1981-82 gross PAYE receipts represented almost 50 per cent of total taxation revenue. In 1981-82 gross PA YE receipts totalled $18.8 billion. This year PAYE taxpayers will be socked a further $3 billion. In other words, PAYE receipts will go up to $21.1 billion. Why? Because the Government has failed in the collection of tax due to it. It has allowed all the criminals, white collar and others, to run away with schemes which on the statistics were very obvious as far back as four years ago. It is obvious that there was legal advice that something should have been done then. Why should we continue to perpetrate a system which clearly indicates that the PAYE taxpayer will still have to bear the burden? So apart from collecting the revenue due, and the double amounts which are the appropriate penalty under section 231 of the Income Tax Assessment Act, what about developing some new concepts of collection so that there is a guarantee that the money is not given into the hands of these people but is paid to the Taxation Office first? We appreciate that now people who dispute their tax liability are at last obliged to pay money in advance. If they win their case, they get the money back. That is a healthy improvement. But I come to the point: Where is the Government's policy to ensure that as from now people who are getting income and not paying tax on it will be obliged to make some contribution immediately that income is received? That would guarantee that there would be no continuation of the loss of billions of dollars. It would guarantee that the burden on PAYE taxpayers would be reduced. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard the statements that I mentioned. They relate to company registrations, annual returns, resident and non-resident private companies and tax paid by individuals.

Mr LIONEL BOWEN-I thank the House.
Dr EDWARDS (Berowra) (12.53)-It is clear that the Opposition is bent on extracting the maximum mileage it can out of the report of the Costigan Royal Commission on the Activities of the Federated Ship Painters and Dockers Union. I remind the Opposition that that report was commissioned by this Government jointly with the previous Victorian Liberal Government. We do not underrate in any way the gravity of the findings of that report. Indeed, the Government has reacted strongly to the report. A special task force has been established and it will pursue with

the utmost vigour the matters to which the Deputy Leader of the Opposition (Mr Lionel Bowen) referred, He succeeded in creating a totally wrong impression-that only Costigan perceived the trends to which he referred. The truth of the matter is that the Government has already perceived these trends. For instance, the Treasurer (Mr Howard) announced in July his intention to legislate with a view to recovering, if necessary from former shareholders, moneys properly owing to the Government. That action long preceded the Costigan report to which the Deputy Leader of the Opposition referred. In every way this Government has a record second to none for recognising, publicising and adopting measures to counter tax avoidance and evasion.

Dr EDWARDS-I thank the House. It will be very obvious from that table just when the mass-
ive change to increased reliance on personal income tax contributions first occurred. In 1972-73, for instance, the proportion of total tax receipts raised from personal income taxpayers was 48.2 per cent; by 1974-75 it increased to 54.8 per cent; and it was a similar percentage in 1975-76-very nearly 55 per cent. I concede that there was a peak last year, 1981-82, of 56.2 per cent, but the projected percentage of total receipts for 1982-83 shows a reduction to 55.8 per cent. That reduction would be significantly greater but for the fact that the taxation measures announced in the Budget take effect only from 1 November. Therefore, we recognise the large incidence of personal income tax contributions as a proportion of total tax revenue and have already moved to reduce that percentage.
I take the opportunity of this debate to stress the character of the personal income tax changes which have been effected in this Budget. We are talking about the distribution of the tax burden. The Deputy Leader of the Opposition spoke about certain aspects to which I will come back, but I want to speak about other aspects of this alleged maldistribution of the tax burden. If we consider the tax changes announced in the current Budget the basic change is the increase in the tax threshold-the level of income below which no tax is payable and after which tax is payablefrom $4,195 per annum to $4,595 per annum, an

increase of $400. This is combined with an increase in the spouse rebate for families with dependent children which represents an increase in disposable income of $200, or roughly $4 a week, and there is the rebate which has been granted to those paying off a mortgage. With a typical mortgage of the order of $25,000 and interest at 13.5 per cent per annum, the result of these reductions in tax, the housing rebate and the increase in family allowances of $4.25 a week, is a total saving for a taxpayer with a dependent spouse and two dependent children and earning $300 a week of $19.84 a week, nearly $20 in round figures. This represents an effective increase in the disposable income of such a family of the order of 7.8 per cent. If we look at the measures as applied to a person on a much lower income of $200 a week, the increase is nearly 10 per cent.
Sitting suspended from 1 to 2 p.m.

Dr EDWARDS-I thank the House. The table shows quite clearly that for a taxpayer with a dependent spouse and two dependent children the aggregate of the benefits provided in this Budget, though not allowing for the impact of the indirect tax increases, represents an effective increase in disposable income of 9.6 per cent-virtually 10 per cent-for the low income earner on $200 a week and a smaller proportionate increase of 5.2 per cent for the high income earner. The point I make is that such a change, providing proportionately more for low and middle income groups, is the sort of fair go, in tax change that most Australians would want to see. I stress that this thrust is an integral part of the Government's overall strategy in this Budget. For the typical taxpayer to get those benefits-of the order of $17 to $20 a week-through his wage or salary he would require a gross salary increase of $25 and upwards, or something like 9 per cent. What we need is some reasonable co-operation from the trade union movement in dampening wage claims-and I am optimistic that, despite the reaction on Budget night of the President of the Australian Council of Trade Unions, Mr Cliff Dolan, who described the Budget as 'horrible' there will be a change in stance in that respect. These changes are at once anti-inflationary as the employer will not be subject to the increase in gross wage costs which would otherwise occur. They will also uphold activity and employment. The employer, not being subject to that gross wage increase, will be able to stay in business and maintain existing and provide additional jobs.
The next point to which I refer relates more closely to that raised by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition. I refer to the difference that we have observed in recent years in the growth of receipts from the PAYE taxpayer as against other personal taxpayers. Again, I make the point: It has not taken Costigan to draw the attention of the Government to the trend referred to by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition. It is recognised by the Government. The estimates of total receipts on page 245 of Budget Paper No. I show that it is anticipated that the receipts of income tax from individuals other than PAYE taxpayers will increase by nearly 15 per cent compared with only an 8.9 per cent increse for PAYE taxpayers

because of the tax measures that I have outlined. The 15 per cent increase reflects several matters. It reflects the determination of the Government that its tax avoidance measures will bite; and bite they will. It begins to reflect also the additional measure that the Government has proposed, that is, to institute a procedure for the taxation of cash payments. A document on that subject has been prepared with great thought and is indicative of the way in which this Government has been continuously concerned with tax avoidance and tax evasion. Doubtless, an important part of the trend referred to is the increasing resort by individuals not only to form themselves into companies of which they are both employee and shareholder, thereby resorting to legitimate-if I may use that word-forms of tax avoidance, but also to significant engaging in tax evasion of the cash-inhand or cash economy variety.
I do not enlarge on the other principal point raised by the Deputy Leader of the Opposition. He suggested that there was a disparity between the registration of companies with the various State corporate affairs commissions and the comparitive growth of income that might be attributable to those companies. There are so many factors involved in that matter that the one on which he put emphasis, again making reference to Costigan, would be the smallest. I say that without in any way substracting from the gravity of those aspects.
The final point I make in the debate on this matter of public importance is that Australia's tax system relies very heavily on personal income tax and only to a very small extent on consumption taxes-taxes at the point of spending. Australia ranks fourth out of 22 member countries of the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development in its dependence on personal income tax and sixth in relation to corporate taxes. We rank second out of 22 countries in those two forms of direct taxation taken together. In contrast, we rank twenty-first out of 22 countries in relation to consumption taxes. This shows Australia's relatively low dependence on such taxes. It is the Government's policy to move to less reliance on personal income tax and more on consumption

990 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Petitions

taxes. The Australian Labor Party has the opposite policy. It is the party of high personal taxation.
There is a variety of good reasons for the Government's policy, but the overriding one is precisely that taxes imposed at the point of spending-consumption taxes-are more difficult to avoid or evade than income tax. The Government has already taken important steps in this direction. Everybody, including the beneficiaries of the cash economy, the tax evader and the capital gainer-that should please the Opposition's heart-are caught at the point of spending. This Government has embarked on a gradual process of reducing personal income tax and increasing indirect taxes with a view to making everyone pay his or her fair share of taxation. It is important, in connection with the question of tax avoidance and tax evasion that was raised by the Opposition, that this process be continued.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER (Mr Millar)- Order! The honourable member's time has expired.
Dr EDWARDS-Mr Deputy Speaker, this Government is concerned about these matters.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-The discussion has concluded. The honourable member will resume his seat.
Dr EDWARDS-Under the tenure of this Government and the Treasurer, the honourable member for Bennelong, Mr Howard, we have a record in combating tax avoidance and evasion second to none.
Mr DEPUTY SPEAKER-Order! The honourable member will resume his seat. If he persists in talking after being called to order by the Chair I shall have to deal with him.

Taxation
To the Right Honourable Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled: The humble petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that whereas the current rates of personal income tax are onerous and destructive of personal incentive, particularly since the cessation of income tax indexation.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Australian Government formerly call upon the Parliament assembled to legislate such changes to the current taxation law as will-
raise the minimum level at which income tax is first payable to at least $4.500.00,
proportionately increase the figures at which the higher rates of tax attract,
allow taxpayers to claim the full amount for each item within the Concessional Expenditure Rebate, and
adjust the rates of tax payable so that no higher rate of tax is payable than fifty per cent of income earned.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Armitage and Mr John Brown.

That the population of harp seals has not been clearly shown to be increasing.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the Australian Government formally call on the Canadian Government to end the harp seal hunt and use its constitutional powers to halt the importation into Australia of products derived from the harp seal, Pagophilus groenlandicus.

University of Queensland
The Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. The petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia showeth that:
The students and staff of the University of Queensland express their dismay at the lack of adequate Federal Government funding to our University, which has resulted, inter alia, in the erosion of our library facilities-we note that:
The opening hours of our main library have been reduced from 93t hours a week in 1975 to 72 hours a week in 1982.
The number of new books purchased per year has fallen from over 50,000 in 1976 to under 23,000 in 1981.
The number of books in storage and inaccessible to students and staff has risen from none in 1977 to over 40,000 in 1981.
The number of books discarded in 1976 was over 6,OOO-by the end of 1981 over 70,000 had been discarded.
We believe this to be educationally retrograde and economically wasteful.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that government funding levels be increased to a level sufficient to allow the University to restore our previous library facilities.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by MrCross.
Petition received.
Proposed Student Loans Scheme
To the Honourable the Speaker and Members of the House of Representatives in Parliament assembled. This petition of the undersigned citizens of Australia respectfully showeth that the introduction of a comprehensive student loans scheme will:
I. Restrict Access to Education--People from poorer backgrounds will be deterred from studying by the prospect of incurring large debts. These people, if they

enrol, will be forced to amass loan debts. Richer students do not face this handicap.
2. Undermine the Tertiary' Education Assistance Scheme-TEAS should cover the basic living costs of students. Currently the scheme only assists 38% of full time students and the maximum level of TEAS is a mere $49.67. Government statements indicate TEAS will be further downgraded if loans are introduced.
3. Disadvantage Women-Women students would be disadvantaged by a loans scheme as they have a lesser ability to repay loan debts, because on average women earn less and have a higher unemployment rate.
4. Disadvantage Mature-Age Students-Usually mature age students forego income to study and already have large financial commitments. They could not return to study if it meant incurring substantial debts.
5. Distort Educational Choices-Mounting loan debts would force students into studying shorter courses or not continuing with post-graduate study.
6. Result in Massive Attrition Rates-Students will attempt to undertake extensive part-time work or overload courses to minimise their loans. Increased failure rates will result forcing students to "drop-out" rather than continuing study and increase their debts.
7. Cause Administrative & Financial DifficultiesOverseas experiences shows high default rates will occur on student loans. Government money will be channelled away from TEAS to paying off banks. The government will also bear the administrative burden of pursuing defaulters.
Therefore, the proposed student loans scheme will have disastrous effects on tertiary education, which we believe benefits the whole community, not just the individual student.
Your petitioners therefore humbly pray that the House of Representatives will reject the introduction of a student loans scheme.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Dawkins.
Petition received.

(3) We believe that it will cause the Australian seed industry to become foreign controlled:
(4) We believe that a full public debate has not taken place and should take place before any laws are passed on the patenting of a life form.
Your petitioners, therefore, humbly request that Parliament take positive action as soon as possible to arrest the implementation of such legislation.
And your petitioners as in duty bound will ever pray.
by Mr Hodgman.

AUSTRALIAN EMBASSY, TEHRAN
Report and Ministerial Statement
Mr STREET (Corangamite-Minister for Foreign Affairs)-I present the report, together with appendixes, of an investigation into allegations of improper financial dealings at the Australian Embassy, Tehran, carried out by the Department of Finance.
Motion (by Mr Street)-by leave-agreed to:
That this House, in accordance with the provisions of the Parliamentary Papers Act 1908, authorises the publication of the Report, together with appendixes, of an investigation into allegations of improper financial dealings at the Australian Embassy, Tehran, carried out by the Department of Finance.
Mr STREET-by leave-On 29 April in the Parliament, Senator Primmer made a series of very serious allegations against a senior officer of the Department of Foreign Affairs and against the Secretary of that Department. He alleged, without providing any evidence to justify his allegations, that Mr I. G. Bowden, formerly Australian Ambassador in Tehran and now a senior officer in the Department of Foreign Affairs, had been guilty of extensive financial wrong-doing, that this had been covered up by the Secretary of the Department, Mr P. G. F. Henderson, that I had been lied to by officials of my Department and that, as a result, I in turn had lied to Senator Primmer and thus to the Parliament. On 30 April, I made a statement deploring actions taken under protection of parliamentary privilege which impugned the integrity of individuals and which described allegations as 'facts'. I said that I had already initiated an investigation by the Department of Finance into allegations made earlier by an officer of my Department, Mr D. W. Witheford, about alleged embezzlement in the

Australian Embassy, Tehran

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 993

Australian Embassy in Tehran, and that the Secretary of the Department had invited the Australian Federal Police to consider the results of the investigations being conducted by the Department of Finance. I made it clear that I believed that anyone, including a member of parliament, who made an allegation was under a clear obligation to bring forward any facts in his possession which ought to be investigated. Senator Primmer has not produced any facts or evidence other than the allegations made in the adjournment debate on 29 April.
On 20 July, I made a statement confirming that the final report made by officers of the Department of Finance had been received from the Minister for Finance (Senator Dame Margaret Guilfoyle). I said then that I was not able to comment in detail at that time, first, because I was waiting for a report from the Federal Police on this case, and second, because, as this matter had been raised in Parliament I should make a detailed statement to Parliament. I did say in that statement, however, that the investigating officers were, and I quote, 'satisfied that, on the evidence available, there is no basis for action against Mr Bowden by the Commonwealth in respect of the financial matters examined'. I made this statement at that time because serious allegations had been made and that, in view of the widespread publicity given to the allegations, it would be quite unfair to the officers named to wait until Parliament resumed to say what could be said at that time. I now inform the House in detail of the results of those investigations, and of the subsequent consideration of the allegations and the finance report by the Australian Federal Police. I think it is important first to set out precisely what the allegations are and how and by whom they were made. On 9 April Mr D. W. Witheford, an officer of my Department, submitted to me a statement making the following allegations:
(A) Related irregularities (fares to Australia from the United Kingdom): It was suggested that in 1960 there was an irregularity in the payment of the Bowden family completion-of-posting fares by the Commonwealth from England to Australia in that a nanny to the Bowden children was included as part of the family.
(B) Related irregularities (fraudulent conversion of a cheque in Hong Kong): Reference was made to the fraudulent conversion of a cheque issued by the Australian Commission in Hong Kong when Mr Bowden was Commissioner there. It was not suggested that Mr Bowden was directly involved.

(C) Tehran: Payment to children: Mr Witheford questioned the necessity-and perhaps the propriety-of Mr Bowden as Ambassador in Tehran employing his sons from time to time, at Commonwealth expense, to remove snow from the official residence in Tehran. It was further suggested that Mr Bowden effected certain administrative changes because certain administrative officers refused to certify the necessary transactions.
(D) Tehran: Purchase of air tickets for children over 18: It was suggested that Mr Bowden may not have declared income of the children over the age of 18 years when arranging for reunion entitlements during his term of office in Tehran.
(E) Tehran: Servants' wages: It was suggested that Mr Bowden had for some time been retaining a substantial portion of the salaries of his two domestic waiters or stewards although he was receiving the full amount in reimbursement from the Embassy.
(F) Tehran: Misuse of cars: It was stated that Mr Bowden, his wife, and on one occasion a private guest of the Bowdens, used official cars for private purposes and suggested that there were improper uses of official cars. Mr Witheford referred to an alleged complaint by former Prime Minister McMahon that he was not provided with an official car on a visit to Tehran in 1975. The statement was made also that Mr Bowden would not permit official cars to be made available for transporting officers' children to and from school.
(G) Tehran: Representation allowance: The suggestion-was made that Mr Bowden may have been unduly secretive about his representation allowance files and that there were irregularities in the reimbursements claimed by Mr Bowden.
(H) Tehran: Further irregularities: Under this heading Mr Witheford's statement refers to his recollection of '20 or 30' irregularities listed by Internal Audit at the time of visiting the Embassy in 1975. The Department of Finance conclusion in respect of each of these allegations was:
(a) As to the nanny's fare, the report notes that, although Mr Bowden did not volunteer the information that the nurse's travel was included in the family booking, there was no loss of public moneys as Mr Bowden paid for the nurse's fare on request. The report further notes that there was a failure to comply with prescribed procedures and that the relevant papers suggested that at the time both the general understanding of officers as to their entitlements and the administrative procedures in the Department in this regard were then less than satisfactory. I note here that the

994 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Australian Embassy, Tehran

procedures in force at that time-22 years agohave been amended since that time and the administrative weaknesses apparent at the time have long since been rectified. In particular, travel at official expense must be preceded by the completion of the necessary movement requisitions and approval by the Minister's delegate.
(b) As to the fraudulent conversion of the cheque in Hong Kong there was, in this case, a loss of public moneys amounting to $8,734.99. When the matter was discovered in October 1973, some 12 months after it happened, it was investigated by the Hong Kong Police and the Commonwealth Police Force, but the offender or offenders could not be identified. The fraudulent conversion occurred before Mr Bowden took over the post and while it was administered by the then Department of Trade. The inconclusive outcome of the police investigations was accepted by the Department of Foreign Affairs and the Treasury. The delay of a year in reporting the loss to Canberra was noted by the Department of Finance as refiecting adversely on the administration of the post at the time the fraudulent conversion took place, but the report notes that the period concerned coincided with the handover of the administration of the mission by the then Department of Trade to the Department of Foreign Affairs and that 'the post was in some administrative disarray on the financial side at that time'. The report goes on to say that: 'it seems clear that when Bowden and Meredith-the senior Foreign Affairs Administrative Officer-learned of the matter, proper follow-up action was taken reasonably promptly.
(c) The report concludes that claims for Mr Bowden's sons for clearing snow were submitted in the proper manner and payments made in the prescribed manner. Mr Bowden has explained to the Department of Finance investigation that the job of clearing snow from the fiat roofs of residences was not regarded in Tehran generally as a function of the gardeners employed, but was normally performed by casual labourers. The gardeners assisted with other snow clearing tasks, including clearing the driveway, steps and outbuildings. Mr Bowden merely substituted the employment of his sons for the employment of such casual Iranian labourers. Comparison of accounts submitted for this snow clearing with other clearing accounts at the time suggested that the claims made were reasonable. Snow clearing from the Ambassador's residence was a legitimate official charge. The propriety of members of the family of the Ambassador or indeed any

Australia-based officer engaging in Commonwealth employment has been a matter for personal judgment. No guidelines have been issued on this subject and it is a matter for the head of mission and the officer appointed by the Minister-not normally the Ambassador-to approve expenses. The report notes that it is not uncommon for members of the families of Australia-based officers to obtain permanent and/or temporary employment at overseas posts. I believe that there should be some clarification of the circumstances under which this practice is acceptable. I have asked my Department, in conjunction with the Public Service Board and other departments with staff serving overseas, to establish a set of instructions to regulate this practice.
(d) As to reimbursement for Mr Bowden's sons' air fares, the issue in this matter arises because, in respect of children over 18 years who are entitled to be reunited with their parents at a post, any income earned by the children must be taken into account in calculating the amount payable by the officer as a contribution to the reunion fares. At the relevant time Mr Bowden's sons were beneficiaries of income from a trust; they had no access to this income, which was used mainly to meet expenses connected with their education. Mr Bowden was not, under procedures in force at the time, asked to disclose the children's income when applying for the visits. If he had been, it would have been a question of interpretation as to whether there was in fact any income to declare under the regulations, which are not clear about what constitutes income. The report notes that according to the Public Service Board advice Mr Bowden's boys would have received sufficient income to warrant a parental contribution to reunion fares. The report notes on the other hand that it appears from the Department of Foreign Affairs files that according to normal practice Mr Bowden was not asked to disclose the children's income when applying for visits-nor, if such information was sought, would the Public Service Board's interpretation of the determinations relating to overseas service be necessarily the one applied. The Department of Finance concludes that the point at issue was one of unsound Central Office procedures, which it notes have now been changed, and an imprecise wording of the relevant provisions which the Department of Finance will suggest should be amended. I should make it clear that the departmental procedures referred to by the Department of Finance had been changed long before the present investigation was made.

Australian Embassy, Tehran

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 995.

(e) As to the wages paid to stewards, the circumstances of the matter were that Mr Bowden, through the Bangladesh Ambassador in Tehran, replaced two Iranian stewards with two stewards recruited from Bangladesh. The Department of Foreign Affairs had agreed to this before the arrangement was made on the understanding that Mr Bowden personally should meet the air fare to Tehran, provide accommodation and the return air fare at the end of the contract. The Department had expressly approved that Mr Bowden pay the air fares in the first instance and that he be reimbursed an amount up to the wage level approved for Iranian servants-who lived out-so that he could progressively reimburse himself for this expense out of the difference between this figure and the amount paid to the Bangladeshi servants. The records clearly show this approval as well as the amounts of deductions made. The money collected to cover the cost of their return fares was retained, together with the necessary documentation, and handed over on Mr Bowden's departure to a member of the Embassy for Mr Bowden's successor. The Department of Finance states that its examination of the papers did not reveal that there was any breach of the law or any misappropriation of public money. Mr Bowden was acting with full knowledge of the Department of Foreign Affairs.
(f) Under the terms and conditions of his appointment, Mr Bowden was provided with a car for the official use of the head of mission. A substantial deduction is made from the head of mission allowance at the post to take account of the additional use of official vehicles to which he is entitled. Mr Bowden denied that he, his wife or friends misused official cars and there was no corroboration of Mr Witheford's allegations from other officers. There is no record of any complaint having been made by former Prime Minister McMahon that he was refused Embassy transport and enquiries of the Department of Administrative Services indicate that in any case he would not have been entitled to the use of an official car in Tehran. Nevertheless, it is understood that an official of the Embassy met Sir William at the airport on his arrival and Mr Bowden himself drove Sir William back to the airport on his departure. As to the refusal by Mr Bowden to authorise the use of official cars to transport children to school, the Department of Finance noted that this calls for no comment as it is simply an exercise of the authority vested in the Ambassador. The regulations provide that the actual cost of school bus transport may be accepted for reimbursement but no provisions are made for the transport of preschool children.

(g) As to suggestions that Mr Bowden was unduly secretive about representation allowance files and that there were irregularities in the reimbursements claimed by him, the report notes that there is no indication in the papers of any departure from the requirements contained in the Public Service Board determinations or departmental instructions. Reimbursements in respect of representation expenditure were made directly between the Ambassador and Canberra. The officer who is said by Mr Witheford to have been refused when he asked to have access to the Ambassador's representation files could not remember why he wanted to see them. The Department of Finance notes that scrutiny by the Department of Foreign Affairs of representation returns was comprehensive and followed a set pattern. The Department did not dispute the level or cost of Mr Bowden's representational activities.
(h) With reference to financial irregularities, the Tehran Embassy was subjected to internal audit reviews in 1975, 1977 and 1978. The 1975 review was extremely critical of the financial administration at Tehran; the 1977 review was requested by the Embassy and that review and a subsequent inspection in 1978 reported a progressive improvement in financial administration. Mr Bowden arrived at the post in July 1974 and remained until March 1978. None of the reports which were made by appropriate officials suggested that any improper official dealings had occurred at the post. The competence or veracity of these reports has not been questioned. The report examines other matters raised by Mr Witheford which are essentially matters of procedure. One matter raised by Mr Witheford, that of the procedures for the payment of salaries to Australia-based staff, would appear to have arisen from a misunderstanding by him of those procedures. The Department of Finance concluded that it was obvious from the adverse 1975 internal audit report that the Treasury regulation 132 certificates then used did not represent the true position at the post; their use was in any case discontinued in 1977. They have been replaced by a more truly representative system of reports. As I have said, the Department of Finance report notes that subsequent audit inspections in 1977 and 1978 reported a progressive improvement in financial administration.
With respect to all allegations, the Department of Finance's conclusion was that the investigating officers were 'Satisfied that, on the evidence available, there is no basis for action against Mr Bowden by the Commonwealth in respect of the financial matters examined'.
