Australian Embassy, Tehran

In order that there should not remain any element of doubt about whether there had been any irregularities, and because of the terms of the allegations made in the Parliament by Senator Primmer, the Permanent Head of my Department requested, as I indicated earlier, that the Australian Federal Police consider the result of the Department of Finance investigation, particularly to see whether any other action of any kind was warranted. The Australian Federal Police considered the report by the Department of Finance and interviewed persons having direct knowledge of the matters, including Mr Witheford and Mr Bowden, and persons described by Mr Witheford as his informants. Subsequently the Federal Police sent to the Secretary to my Department a report on the matter.
Mr Witheford has complained that the police investigation was not properly conducted and has made a complaint to the Ombudsman against the police investigators themselves. Under the provisions of the Complaints (Australian Federal Police) Act 1981 this compaint must itself be the subject of an investigation by the Internal Investigation Division of the Australian Federal Police. This is now being pursued. In the meantime, the police have advised that I have their agreement to use the following words:
After investigation of all of the matters raised by Mr Withe ford the following points became apparent to investigating police:
The matters raised do not all come within the ambit of the criminal jurisdiction and hence, did not warrant police investigation.
Mr Witheford possesses no evidence in the strict sense of the matters alleged.
None of the alleged informants of Mr Witheford possess evidence to support the allegations made by Mr Witheford.
None of Mr Witheford's informants placed the same construction on events as Mr Witheford and do not support his contentions.
In summary, therefore, the police report that their 'inquiries reveal no evidence which would substantiate any of the allegations made by Mr Witheford. Indeed, there is insufficient evidence to demonstrate that Mr Bowen acted in an improper manner, let alone criminally.' I have a letter from the Minister for Administrative Services (Mr Newman) containing the quotes which I have just made, and I table that too. In my statement of 30 April I reiterated publicly that anyone, including a member of parliament was under a clear obligation to make any relevant information available. Some four months have now elapsed since this request was made. With the exception of Mr Witheford there has been no response to this request.

All persons holding public office must be accountable for their actions. Any allegations or evidence of wrongdoing must be examined and action taken if it is warranted. If, moreover, any person believes that there have been wrongful acts committed, that person, and particularly an officer of the Public Service, has a clear duty to bring forward his or her concerns and, of course, must be entitled to protection against any possiblity of vindictive action simply because allegations have been made.
There should be no equivocation or hesitation by officers themselves or by the Government in discharging the obligation to dispose of any suspicions that wrongful actions have been committed. But this obligation must be discharged with a proper regard for the rights of all individuals involved. In particular, where allegations are made they must be treated as no more than allegations until they have been investigated. In my statement of 30 April, I deplored the fact that a member of this parliament had, under privilege, made unsubstantiated allegations as though they were facts, in complete disregard of the rights of the individuals concerned.
But Senator Primmer went further than simply making allegations against Mr Bowden. He also said of the Permanent Head of my Department that he had protected Mr Bowden; that both Mr Henderson and Mr Bowden were members of 'a well-known faction, namely the Geelong Grammer Mafia'; that Mr Henderson had lied to me; that he, Mr Henderson, ought to be suspended from duty, implying that Mr Henderson was a dishonest officer; Mr Henderson had denied promotion to 'honest' officers; questioned whether Mr Henderson had been guilty of 'white collar crime' and 'a massive cover-up'. There has been no evidence produced to support any of these allegations.
These allegations depend on the propOSitIOn that Mr Bowden had been guilty of wrongdoing. That has been clearly disposed of. But some might even then argue that Mr Henderson or the Department had resisted any investigation for fear that there had been a wrongful act, or out of special knowledge. There is absolutely no evidence to support this either. I believe that Senator Primmer, having presented as facts allegations against a loyal and dedicated public servant, who has served successive governments with great distinction for over 30 years, must now ask himself a serious question. Have his actions been consistent with the standards expected of a member of this Parliament?

EXPORT MARKET DEVELOPMENT GRANTS AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Anthony, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr ANTHONY (Richmond-Minister for Trade and Resources) (2.42)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
On 19 July the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) outlined the Government's decisions on industry and related policies which followed its consideration of Industries Assistance Commission reports on industry assistance, protection and export incentives. In my subsequent statement of 19 July I elaborated the decisions taken on export incentives. This Bill gives effect to those decisions.
The Government has decided that the Export Market Development Grants Act should be substantially amended so that it can more effectively stimulate the market development activities of Australian exporters and that the Export Expansion Grants Scheme should be allowed to lapse on 30 June 1983. This decision should be seen in the context of the total package of industry assistance

announced by the Prime Minister. The Government's decision to terminate the EEG Scheme and to increase benefits under the EMDG Scheme reflects its view that the EMDG Scheme provides a more cost effective means of stimulating the growth of exports. The majority of EMDG claimants are firms producing technologically advanced products which, by their nature, must rely heavily on innovative marketing and promotion to ensure success against international competition. The Government was also cognisant of international attitudes towards performance based export incentives and sees its decisions as being consistent with Australia's objective of achieving a multilateral reduction in subsidies to international trade.
I should now like to draw the attention of the House towards the specific measures aimed at further increasing the effectiveness of the Export Market Development Grants Act. The EMI)G Scheme, which was introduced in 1974, provides financial incentives in the form of taxable grants based on eligible expenditure incurred in such activities as overseas market research and development, advertising, the cost of participating in overseas exhibitions, fares incurred in overseas travel and the cost of bringing agents or buyers to Australia where the promotion of export sales is the objective. The Act was subsequently amended in 1978 when a single grant rate of 70 per cent of eligible expenditure was introduced and benefits were extended to the tourist industry.
Taking into account the present difficult international trading climate, the Government is anxious to ensure that exporters are able to make use of the increased benefits as soon as possible. These benefits will therefore apply from the grant year commencing 1 July 1982 for a further six years. Exporters have therefore been provided with a stable base on which to plan long term marketing strategies.
The main changes now proposed for the legislation are as follows: The maximum annual grant available to a claimant will be raised to $200,000, compared with the present level of $100,000, with an additional $25,000 for expenditure incurred in Government sponsored promotions. This latter provision will be deleted in line with the recommendation of the Industries Assistance Commission. The Government has accepted the view of the Commission that deletion of the provision will improve the effectiveness of the scheme by allowing firms to determine for themselves the form of export promotion which would give the greatest returns in terms of export sales. The doubling of the basic $100,000 ceiling will restore the benefit to a real level commensurate with that

Export Market Development Grants Bill

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 999

existing at the commencement of the scheme in 1974.
Over the period of operation of the Export Market Development Grants Scheme there have been changes in the relative importance of the different elements which make up the costs of market development. Expenditures on accommodation and sustenance are now significant charges which previously had been fully borne by the exporter. It has now been decided that the Government will make a contribution to these costs. This contribution will take the form of the payment of an amount of $50 for each full day of the visit up to a maximum of ten days. This is a set figure and will not be subject to an application of the 70 per cent grant rate. The amount has been set at a level consistent with the 'shared cost' principle inherent in the scheme. All claims for the payment of an amount under this provision will be subject to detailed scrutiny by the Export Development Grants Board in accordance with procedures developed by the Board in its examination of eligibility of overseas fares. Claimants will be obliged to substantiate to the Board's satisfaction that .each visit was undertaken primarily and principally for the purpose of export market development.

tourist promotions as approved by the Department of Trade and Resources and the Department of Industry and COmmerce will continue to be exempt from this provision even though the provision for Government sponsored promotions will be deleted.
The Construction Industry
A new provision has been introduced which takes into account the special problems encountered by the construction industry when contracting for overseas business. Australian firms competing for overseas construction tenders are often obliged under the terms of the contracts to include certain inputs from local sources. At present the legislation generally results in claims being allowed only in the same proportion as the Australian inputs bear to the total contract value. The legislation has been liberalised to allow all tender-quotation costs to be admissible when Australian input is not less than 20 per cent of the contract value and for a proportion of costs, according to a sliding scale value, to be eligible when Australian input falls below 20 per cent. Eligible expenditure will therefore reflect more satisfactorily the costs incurred in tendering for these contracts.

Increased Benefits for the Tourism Industry
In its report the lAC drew attention to certain anomalies in relation to the treatment accorded to the tourist industry under the legislation. The Government has accepted the proposition that wherever possible benefits provided for tourism should be consistent with those offered to other industries and has therefore decided to extend certain benefits accordingly. Regional tourist bodies will no longer be subject to incurring eligible expenditure only in relation to Government sponsored promotions. The costs of tenders and quotations for the supply of special tourist tenders will now be eligible. A similar provision already exists in respect of the export of goods and services.
The Bill provides also that where discounts are offered to overseas tourist industry representatives as an inducement to buy Australian tourist services one half of the amount of the discount may be admitted as eligible expenditure. This is also consistent with the treatment of free samples provided to overseas clients by manufacturers under the existing legislation. The existing requirement that tourist industry claimants must spend at least $5,000 other than on fares to have a grant entitlement will be retained. Recognised

Other Amendments
The opportunity has been taken also to introduce certain amendments which will clarify the legislation and simplify the administration. These include a redefinition of the eligibility requirements when two or more persons who are relatives travel outside Australia at the same time. In order to simplify administration and to clarify the position a discretion previously exercised by the Export Development Grants Board will be removed. Because of this it is proposed that these amendments will not come into operation until the grant year commencing I July 1983. Other changes include an extension of the concept of approved bodies to cover those associations promoting the export of services, industrial property rights and know-how and an extended power of delegation to all members of the Board.
It is estimated that the increased EMDG benefits made available to exporters will be of the order of $36m in the first grant year of operation. Their effects will be felt in the Budget from 1983-84 onwards. The new EMDG scheme should provide a new impetus to develop export markets. Coupled with the other assistance measures being made available for industry the means now exist to stimulate further expansion in Australia's international trade with resulting

1000 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982 States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill

benefits for the economy as a whole. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
STATES (TAX SHARING AND HEALTH GRANTS) AMENDMENT BILL (No.2)
1982
Bill presented by Sir James Killen, on behalf of Mr Howard, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Sir JAMES KILLEN (MoretonVicePresident of the Executive Council and Leader of the House) (2.54)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The main purpose of this Bill is to amend the States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Act 1981 to provide for: Firstly, the implementation of the State tax sharing relativities adopted at the June 1982 Premiers Conference for the purpose of calculating States' tax sharing grants for the years 1982-83, 1983-84 and 1984-85; secondly, the phasing-in of those relativities over the three years ending 1984-85 at the rate of 33t per cent each year; and, thirdly, a Commonwealth guarantee that the total tax sharing grants payable to each State will not increase by less than 2 per cent in real terms in 1982-83 and by one per cent in real terms in both 1983-84 and 1984-85.
The new tax sharing arrangements are the culmination of a process begun in 1978 when the question of the distribution of the States' tax sharing grants was referred to the Commonwealth Grants Commission for inquiry and report. The Commission was required to assess whether any change was desirable in the per capita relativities between States' grants on the basis of the principle that each State should have the fiscal capacity to provide standard levels of service without imposing taxes and charges significantly different from those imposed by other States.
The Commission's report on State tax sharing entitlements 1981 was considered at the 20 June 1981 Premiers Conference. It was decided that there would be no change in the relativities contained in the States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Act 1981 at that time in respect of the year 1981-82 and that the Commission should be asked to produce a further report on its assessment of State relativities in the light of submissions to be put to it by States and by the Commonwealth. However, in recognition of the likelihood that, following the further review, relativities could be adjusted in favour of the three more populous States the Commonwealth agreed to provide additional grants of a total of $60m to

New South Wales, Victoria and Queensland in 1981-82 as part of the total contribution by the Commonwealth towards assisting States to adjust to the new relativities.
The Commission presented its second report on 31 May 1982. In it the Commission confirmed the view expressed in its 1981 report that a change was desirable in the existing State relativities. The June 1982 Premiers Conference adopted a proposal put by the Commonwealth regarding the distribution of tax sharing grants to apply in 1982-83, 1983-84 and 1984-85. This Bill gives effect to these new tax sharing arrangements. The Commonwealth Grants Commission's 1982 assessed relativities have been modified so that the benefits of South Australia's and Tasmania's hospital cost sharing grants, which continue for the next three years, do not adversely affect their tax sharing grants. This modification is consistent with clause 7.1 of South Australia's and Tasmania's continuing hospital cost sharing agreements with the Commonwealth which states that any above average per capital hospital cost sharing grants paid to those States are not to be offset by the Commonwealth against general revenue payments. Clearly, the Commonwealth has honoured these agreements. The relativities so modified are to be phased-in over the three years ending 1984-85 at the rate of 33t per cent each year. The phasing-in provisions take account of the financial difficulties that would have been created for the three less populous States if the assessed relativities had been implemented in full in 1982-83.
In addition, the Bill provides for a guarantee that each State's tax sharing grant will increase by at least 2 per cent in real terms in 1982-83, and one per cent in real terms in each of 1983-84 and 1983-84 and 1984-85. An amount will be set aside from the tax sharing pool to be used, in the first instance, to fund this guarantee with any portion not required for this purpose being distributed among six States. The amount to be set aside, totalling about $149m in 1982-83, represents the escalated value of the $60m provided by the Commonwealth to the three more populous States in 1981-82, to which I referred earlier, plus the escalated value of the next budgetary gains accruing to South Australia and Tasmania from the Commonwealth payments to those States in return for the transfer of their railways to the Commonwealth in 1975-76. The Commission had assessed that, under the fiscal equalisation principle, South Australia and Tasmania should not continue to have a higher share of tax sharing grants on account of these additional grants.

States (Tax Sharing and Health Grants) Bill 26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1001

To give effect to the decisions taken at the June 1982 Premiers Conference, the Bill provides that the total tax sharing grant in respect of each of the three remaining years of the tax sharing arrangements will be divided into two pools: Firstly, a basic tax sharing pool, equal to 20.323 per cent of total Commonwealth tax collections in the previous year and, secondly, a supplementary tax sharing pool, equal to 0.397 per cent of such collections. The distribution of the basic tax sharing pool among the States in each year will be calculated on the basis of the populations of each State at 31 December in the relevant year weighted by the new phased-in per capita relativities adopted at the June 1982 Premiers' Conference.
The distribution of the supplementary tax sharing pool among the States is, in the first instance, designed to fund the guaranteed minimum tax shares, with any portion not required for that purpose being further distributed among the six States on a specified basis. A State that receives a payment as a result of the guarantee is not entitled to receive a total tax sharing grant greater than the guaranteed amount. The total amount of tax sharing grants payable to the six States in 1982-83 is $7,772.5m, an increase of 16.2 per cent over the previous year. I seek leave to have incorporated in Hansard a table which contains the latest estimates of the tax sharing grants payable to each State in 1982-83. This distribution is subject to revision in accordance with revised estimates of the States' populations and the respective capital city consumer price indices.
Leave granted.

Sir JAMES KILLEN-I thank the House. The main features of the existing tax sharing arrangements will continue under the new arrangements. I refer in particular to the retention of the right of the States to impose surcharges or to give

rebates under stage 2 of the federalism arrangements as provided in the Income Tax (Arrangements with the States) Act 1978 and the retention of the Commonwealth's right to be able to declare an increase in, or rebates of, any Commonwealth tax to be a 'special surcharge or rebate', and hence excluded from the total tax sharing base. One of the provisions of the Bill is that the previous guarantee that the State's grants in any year will not be less in money terms than they were in the previous year is replaced by a significantly more generous guarantee that each State's tax sharing grant will increase by 2 per cent in real terms in 1982-83 and by 1 per cent in real terms in each of the years 1983-84 and 1984-85. In view of the generosity of the guarantee and the phasing-in arrangements for the new relativities, the Commonwealth required, and the States accepted, that no special grants will be paid by the Commonwealth during the phasing-in period of three years.
The Commonwealth Government has acted responsibly and equitably in respect of the appropriate relativities to apply during the next three years. Clearly, changes to the distribution of the tax sharing grants between the States have to be made consistent with the principle of fiscal equalisation. Equally, the provisions of the Bill will ensure that the changes take place at a pace compatible with the fiscal capacities of the States. No State will suffer a decline in the real value of its annual tax sharing grants. As a result of decisions taken at both the Premiers Conference and the Loan Council meeting in June 1982 the States will have available in 1982-83 a substantially increased volume of funds from Commonwealth assistance and borrowing programs-excluding electricity authorities-approved by Loan Council. It is currently estimated that the States will receive an increase in such funds of over 13 per cent-a significant real terms increase. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
ASIAN DEVELOPMENT FUND BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Street, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr STREET (Corangamite-Minister for Foreign Affairs) (3.6)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The purpose of this Bill is to authorise a contribution by Australia of $A212.33m towards the third replenishment of the Asian Development Fund. Honourable members will know that, since the Asian Development Bank was established in

I002 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Asian Development Fund Bill

1966, Australia has been and remains a strong and active supporter of the Bank. We continue to regard it as an efficient and effective organisation for providing aid to developing countries in our own geographic region. The main role of the Bank is to assist the economic development of its developing member countries and to foster economic and social growth and co-operation in the Asian and Pacific regions. It does this by lending funds, promoting investment and providing technical assistance on a scale that bilateral donors could not achieve alone. The Bank has continued to approach its complex development task with flexibility and vigour, especially over recent years during which it has been confronted by the growing resource constraints imposed by the global economic recession.
Since its establishment the Bank has lent about $USIO billion for projects covering all the major sectors of economic development with emphasis on the development of transport, communications, industry and electric power as well as projects for agriculture, education, water supply and urban development. In recent years an increasing proportion of the Bank's lending activities has been devoted to agricultural development and to social development-for example, in health, population and water supply projects. The Bank's lending activities are divided into ordinary and special operations. Ordinary operations are financed on the basis of the Bank's capital resources and its borrowings on the international capital market at commercial or near commercial terms. Special operations involve loans made on highly concessional terms to the Bank's poorest and least developed member countries and are funded from the Asian Development Fund.
Australia played a leading role in the establishment and subsequent growth of the Asian Development Fund which was set up in 1974 and first replenished in 1976. The Fund operates in the Asian and Pacific regions in a similar fashion to the World Bank's soft lending affiliate, the International Development Association. Australia contributed $A97m or 5.14 per cent to the last replenishment target of $US2.15 billion, to cover concessional lending for the period 1979 to 1982. By the end of this year the Bank will have fully committed the resources from this-secondreplenishment. In July 1981 donor countries commenced negotiations for a third replenishment of the Fund, to provide resources for the period 1983 to 1986. Bank management originally suggested a replenishment target of $US4.1 billion in order to maintain growth in the Fund's annual rate of lending at around 17.5 per cent. Australia supported this target and announced its willingness to

maintain its share in the Fund at around 5.14 per cent. However, when it became clear that the United States intended to reduce significantly its level of contribution, the target was revised to $US3.2 billion. Agreement and donor commitment were achieved earlier this year on this level which will enable the Fund to maintain the level of its concessionallending over the next four years in real terms.
In order to help meet the agreed target and to maintain a position appropriate to our standing in the Bank, Australia decided to increase its share in the Fund to approximately 7.2 per cent. This represents about the same funding level as the previous 5.14 per cent share of the original target of $4.1 billion and is commensurate with our share in the Bank's capital stock. Australia's decision to increase substantially its share in the fund proved to be critical at the final donors' meeting in April this year at which the target of $US3.2 billion was reached. The exact level of our contribution to the third replenishment of the Asian Development Fund has been set at $A212.33m. This amount will not be subject to future adjustments in exchange rates. In accordance with past practice with respect to similar contributions to the Asian Development Fund and the International Development Association, Australia will make its contribution by lodging non-negotiable, non-interest bearing promissory notes cashable on demand as and when funds are actually required for making loan disbursements. This will limit the impact on the Budget in the next year or so, as cashing of these notes is not expected to begin before 1983-84.
The Asian Development Bank and its Asian Development Fund have a most important role to play in continuing to foster economic and social development in the region of immediate interest to Australia. Our contribution to the third replenishment of the Fund will confirm our willingness to continue helping the poorest developing countries in our region with highly concessional assistance. While Australia is fully prepared to play its role as one of the few developed country regional members of the Bank, it is a matter of concern that some non-regional donors have significantly reduced their level of support. Australia appreciates that global economic difficulties have required domestic economic restraint by donor governments, but it should not be forgotten that it is the developing countries that have been hardest hit by the economic problems of our times. I commend the Bill to honourable members.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.

Statute Law Bill (No.2)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1003

STATUTE LAW (MISCELLANEOUS AMENDMENTS) BILL (No.2) 1982
Bill presented by Mr N. A. Brown, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr N. A. BROWN (Diamond Valley~ Minister for Communications and Minister Assisting the Attorney-General) (3.13)~1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill continues the practice of introducing an omnibus miscellaneous amendments Bill during each period of sittings of the Parliament as an expeditious way of making amendments of a noncontroversial character to a large number of Acts. Some of the amendments made by the Bill correct printing or drafting errors. Others make drafting or other technical amendments. Those provisions do not call for particular comment. Other provisions go into matters of substance in varying degrees. I propose to explain in some detail the nature and effect of those provisions. In addition, a detailed explanatory memorandum covering all the clauses of the Bill has been circulated.
Acts Interpretation Act 1901
Part VI of the Bill inserts in the Acts Interpretation Act 1901 a definition of 'the Department'. It is hoped that this general definition will overcome to a large extent the need to define desparateIy the word in particular legislation. having regard to this amendment, the opportunity has been taken, in regard to the formal machinery of Government amendments contained in Schedule 12 to the Bill, to relay as much as possible on that general definition. Particular amendments made by various Parts of the Bill remove express references in Acts to particular departments or to particular Permanent Heads of departments. Honourable members will recall the commitment the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) made to the Parliament in a statement concerning parliamentary disallowance of delegated legislation on 26 May 1981 in relation to certain reports of the Senate Standing Committee on Regulations and Ordinances. The Attorney-General then said that a careful study would be made of existing disallowances provisions with a view to also bringing those into line with the Government's policy that future disallowance provisions should, unless there is good reason to the contrary, incorporate the substance of the principles contained in sections 48, 49 and 50 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1901. That study is now well under way and the amendments to the undermentioned Acts bring those Acts into

conformity with those sections of the Acts Interpretation Act to the extent practicable in the particular case:
Aboriginal and Torres Strait Islanders (Queensland Reserves and Communities SelfManagement) Act 1978;
Aboriginal Councils and Associations Act 1976;
Aboriginal Land Rights (Northern Territory) Act 1976;
Customs Tariff Act 1966;
Dairy Industry Stabilization Act 1977;
Defence Act 1903;
Defence Amendment Act 1979;
Explosives Act 1961;
Representation Act 1905;
Seat of Government (Administration) Act 1910; and
State Grants (Petroleum Products) Act 1965.
Amendments to other legislation, if any, which should also be subject to the provisions contained in sections 48, 49 and 50 will be brought forward and identified.
Audit Act 1901
Section 628 of the Audit Act 1901 restricts the investment of moneys standing to the credit of the Trust Fund maintained under Part IX of the Act to Commonwealth and State securities, deposits in a bank and in the purchase of coinage. Part VII of the Bill amends section 628 of the Audit Act so as to provide wider avenues of investment of such moneys. The amendments will permit investment in securities issued by government authorities, on loans to authorised dealers in the official money market, in certificates of deposit and in clean bills of exchange. Also the Minister for Finance will be empowered to approve other suitable forms of investment.
Australian Federal Police Act 1979
Part XI of the Bill makes a number of amendments to improve the operation of the Australian Federal Police Act 1979. Section 26A of the Australian Federal Police Act 1979 provides that former Narcotics Enforcement Branch staff may, if qualified and suitable, be appointed to the Australain Federal Police. Arising from recent legal advice the Government has become aware that persons so appointed might not, by reason of the legal preference contained in section 73 of the Act, have equal rights to promotion and transfer within the Australian Federal Police as former

1004 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Statute Law Bill (No.2)

officers of the former Commonwealth Police Force and the Police Force of the Australian Capital Territory. The Bill makes the necessary amendments to the Australian Federal Police Act to ensure that equality.
Banking Act 1959
Part XII of the Bill adds a new Part IIA to the Banking Act 1959 to facilitate the operation of State and Northern Territory legislation relating to the merging of the banking businesses, respectively, of the Bank of New South Wales and the Commercial Bank of Australia Limited, and of The National Bank of Australasia Limited and The Commercial Banking Co. of Sydney Ltd.
Legislation now passed, or in course of passage, in each of the States and the Northern Territory will assist the merging banks and their many customers by removing the administrative burden that would otherwise be involved in completing the merger of the banks' businesses in those jurisdictions. Within the Australian Capital Territory ordinances will shortly be made to facilitate the merger of the banks' businesses in the Territory.
The amendments made to the Banking Act by Part XII of the Bill will remove a range of uncertainties arising from potential inconsistencies between various areas of Commonwealth legislation and the above mentioned State and Territory legislation. The amendments made by the Bill to the Banking Act will avoid the possibility that, by reason of any such inconsistency, some portion of the relevant State or Northern Territory legislation might be held to be invalid. In addition, the amendments to the Banking Act provide for the States and Northern Territory legislation to bind the Crown in the right of the Commonwealth, of the Northern Territory and of Norfolk Island.
Provision is also made by the Bill for the amendments I have outlined to be applied, at the discretion of the Treasurer, in the event of any future bank merger being implemented by the same method as has been adopted for these mergers. No such merger is, of course, contemplated at this time, and I remind honourable members that in g~nting his consent under section 63 of the Banking Act to the present mergers the Treasurer pointed out that it should not be assumed that consent would necessarily be forthcoming in respect of any future merger proposals. Nevertheless, in the event, at some future time, of a merger being duly considered and accepted, this provision would obviate the need for further Commonwealth legislative action to ensure that no constitutional difficulties arise from the enactment of State and Territory facilitating legislation.

I should point out that the amendments of the Banking Act made by the Bill will not have the effect of altering the substantive rights and obligations which presently exist between the continuing banks and their customers. It simply ensures that the business of the merged banks is able to be transferred to the continuing banks with minimum disruption to the service provided by the banks.
Commonwealth Electoral Act 1918
The amendment made by Part XVI of the Bill allows licensed premises to be used as polling booths provided the Chief Electoral Officer is satisfied that no intoxicating liquor is available for sale on those premises whilst they are being so used. Typical examples of such premises would be licensed town halls in larger towns and the cities.
Companies Act 1981
The Bill provides for two categories of amendment of the Companies Act 1981. The amendments have been approved by the Ministerial Council for Companies and Securities.
The first category, which involves amendments to sections 509 and 417 of the principal Act, is designed to ensure that there is greater consistency in the obligations imposed on different types of corporations as to the amount of information which they must disclose on documents such as cheques. This consistency is to be achieved by a reduction in the amount of information required.
Pursuant to the amendments made by the Bill, it will no longer be necessary for any corporation to indicate on bills of exchange, promissory notes, cheques or other negotiable instruments any details as to the State or Territory in which it is incorporated, registered as a foreign company, or formed, as the case may be. It is mentioned that under the Companies Act 1981 as it presently operates, an officer of such a corporation who signs such a document in the Australian Capital Territory could be liable to the holder of the document if he does not indicate the requisite details.
The amendments will also relieve any corporation, which has limited liability but does not have the words 'Limited' in its name, of the need to indicate in those same documents that it has limited liability.
Both types of information covered by the above mentioned amendments will still be required in any business letter, statement of account, invoice, order for goods, order for services, receipt, official notice or publication of a company.

Statute Law Bill (No.2)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1005

The second category of amendment to the principal Act involves amendments to sections 5 and 581. Those amendments are designed to preserve the operation of the provisions of three Australian Capital Territory Ordinances which are dependent on the relevant companies legislation in force in the Australian Capital Territory at any particulartime.
Conciliation and Arbitration Act 1904
The amendment made by Part XXI of the Bill allows a deputy president whose present appointment to office ran until he attains the age of 70 years to be appointed as President of the Australian Conciliation and Arbitration Commission without any diminution of the right to serve until he attains the age of 70 years. The amendment ensures that if such a person is appointed as President he is not required to retire at the age of 65 years which would otherwise be the case with all new appointments to presidential office in the Commission.
Defence Service Homes Act 1918
At present, assistance under the Defence Service Homes Act 1918 cannot be given to an eligible person if he or his spouse owns another home. One effect of this provision is to exclude from assistance a person who does not own a home and who is permanently separated, but not divorced, from his spouse if that spouse owns a home. The proposed amendments will enable an advance to be made in such circumstances notwithstanding the separated spouse's ownership of a home.
Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Act 1975
Part XXXVI of the Bill amends sub-section 66 (5) of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Act 1975 to overcome a practical problem which has become evident in the light of experience with the preparation of zoning plans for newly declared areas of the Marine Park.
Sub-section 66 (5) of the Great Barrier Reef Marine Park Act provides that regulations shall not apply to an area within the Marine Park in respect of which no zoning plan is in force if more than 12 months has elapsed since that area became part of the Marine Park. Experience has shown that, because of the timing requirements in the principal Act relating to the preparation of zoning plans, the period of 12 months has proved to be insufficient. The amendment to sub-section 66 (5) made by the Bill will allow regulations to apply to such areas for a period of up to two years from the date an area becomes part of the Marine

Park. The amendment will avoid interim regulations lapsing before a zoning plan comes into effect.
Industrial Research and Development Incentives Act 1976
The amendments made by Part XLIV of the Bill seek to overcome certain technical deficiencies in the Industrial Research and Development Incentives Act 1976. They will ensure that the Government's long standing policy intentions, in respect of the provision of encouragement by way of commencement grant to companies to commence systematic research and development activities, are given full effect.
The Act was intended to provide that entitlements of any company to commencement grants be restricted to five consecutive years, with this entitlement reduced by one year for each grant received under the preceding grants scheme. This policy was announced when the Act was first introduced in the House in 1976. It has been very well publicised since.
However, amendments made to the Act in 1981 did not adequately secure this policy of limited entitlement. The proposed amendments to sections 25 and 26A will overcome these deficiencies. The much simplified section 25 will establish the term of available assistance. Amended section 26A will prevent circumvention of the intent of section 25 by ensuring that the device of corporate restructuring is not available to beneficiaries as a means of securing additional assistance under the commencement grant provisions or the preceding grants scheme.
The amendments made by Part XLIV of the Bill also relate to the cut-off date for lodging of applications for commencement grants. Previously similar provision had been made for this purpose by regulation but recent legal advice has expressed doubt about the validity of that regulation. The provision now to be made in the Act will provide for a cut-off date of 30 September next after the end of the grant year. Also there will no longer be a discretion to extend that date. The amendments will provide an automatic extension for lodging an application to 30 November next in each grant year provided the company concerned had notified, before 30 September, that it would be unable to meet that cut-off date. Where an application covered by that automatic extension to 30 November is received, the amendments made by the Bill will provide for deferral of payment of a commencement grant to the next financial year.

1006 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Statute Law Bill (No.2)

Navigation Act 1912
The main feature of the amendments made by Part XLVIII of the Bill to the Navigation Act 1912 is to provide that regulations made pursuant to section 232 of the Act may empower the Minister to make orders in respect of matters able to be prescribed by the regulations made under that section. The Bill also inserts a new section 295B in the Navigation Act to give effect to an agreement between the Commonwealth Government and the Government of Victoria to exempt historic shipwrecks and relics within the waters of that State from the provisions of the Act governing the salvage and disposal of wrecks. The amendments made by clauses 176 and 177 give to historic shipwrecks and historic relics within the meaning of a law of a State or of the Northern Territory relating to such shipwrecks or relics the same exemption as have Commonwealth historic wrecks from certain provisions of the Act.
Two further amendments made by the Bill will provide procedural improvements in the administration of the Act. A ship survey authority will be able to be approved by ministerial instrument rather than by the regulations. Furthermore, dangerous goods are now to be identified for the purpose of the carriage provisions of the Act, by express reference, to the International Maritime Dangerous Goods Code, which is an extensive and frequently amended publication of the International Maritime Organisation.
Overseas Telecommunications Act 1946
Under the original provisions of the Petroleum (Submerged Lands) Act 1967, laws in force in the relevant States and Territories, including Commonwealth Acts, are applied to activities connected with petroleum exploration and exploitation activities in the off-shore 'adjacent area' of the State or Territory in question as defind in the Schedules to that Act. With the entry into force of the Petroleum (Submerged Lands) Amendment Act 1980, however, such Commonwealth Acts will cease to apply automatically in adjacent areas. The scope of the application of Commonwealth Acts will then be a matter to be provided for specifically in the particular Act.
At present, the Overseas Telecommunications Commission (Australia) operates services to oil exploration and drilling platforms or structures on the continental shelf. Without the amendments made by part L II of the Bill to the Overseas Telecommunications Act 1946, the validity of the services provided by the Overseas Telecommunications Commission (Australia) in adjacent areas would be in doubt when the Petroleum

(Submerged Lands) Amendment Act 1980 comes into force.
These amendments therefore apply to the Overseas Telecommunications Act 1946 in the off-shore 'adjacent area' when the Petroleum (Submerged Lands) Amendment Act comes into force. Similar amendments are also required to the Postal Services Act 1975 in respect of services provided by the Australian Postal Commission and to the Telecommunications Act 1975, in respect of services provided by the Australian Telecommunications Commission. The necessary amendments are made by Parts LVII and LXX III respectively of the Bill.
Parliament House Construction Authority Act 1979
The main purposes of the amendments made by Part Llil of the Bill to the Parliament House Construction Authority Act 1979 are to: Enable the Parliament House Construction Authority to delegate its powers, except for the power of delegation, to the Chief Executive or another officer of the Authority; change the title of the Executive Officer of the Authority to that of Chief Executive; and enable a resolution to be deemed to have been duly passed if a majority of the members sign a document or documents stating that they are in favour of the resolution of the Authority as set out in the document or documents.
Petroleum (Submerged Lands) Act 1967
In part LIV of the Bill the boundaries of the Queensland adjacent area under the Petroleum (Submerged Lands) Act 1967 are amended to take into account the Australia-France Maritime Boundary Agreement signed on 4 January 1982. The Agreement requires the removal of a small portion of the Queensland adjacent area and allows the extension of the area over part of Australia's continental shelf not previously covered by the off-shore petroleum legislation.
Public Accounts Committee Act 1951
The Public Accounts Committee Act 1951 provides for the formation of sectional committees of three or more members and that the provisions applying in relation to the Committee, with some exception, apply to the sectional committees. Since the 1979 amendments to the Act difficulties have arisen in application of the quorum provision to those sectional committees which have only a small number of members. The amendments made by the Bill to section 9 of the Act specify the quorum of a sectional committee depending on the number of members of the Committee.

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1007

Science and Industry Research Act 1949
Part LXVI of the Bill amends the provision made by the Science and Industry Research Act 1949 which established the Commonwealth Scientific and Industrial Organisation State committees in each of the six States. In 1981, following representation from the Chief Minister of the Northern Territory, the Government established a committee in the Northern Territory administration. The amendment made by part LXVI now puts the Northern Territory committee on a statutory basis.
Primary Industry Legislation
Finally, a number of amendments of a minor nature are made by the Bill to certain primary industry legislation designed to facilitate the operation of various statutory bodies and to make other improvements related to the administration of that legislation. These provisions are explained in the explanatory memorandum that has been circulated. The Parts in question are Parts VIII, X, XV, XXV, XXVI, XXXI, XXXII, XLII, XLIII, XLV, XLVI, LV, LVI, LVIII and L1X. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
CUSTOMS TARIFF BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Hodgman, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce) (3.39)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
The Customs Tariff Bill 1982 now before the House provides for a new Customs Tariff Act to replace the Customs Tariff Act 1966. The requirement for a new Act results from the Government's decision to introduce a simplified tariff based on recommendations made in the Industries Assistance Commission's report of 30 November 1981 on Customs tariff simplification. The recommendations do not substantially affect the assistance afforded to Australian industry. In general the changes in rates of duty do not exceed plus or minus five percentage points with equivalent changes for rates not expressed in ad valorem terms.
The Bill contains some changes to the lAC's recommendations to take account of Government decisions taken on particular industry reports after the lAC had submitted its simplification report. It was also necessary to make a number of

other minor changes in line with the overall simplification objectives. Other variations have been made to take account of obligationsunder the General Agreement on Tariffs and TradeGATT-and bilateral trade obligations such as those under the Papua New Guinea-Australia Trade and Commercial Relations AgreementPATCRA-and the Canada-Australia Trade Agreement-CAN ATA. On 13 April the Government announced its decision to introduce a simplified tariff and indicated that the changes I have just outlined would be made to the lAC recommendations. In addition it was decided that the current tariff structures and rates of duty should continue for the time being where goods are covered by an lAC report not implemented at 2 July 1982 or due for report before I January 1983. It is intended that the new Act should come into operation on I January 1983. Amendments to take account of government decisions made before then, together with amendments to provide for New Zealand rates and some developing country rates yet to be decided, will be made by Gazette notice after proclamation of but before the date of operation. In its announcement earlier this year, the Government recognised that the Australian tariff had become, for the public and private sectors alike, an increasingly difficult document to work with.
This Bill includes a major revision of the sections of the current Act and a reduction in the number of schedules of rates of duty. Provisions for parts directions and concentrates orders have been eliminated, together with all references to support duties and primage duties. Significant reductions have been made in the number of tariff classifications and the number of different rates of duty contained in the present tariff. As lAC reports not yet finalised or still under consideration are implemented, it can be anticipated there will be further reductions. In summary, the simplified Customs tariff is easier to read with less complex schedules, and improved precision and clarity in description of goods and rates of duty. I am confident that users will find the new tariff better to work with than the present document. I know my colleague, the honourable member for Parramatta (Mr John Brown) who will lead for the Opposition, would wish to join me in warmly congratulating the team of officers who have taken on the task of introducing, for this notion, a simplified tariff. As I said, this Bill replaces the Act which has been in existence since 1966. I believe all officers in the Department are to be complimented along with parliamentary counsel. We have made a giant step forward in giving the business community of Australia a simplified

1008 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Customs Legislation

tariff from which to work for the benefit of their business and for the benefit of the community. I commend the Bill to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.

The Bill amends sections 8 and 10 of the principal Act where references are made to schedules of the Customs Tariff Act which have been renumbered in the Customs Tariff Bill 1982. I have much pleasure in commending the Bill to the House.

CUSTOMS TARIFF (MISCELLANEOUS AMENDMENTS) BILL 1982
Bill presented by Mr Hodgman, and read a first time.
Second Reading
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce) (3.44)-1 move:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
This Bill amends the following Acts: The Administrative Decisions (Judicial Review) Act 1977; the Consular Privileges and Immunities Act 1972; the Customs Act 1901; and the Sales Tax (Exemptions and Classifications) Act 1935. The Bill is complementary to the Customs Tariff Bill 1982 which I have just introduced and, with the previous Bill, makes the Customs trilogy. The amendments take into account the redrafting of the sections and renumbering of the schedules to the Customs Tariff Act, to which references are made in the various Acts. The only change of substance is the redrafting of the sections 151 and 151 A of the Customs Act 1901. These new sections need to be read in connection with the new section 15 of the Customs Tariff Act 1982 when determining the conditions under which certain

goods from specified countries are eligible for preferential tariff treatment. I commend the Bill to the House. I think that the officers who put these Bills together can enjoy themselves tonight.

TARIFF PROPOSALS
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce) (3.47)-1 move:
Customs Tariff Proposals No. 20 (1982).
The Customs tariff proposals I have just tabled relate to proposed alterations to the Customs Tariff Act 1966. Proposals No. 20 (1982), which operates from tomorrow, contains tariff changes resulting from the Government's decisions on recommendations by the Industries Assistance Commission in its reports on: Miscellaneous manufacturing group B: Dolls, toys and models; miscellaneous manufacturing group C: Articles of adornment including clothing and toilet accessories, et cetera; and fluorescent and filament lamps. In respect of miscellaneous manufacturing group B: dolls, toys and models, the Government has decided, except for some minor variations, to accept the Industries Assistance Commission's recommendations. For most goods under reference the lAC recommended that the general tariff rate reduced from 34 per cent to a rate of 20 per cent. The Government has decided to adopt a program of phased reduction to give the industry time to adjust to the lower duties. The duties to apply are 30 per cent general on implementation, 25 per cent after one year and 20 per cent after a further year. These reductions should assist in facilitating an orderly adjustment of production and related assembly and packaging operations especially on the part of those sheltered workshops involved. There are many throughout Australia. Young retarded and handicapped people in sheltered workshops play an important role in this industry. 'Toy' books and microscopes will continue to be dutiable at minimum rates and 'toy' radio remote control apparatus at 30 per cent general. In respect of 'toy' radio receivers the Government considered that the slight tariff simplification that would result from the lAC's recommended rate of 30 per cent general does not justify departing from the uniform rate of 35 per cent for all other radio receivers.
In respect of miscellaneous manufacturing group C: articles of adornment, including clothing and toilet accessories et cetera, the Government has decided in most cases on long term rates in accordance with the recommendations of the lAC.

Tariff Proposals

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1009

The long term rate of 20 per cent on buttons of synthetic and certain other materials will be phased in over a period of two years. Local production of these types of buttons has been assisted by specific rates which have provided a very high rate of assistance when applied to the cheaper utility type of button. In the main area of local production immediate introduction of the long term general rate could have led to a rapid increase in imports of this type of button and to a substantial consequential disruption of the local industry. The Government has decided that local producers should be allowed time to adjust to the revised duty regime. The phasing arrangements will not apply to button blanks.
In respect of picture frames imported with paintings, drawings and pastels executed entirely by hand, the Government has decided that minimum rates should apply instead of the 15 per cent recommended by the Commission. The general rates to apply to the goods under reference are: Buttons other than those referred to earlier, blanks for buttons, certain press fasteners, slide fasteners and parts there for, studs, cuff links, combs and hair slides will be dutiable at 20 per cent with the by-laws for button blanks being cancelled.
I come now to lay figures. I like to try to understand what these matters are about.
Mr John Brown-What are lay figures?
Mr HODGMAN-I am about to inform the honourable member. The words 'lay figures' puzzled me somewhat. I sought from the Department a definition of what was a lay figure. The reference is to 'lay figures other than tailor's dummies '. I can assist the House by saying that, according to the definition, lay figures are representations of the human body or parts of the human body-for example, the head, trunk, legs, arms and hands-used to display articles of clothing, headgear, stockings, gloves, et cetera. I do not take it further than that. But that is what lay figures are. I hope that everybody in the House and in the rest of Australia is now fully educated. Lay figures other than tailor's dummies will be dutiable at 25 per cent. Other goods covered by the report will be dutiable at minimum rates.
In respect of fluorescent and filament lamps the Government has accepted the lAC's recommendations for long term assistance. Fluorescent lamps will now be dutiable at 20 per cent and filament lamps will continue to be dutiable at 10 per cent. The previous long term rates on fluorescent lamps and filament lamps were 15 per cent and free-plus the 2 per cent special duty-respectively.

Mr Young-How much will that make them in the shop?
Mr HODGMAN-I am sure that my wife and the wife of the honourable member will find out a lot quicker than we will. These rates were subject to short term additional rates of 10 per cent and 8 per cent respectively since I April 1981 following the Government's consideration of an interim report by the Commission.
The proposals also contain tariff changes exempting sodium nitrate mixed or combined with potassium nitrate and horseshoe nails from the 2 per cent special duty. These changes are necessary to meet Australia's international trade commitments under the General Agreement on Tariffs and Trade.
Administrative amendments are also contained in the proposals. The wording of tariff item 42.03.3 is aligned with the format used elsewhere in the tariff, and the scope of tariff item 60.05.15 is clarified to remove a tariff classification-quota category anomaly which has occurred. This amendment ensures that those knitted tops which are the like of shirts and blouses are dutiable at the same rate as shirts and blouses.
A comprehensive summary setting out the nature of the changes has been prepared and is being circulated to honourable members. I commend the proposals to the House.
Debate (on motion by Mr John Brown) adjourned.
INDUSTRIES ASSISTANCE COMMISSION
Mr HODGMAN (Denison-Minister for the Capital Territory and Minister Assisting the Minister for Industry and Commerce)-For the information of honourable members. I present, on behalf of my colleague the Minister for Administrative Services, the reports of the Industries Assistance Commission on miscellaneous manufacturing group B, dolls, toys and models and miscellaneous manufacturing group C, articles of adornment including clothing and toilet accessories, fluorescent and filament lamps.
REMUNERATION AND ALLOWANCES AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 19 August, on motion by MrNewman:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
Mr HAYDEN (Oxley-Leader of the Opposition) (3.55)-ln supporting this proposal, I have a few comments to make in relation to some views which the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm

I010 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Remuneration andAllowances Bill

Fraser) has expressed on this matter, that is, specifically, his proposal that the rate of increase in the salaries of parliamentarians this year, cut back below the rate of increase in the cost of living, should be seen as a sterling example of wage restraint to be pursued by the rest of the community.
First of all, I make it clear that the Opposition supports the proposals which are before the House. It should be noted for the record that, over many years, if one traces the graph outlining movements in the consumer price index as a measurement of inflation in this country against salary movements for members of parliament, the movements in the salaries of parliamentarians have been below the movements in the cost of living. Similarly, movements in the salaries of parliamentarians over time, as a rate of increase, have been below the rate of the increase in average earnings. In those circumstances, we find no embarrassment at all in accepting or supporting the proposal which is before the House.
The proposal is for a 7 per cent increase in the salaries of members of parliament not effective until I October, which effectively means that the rate of increase is cut back to something like 5 per cent. That takes place in a year in which it is expected that the cost of living will move by something like II per cent. The Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Bill 1982 also makes provision for adjustments in other emoluments provided for members of parliament and in the salaries and emoluments provided for senior public servants and members of the judiciary. We quibble in no way at all with the proposals which are before the House.
However, I wish to dwell for a few minutes on the Prime Minister's proposal that this rate of increase of 7 per cent in the basic salary of parliamentarians for the period for which it applieseffectively 5 per cent over a full year-is a compelling example for the rest of the community to follow. I find it no such thing. Certainly it is no substitute in any way at all, even as an ingredient, in some sort of proposed wages policy. I am not quite sure what the Prime Minister is talking about when he talks about this and a range of adjustments which took place in the Budget in connection with income tax and certain welfare benefits as being a wages policy. They are no such thing.
Let us look at the issue of wage restraint. Certainly the rate of increase of 7 per cent for members of parliament for the period in which it applies or 5 per cent over the year falls well below the rate of inflation. Let us look at this in money

terms and focus on it in the way in which average members of the community would, that is, address themselves to it in the light of the experiences that they have had from day to day as they live in our society. They see us seeking to moralise about wage restraint on our part when we will get an increase of nearly $50 a week in our income.
A tradesman, for instance, on $250 a week, being expected to accept an increase of only 7 per cent in his wage over the course of this year, would see his income falling by $10 a week in real spending terms. I do not think anyone-certainly no one in this House-could suggest that $250 a week represents an ample inco,me for a very generous, comfortable standard of living for a man supporting a wife and two children today, more especially with the obligations of paying off a house, Already the interest rate policies of the Government-they are the interest rate policies of this Government-are designed to attract money from overseas into this country by establishing an extraordinarily high interest rate regime. The purpose is to paper over a very big hole in the balance of trade and the balance in current account to give an overall balance in the balance of payments. That is a very high cost for the rest of the community to pay. It represents about $20 a week extra in housing mortgage repayments. It is avoidable through other measures which I outlined in the course of my speech on the Budget.
The same family will be expected to pay an extra $2 a week in health insurance because of the Government's squeeze on State governments in relation to the provision of funds for their public hospitals. The consequence of that is that charges for non-public ward forms of inpatient treatment will be, and in some cases have been, substantially increased. Allowing for the fact that there are fairly stringent means tests for non-paying public ward treatment in all States except Queensland and Tasmania, there is very little choice for most families as to whether they pay this extra health insurance, The extra $2 will further deplete their income. The point I make is that in many ways it is an offensive nonsense for the Prime Minister to stand on any platform available and claim that we are exercising wage restraint when we will get an extra $48 a week and to go on and say that people on moderate to middle incomes should exercise similar restraint and settle for a 7 per cent increase. That means that they will suffer a reduction of $10 a week in their spending power in real terms. That is a savage depletion of people's living standards, Nothing that the Prime Minister says can be interpreted as a wages policy.

Remuneration and Allowances Bill

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1011

In any event, for a prices and incomes policy, which is the only thing we can credibly talk about, to be put in place it must cover things besides wages and salaries. It must cover non-wage income, profits and prices and it certainly must involve an effective smashing of the tax avoidance and tax evasion industry. That industry alone, as we graphically saw this week, apart from involving the mandarins of the Liberal Party at various State branch offices such as Western Australia, means that tax evaders are evading something like $3,500m a year in income tax and the avoiders are evading a similar amount, some $7,000m a year. It means that ordinary families have to pay it-that is pay an extra $35 a week on average for Australian households. Unless the Government cracks down and smashes this industry as one of the many ingredients of a fair and mutually agreed upon prices and incomes policy it is nonsense to talk about wage restraint.
The Australian Labor Party has been putting in place a wages and incomes policy and it recognises the case for justice, for fairness and for action on all those fronts I mentioned. The Budget proposals are derisory, just as their so-called benefits are illusory. My colleague the honourable member for Gellibrand (Mr Willis) has put together a series of unassailable calculations which show that a man supporting a wife and two children on average weekly earnings will be less than $3.90 a week better off in November when these benefits commence. That is a one per cent increase in spending power for someone on average weekly earnings in a year when the cost ofliving will increase by more than 11 per cent.
The trade union movement has sophisticated research units backing it. Some of the best qualified young people graduating from our tertiary institutions work in those research units. They are backed up by an array of other information sources. They quickly divine the fabrication behind the presentation of this sort of Budget. They immediately recognise that it is nothing more than a thin confidence trick. Accordingly, they will not cop this sort of moralising posturing, as empty as it is, on the part of the Prime Minister that he is giving them some big deal. He is giving them some big nonsense. Until the Government gets down to productive discussion with the trade union movement based upon the presumption that there is mutual respect between the consulting parties and until that is projected in those negotiations, this country will get nowhere. The Labor Party is getting somewhere. It has made a lot of headway in respect of a prices and incomes policy. The principles we are adopting in a general sense and our approach have

been endorsed by the Australian Council of Trade Unions. Much more detailed negotiation is to be undertaken. Unless we have a prices and incomes policy in operation, a cornerstone of successful economic management has been yanked out of the system. Being wrenched out of the system inthat way, the system will distort and lurch badly.
Unless we have this policy in place it is sheer fantasy ever to expect, in the difficult economic conditions prevailing here and internationally, to work for sustained growth of a higher order than we have been used to in the past few years. Growth will work. It worked in 1981. We saw a five per cent economic growth then; we saw employment levels coming down and the work force start to grow; we saw more prosperity than we had seen for quite a while. It was generated by a series of measures which expanded demand, but it was snuffed out. In 1981-82, in the wake of this, wage movements started to accelerate and ran over the contractionary measures of the Government. Why did they start to accelerate? Very simply, it was because in the past seven years the Fraser Government has applied a series of taxation policies and pricing policies on things such as health insurance, petrol, sales tax and excise duties which, apart from being very inflationary, also depleted people's living standards.
The trade union movement with its research knows that for an average family today to have the same standard of living in terms of real spending power it would have to have something like $40 a week more than it is getting today. In those circumstances any time economic recovery starts to show up there will be wage pressure as wage earners apply that pressure to their union movement to try to restore living standards, and the pressure comes from families in this country. If we had a prices and incomes policy we would have agreed guidelines. While real living standards could be sustained the rate of adjustment and progress could proceed according to those guidelines. Offsets could be built into the system to make allowances for benefits which are contributed. Our universal health insurance scheme would provide cheaper health insurance for three out of four families, and cover everyone in the community. We would have a better retirement allowance system. Benefits of that nature would be covered, as would reductions in the tax regime-real reductions, not the Mickey Mouse ones we have at present which, as I have illustrated, do not take us very far at all. It is very important that we understand that and get away from the empty propagandising of the Prime Minister. It deludes no family which is trying to make an ever-shrinking wage packet do as much as it

1012 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Remuneration and Allowances Bill

did seven years ago. Mr Deputy Speaker, I have nothing further to add to the matter.
Question resolved in the affirmative.

The Bill.
Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (4.10)~1 will not delay the House more than a few seconds. It is proposed to move an omnibus amendment. I will seek leave to move it in one part because it covers a number of parts of the Remuneration and Allowances Amendment Bill.
Leave granted.
Mr SCHOLES~ The purpose of the amendment is to transfer the power of disallowance in respect of decisions of the Remuneration Tribunal for the Australian Capital Territory Legislative Assembly to the Australian Capital Territory Legislative Assembly. In this Bill the Government is, in fact, transferring powers relating to Norfolk Island to the Norfolk Island Legislative Assembly. This amendment is in exactly the same terms and achieves exactly the same purpose in respect of the Australian Capital Territory. I apologise to the Leader of the House (Sir James Killen) for not having distributed the amendments. We have just received them. I think it is appropriate that the Australian Capital Territory Legislative Assembly should accept responsibility for determining whether it will accept the determinations of the Tribunal in exactly the same way as the Parliament accepts responsibility in respect of conditions fixed for this Parliament. The Government has done that in relation to other bodies which have a legislative or an elected function. The amendment has the effect of transferring to the Australian Capital Territory Legislative Assembly the power to disallow determinations of the Tribunal in respect of the Australian Capital Territory Legislative Assembly. I move:
(I) Page 6, clause 14, line 7, leave out 'section is', insert 'sections are'.
(2) Page 7, clause 14, after proposed new section 7A, add the following section:
Determinations relating to certain Australian Capital Territory public office holders
'7B. (I) In this section-
'Chairman of Committees' means the Chairman of Committees of the House of Assembly;
'House of Assembly' means the House of Assembly of the Australian Capital Territory;

'Minister' means the Minister for the time being administering the Seat of Government (Administration) Act 1910;
'Speaker' means the Speaker of the House of Assembly; and
'the Territory' means the Australian Capital Territory.
'(2) Where the Tribunal makes a determination under subsection 7 (3) relating to remuneration or allowances payable to a person who holds an office in or in connection with the House of Assembly that can be held only by a member of the House of Assembly, the Tribunal shall furnish a copy of the determination to the Minister.
'(3) The Minister shall furnish a copy of a determination furnished to him under sub-section (2) to the Speaker or, if there is no Speaker or the Speaker is absent from the Territory, the Chairman of Committees.
'(4) The Speaker or the Chairman of Committees, as the case may be, shall cause a copy of a determination furnished to him under sub-section (3) to be laid before, the House of Assembly within the period of 2 months after the determination is received by him.
'(5) If the House of Assembly, within the period of 2 months after a copy of a determination has been laid before the House of Assembly, passes a resolution disapproving of the determination, then--
(a) if the determination has not come into operation-the determination shall not come into operation; or
(b) if the determination has come into operation-the determination shall not have any force or effect in respect of the day on which the resolution was passed or any period after that day.
,(6) If, before the expiration of the period of 2 months after a copy of a determination has been laid before the House of Assembly-
(a) the House of Assembly holds its last meeting before a general election; and
(b) at the expiration of that meeting the House of Assembly has not passed a resolution disapproving of the determination,
the copy of the determination shall, for the purposes of subsection (5), be deemed to have been laid before the House of Assembly at the first meeting of the House of Assembly held after the general election.

The Government has not had an opportunity to consider all of those implications, As a consequence the Government rejects the amendment.
