Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1013

AIR NAVIGATION (CHARGES) AMENDMENT BILL 1982
Second Reading
Debate resumed from 19 August, on motion by MrFife:
That the Bill be now read a second time.
(Quorum formed).
Mr MORRIS (Shortland) (4.15)-The purpose of the Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Bill 1982 is to provide for increases in air navigation charges of 20 per cent in respect of domestic, trunk and regional airlines and 12 per cent for general aviation. We do not oppose the Bill as it is part of the Budget package. I stress that application of these increases will be from I October 1982, a date two months earlier than the traditional date of increases in air navigation charges. This Government then will grab an additional two months revenue-an extra $2m-from the domestic and general aviation sectors which will come, in fact, from passengers pockets. The latest imposts will increase cost pressures on the domestic airlines at a time when the industry is suffering an economic downturn and when traffic is almost stagnant. The increases will add at least I per cent to the overall operating costs of the airlines. The airlines will therefore seek to raise domestic airfares to recover the additional air navigation charge costs and this fare increase will, I believe, follow another which is already in the pipeline or is about to enter the pipeline of air fare increases.
The air navigation charge increases maintain the continuing attack of this Government on the domestic aviation industry. Instead of encouraging the airlines to be innovative in their marketing approach the Government is intent on adding to airline operating costs and ripping off air passengers through air fare increases that emanate from increases in government charges. Since the Fraser Government seized power in November 1975 there have been 14 air fare increases, the cumulative total of which is a massive 220 per cent. The effect of those increases and the effect of this Government's policies is that air travel is being increasingly priced beyond the reach of Australians. The accelerating level of air fares has hit hardest those people who live remote from the major capital cities, people in the north and far north of Queensland, such as Cairns, Townsville, the surrounding towns; in the Northern Territory; in the Pilbara; and in the north west of our continent.
The tourist industry throughout Australia, already suffering from government induced economic stagnation and inflation, is being further

damaged by the irresponsible high air fare policies of this Government. The detrimental impact of the Fraser Government's mismanagement can be seen in the fact that the number of air passengers carried in 1981-82 increased by a meagre 0.8 per cent over the previous year 1980-81. The hall marks of the Fraser Government's administration of aviation are incompetence and maladministration. We have seen this over the past seven years in the understaffing of the Department of Transport, the degradation of the supervision of air safety standards, the infamous treatment accorded the Holcroft Independent Public Inquiry into Domestic Air Fares, the Walter ultra-large fire tenders fiasco, the crippling shortages of air traffic controllers that led to the closure of Brisbane airport, the chaos that has been caused in the air services to Canberra-our national capitaland the massive 220 per cent increase in air fares since 1975,just to mention a few examples.
However the Government's irresponsible attitude to transport climaxed last May in the sacking of the Minister for Transport from his aviation responsibilities and in chopping of the ministry of Transport into a separate ministry of Transport and Construction and a ministry of Aviation. This action was not the result of any reasoned research or a competent assessment of national transport needs. It was not the result of representations from industry or the public. Rather it was the result of widespread dissatisfaction with the performance of the then Minister for Transport-the present Minister for Transport and Construction (Mr Hunt)-and brawling in the government Caucus room. The Liberals, on the one hand, wanted this Minister removed from transport while the National Country Party members, on the other hand, were not going to let control of road funds out of the hands of their Minister, especially when he was the kind of Minister who could ensure that scarce national road funds could be allocated to little used roads in his own electorate in preference to important coal export roads which were carrying 50 times as much traffic.
The creation of two separate ministries to handle transport administration is a backward step. In no comparable Western democracy is national transport administration split between separate ministries. The fact that it has been split in Australia where transport is absolutely vital to our economic performance, shows the low priority that national transport policy is now accorded by this Government. Let me, howev~r, compliment the former Minister for Transport In that he did have the wisdom to implement the

1014 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

Australian Labor Party's policies of having a separate organisation to set air fares and providing financial incentives to assist the expansion of Australia's international shipping fleet. They were two good things that he did. On this side of the House we say: 'Thank you, Mr Minister, for implementing our policies.' To his detriment, he confirmed earlier this year the Government's lack of concern about the level of air fares. He said that I should have known that air fares were not a concern of the Government. I know that very well
and I repeat it to the House and to the people of Australia.
His colleague the Minister for Aviation (Mr Fife), who is at the table, has the unique distinction of being the Minister who busily lauded the performance of the Narcotics Bureau, then under his supervision, while it was being steadily infiltra-
ted by criminals and people were being murdered. He is the Minister who sits on the edge of his seat alert and attentive every time the name Nugan Hand is mentioned in this place. He is also the Minister who suddenly discovered that there was a problem with Sydney (Kingsford-Smith) Airport. We welcome this new-found concern for Sydney Airport displayed by the Minister and the Government. It is rather interesting that this Minister was also a Minister in the Askin Government in New South Wales when it obstructed the attempts by the then Whitlam Government to de-
velop a co-operative solution to the problems of Mascot airport. In fact, he was the Minister for Transport in New South Wales for some part of his career. But that is enough of the two Ministers to whom transport and aviation have now been handed down.
I mentioned earlier that general aviation would
incur a 12 per cent increase in air navigation charges under the provisions of this Bill. General aviation is already struggling under the burden of increased sales taxes from last year's Budget and this year's Budget and from increased fuel and wages costs, all of which are combined with a fall-off in demand for the services of general aviation. There have already been widespread retrenchments in general aviation. The increased sales taxes and the increased air navigation charges provided for in this Bill will exacerbate the industry's plight. I mentioned that last weekend Mr Bill Bedser, the Vice-President Administration, of the General Aviation Association (Australia), who is recognised as an able administrator in the industry, called for an independent evaluation of costs in different types of aviation. In the report, which I will not read, in the Australian of 25 August 1982 Mr Bedser referred to the need for an independent analysis of the cost

attribution among the various sectors of the aviation industry. He referred to the need for vigilance in ensuring that there was efficiency of provision of the service that aviation needs. He referred to the need for an outside group of people to look inside into the Public Service, to look inside into the attribution of the costs of the services that are provided to the various sectors of the industry. As I mentioned, those remarks are provided in detail in the report of yesterday's Aus-
tralian that I referred to earlier. I believe that Mr Bedser's remarks were pertinent and appropriate because general aviation has been treated as the poor relation of the aviation industry during the seven disastrous years of Fraserism and its misguided policies. Mr Bedser's call for an independent evaluation of aviation sectors is sensible and reasonable. It ought to be adopted.
The provision of airport facilities, safety and meteorological and air navigation services generate the main expenditure that the imposition of air navigation charges seeks to recover. It is essential, therefore, that the provision of these services is responsibly planned and that those services are efficiently provided. Neither of these criteria has been met by the Fraser Government or its conservative predecessors since World War II. During the 30 years of conservative administration since 1949 this Government and its conservative predecessors have not been able to develop a comprehensive national airport program. Decisions on airport development are made on an ad hoc, unplanned basis, the main factor in the decision making process being the date of the next election and the narrowness of the margin by which the Government holds a seat. Scant regard has been paid to the priorities expressed by the industry or to the impact on the air fare structure of the future costs being generated by airport investment or by the provision of the services that are involved.
Let me stress: There is no strict accountability for the financial performance of the major airports. There is no attempt, nor has there been any attempt by this Government or its conservative predecessors, at a reconciliation of the costs and revenues of major airport operations. The Government knows how to hound the pensioners getting 20c a week too much or how to knock off benefits if somebody's income rises 10c above the fringe benefit means test level but the Government does not know how and does not want to reconcile the massive expenditure, on the one hand, and revenue on the other hand, that is involved in the provision of airport services.
Whilst classes of airport revenues can be identified to a fair extent from departmental accounts,

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1015

classes and apportionment of airport expenditure cannot be similarly identified. One would have thought that a government that asserts that it has a commercial background, that asserts that it is able to manage competently and that asserts that it understands enterprise and commerce, would ensure that there was proper accounting and identification of expenditures and that such procedures would be a basic requirement for the management of the millions upon millions of dollars involved in the provision of airport services, the cost of which air navigation charges seek to recover. The failure to provide detailed accounting for airport management means pork-barrelling decisions and hand-outs to favoured regions, and also that inefficiencies and waste can be more easily concealed. The result is that the industry is loaded with avoidable costs which at the end of the day have to be reflected in, on the one hand, higher air fares or, on the other hand, poorer services for air travellers in this country.
The problems of Sydney Airport are a prime example of the Fraser Government's incompetence. For 15 years the conservatives have fiddled with the airport needs of Sydney, preferring to politicise the issues involved rather than seek a co-operative and sensible solution to the problem. I mentioned earlier the new Minister for Aviation. He was a member of the Askin Government during the time the Whitlam Government was in power back in 1972-75 when it was seeking to find a sensible, co-operative solution to Sydney's air transport needs. It seems quite a long time ago. The conservatives then followed the same track-politicisation in Sydney in the New South Wales Parliament and here. Even today the Country Party-the party of the Minister for Transport and Construction, the former Minister for Transport-is conducting a political campaign throughout New South Wales country towns and local government bodies in an attempt to embarrass politically the Wran Government in New South Wales. Again politics is being given preference over proper planning and common sense in the provision of airport services.
I make it clear that to improve the provision of airport facilities and services a Labor government when it comes into office, whether it is this year or next year, will establish a national airports authority. That authority will have the responsibility for planning, developing and operating the major airports. It will have its own charter and it will for the first time be able to ensure that there is strict and full accountability for the financial performance of our major airports. It will give priority to the provision of efficient, improved services to the public and to the aviation industry.

I mentioned earlier the detrimental impact on Australia of this Government's high air fare policy, and how its policies have produced a massive 220 per cent increase in air fares since 1975. But there are other damaging aspects of the Government's attitude to air services that worsen the situation for those people for whom air travel is essential and is often the only means of transport. As air fares are forced higher and higher by this Government's mismanagement of the economy and by its ever increasing charges on the industry, it is imperative that every opportunity is utilised to minimise costs. Again, in this area the Government's performance has been deplorable.
For several years the Opposition-the Australian Labor Party-has pushed for the introduction of frills-free air services. By frills-free services we mean simple, safe, punctual services at the cheapest possible fare and those people who want the expensive extras pay the full cost of those extras. We believe that air services as a major segment of long distance public passenger transport should be available to as wide a range of the community as possible. We believe that Australian families should have the choice between low cost, safe, simple air services and the high cost premium-type services with their free alcohol and specialty foods for those who want them. In each case the fares charged for each type of service should fully reflect the cost involved in providing those services. Presently, the situation is that first class air passengers are still being subsidised by the fares paid by other passengers.
When Trans Australia Airlines offered a trial frills-free service at a 50 per cent off the standard fare rate last April it was rushed by passengers. Of course it was rushed. The frills-free half price fares enabled many thousands of Australians to visit their friends, to rejoin their families and to see their loved ones in other cities and towns. The frills-free concept-the 50 per cent off fare-has widespread support in the community. I believe its costs could be reduced even further on selected routes by the replacement of the prepaid fare, reserved seat system with, for example, a service with a set timetable where people find their own seat and paying their own fares after boarding the aircraft on a shuttle type air service along the lines of that which operates in some of the other Western democracies. This type of service works well in the United Kingdom and the United States of America. I have seen it in operation. I believe that at the very least it is the type of service which, with its minimum type fares, could be introduced on a trial basis on travel between some of the eastern ports of this country.

1016 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

Unfortunately, despite the success of TAA's frills-free service, it was frowned upon by this Government. The chief executive of Ansett Airlines of Australia, Sir Peter Abeles, had labelled the frills-free fares as a gimmick. Although Ansett fell into line with the frills-free service it was opposed to the concept. Trans Australia Airlines' analysis of the April experience showed a considerable increase in new patronage of air services. About two out of three of those passengers who travelled on the 50 per cent off fare during April were new customers. They were additions to the passenger market. However, as I have said, Ansett did not want the frills-free service. Within this Government-remember that this Government is a captive of the proprietors of the Ansett grouppressure was brought to bear to discourage Trans Australia Airlines from further reintroduction of the 50 per cent off fare or the frills-free service.

is directed at changing market shares when, instead, at a time of economic stagnation and increasing costs for the industry, the industry needs more than anything else not a change in market shares but an expansion in the market. I want to turn to another cost aspect of the airline industry-a matter about which this Government remains mute. I refer to the recent television advertisements by Ansett which depict the DC9 passenger liner as being decrepit and, by inference, unsafe. The DC9 aircraft has a great record of performance around the world and until recently was used by both Ansett and TAA. I find the derogatory type of advertising concernedparticularly that displayed on television-to be negative and fear-inducing. As I said earlier, at a time when the airlines desperately need new passengers this type of advertising is more likely to discourage intending first time air travellers from going by air rather than attracting them to travel by air instead of some other mode of transport. I understand that in some countries derogatory advertising of this nature is prohibited. I certainly do not believe that its cost should be borne by air passengers. It is a cost that passengers and the industry could well do without. Let the airlines concentrate on the positive side of the services they provide.
I want to deal now more specifically with the provision of air services to and within Western Australia. We have seen from time to time a phoney campaign from a number of Government back benchers partly against their Government's obnoxious two-airline agreement and partly in relation to air fares between the east and west of Australia. On both counts the campaign has been a masquerade designed to deceive Western Australians. Let me list the facts. This nation will be burdened with the inflexibility of the two airlineagreement and its heavy protection for Ansett for at least seven years. The same members who mutely endorse the long-standing monopoly of the principal intrastate services in Western Australia by the Ansett subsidiary Airlines of

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1017

Western Australia at the same time are carpingly critical of the performance of Trans Australia Airlines. We should remember that they, as members of the Government of the day, are responsible for the management of that airline. Those same members constantly criticise eastwest air fares but are mute on the level of fares of Airlines of Western Australia. I point out that it costs almost as much to fly north to south in Western Australia as it does to fly east to west across Australia. In fact, on a distance travelled basis, the north to south journey in Western Australia or vice versa is much more expensive than is the journey across Australia. We believe that TAA should have the right to operate intrastate air services within the boundaries of Western Australia.
At present the State Government, to protect its political friends, has denied TAA entry on to
those services and, therefore, has denied Western Australians the right to freedom of choice in the air services they wish to use. Various Western Australian Liberal Governments have, over the years, continually harassed and denied TAA's right to operate on intrastate air routes. Western Australian travellers have suffered through higher air fares and freight charges. The entry of TAA to the northern route to Darwin some years ago, after several years of court disputation, immediately brought competition to that route. It immediately resulted in the lowering of air fares and the improvement of overall services. There was an end to the take it or leave it service that had been provided by the State operator to that point and,
most importantly, for a limited part of the routes the people of Western Australia were given some choice.
For too long the Liberal State Government and this Government have made political decisions on domestic aviation within Western Australia without any thoughts as to the needs of the users, the Western Australian public, and Western Australian taxpayers who pay the costs of providing the infrastructure services that the airlines use. An incoming Federal Labor government would encourage competition in air services where economically possible and encourage TAA to operate intra-State air services on the main internal Western Australian routes. It would do so for one basic reason, that is, to give the people of Western Australia a fair go in the provision of services within that State. The tourist industry needs it and the people of the State need it. The people who have to live in the north and who are isolated from their families need a fair deal in services in Western Australia. Let us have an end to the cant in this place from honourable members from Western Australia with their blinkered eyes who

see only across Australia. They cannot see up and down in their own State and know that the people in Western Australia are being fleeced when it comes to air travel within that State.
Another damaging aspect of this Government's administration of aviation is its endorsement of
the declared policy of Ansett Airlines to confine ongoing travellers from regional feeder and commuter air services to the Ansett jet network. The so-called free market members opposite and this so-called free enterprise Government know full well that Ansett Airlines already owns Airlines of Western Australia, Airlines of New South Wales, Airlines of South Australia and Airline of Northern Australia. They know that Vodas Pty Ltd, which is owned by Sir Peter Abeles and Mr Rupert Murdoch, has purchased the Aeropelican Intercity Commuter Air Service operating be-
tween Belmont and Sydney, the fastest growing and most profitable commuter service in Australia. They already know that Bushies of Queensland-Bush Pilot Airways Ltd, which has now been changed to Air Queensland-has entered into an exclusive arrangement for oncarriage of travellers with Ansett Airlines, whereas previously Bushies allowed its ongoing traffic to be split between Ansett and TAA. Similarly, Wings Australia, whose head office is in the TNT building at Alexandria and which operates extensive commuter services in New South Wales, also feeds its passengers exclusively into the Ansett jet network. Just a few days ago a former senior Ansett executive, Mr Brian Grey, gained control of East-West Airlines, the last remaining regional regular public transport operator outside the Ansett umbrella. East-West Airlines has traditionaHy operated in association with Trans Australia Airlines and we would be strongly opposed to any change in those arrangements.
This Government's two-airline agreement provides for equal capacity between T AA and Ansett on the major air routes. It is thus predicated upon passengers having equal access to both jet networks. The monopoly policy being pursued by
Ansett, which is denying passengers ready choice between the airlines' jet networks, we believe is contrary to the public interest and it undermines the Government's own two-airline agreement policy. In strict commercial terms, its endorsement by this Government constitutes a further featherbedding of the Abeles-Murdoch group at the expense of the publicly-owned airline Trans Australia Airlines.
In summary, this Bill continues the upward pressure on aviation industry costs. It will force air fares higher and higher under the policy of this Government. It will make greater difficulty for

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

the general aviation industry. On coming to government, the Australian Labor Party will encourage the expansion of low cost air fares. We will establish a national airports authority to plan, develop and operate major airports. We will ensure that there is proper accountability of infrastructure costs and their attribution. We will ensure that there will be improved efficiency in the provision of services to the Australian aviation industry and, most of all, that travellers in this country, after many years of conservative administration, will get a fair deal.
Mr GROOM (Braddon) (4.45)-The honourble member for Shortland (Mr Morris) made quite a number of reasonable points-he made many points with which I would totally disagree-but he spoiled his speech, I believe, by his personal attacks, on the former Minister responsible for aviation, Mr Hunt, on the present Minister for Aviation (Mr Fife) and on the very active members from Western Australia on this side of the House who have taken a very active and concerned interest in the whole subject of air fares and airline services, particularly between Western Australia and the eastern States. In the Budget Speech last Tuesday night, the Treasurer (Mr Howard) announced that there would be some increases in navigation charges-a 20 per cent increase for domestic trunk and regional airlines and an increase of 12 per cent for general aviation aircraft. The Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Bill which we are now debating gives legislative effect to the announcement made by the Treasurer last Tuesday. Of course, the increases will apply from I October this year.
Total rt"venue from air navigation charges in 1982-83 is estimated to amount to $119.3m compared with $101.8m from this source in 1981-82. That is an increase of $17.5m. Of that increase $7.lm is attributable to the payment early in 1982-83 of amounts payable in respect of 1981-82 and the balance of $1 O.4m relates to the full effect of the 1981-82 variations to the charges, industry growth and increased revenue resulting from the increases in the charges which are now proposed. By themselves, the increased rates of charges for which this Bill provides are expected to yield additional revenue of $8.4m in 1982-83 and $12.5m in a full year. But, of course, the point has to be made that this money is directed towards improving the aviation services provided by this Government.
Honourable members will be aware that, since 1961, successive governments, of whatever political flavour, have pursued a policy of eventual full

recovery of costs properly attributable to the aviation industry for the provision and the maintenance of the aviation infrastructure around Australia. The decision taken by the Government to increase air navigation charges is in line, of course, with that policy. Overall cost recovery in 1982-83 is estimated, after adjustment for 1981 "82, at about 53 per cent. That is the total figure. The 1981 airlines agreement fully recognises the Commonwealth's entitlement to recover up to 100 per cent of its costs. The 20 per cent increase in air navigation charges for domestic trunk airlines is expected to achieve a recovery rate approaching 90 per cent for that sector in 1982-83. The increases in charges for regional airlines and general aviation are expected to lead to 1982-83 recovery rates from these sectors of about 25 per cent and IS per cent respectively.
What we are dealing with here is the user pays principle. I do not think anyone could argue against that kind of principle. But, at the same time, when the Government seeks to recover the real costs involved in providing these services from the industry and, ultimately, from members of the public, there is an obligation on it to ensure that the Department responsible for providing these services is efficient and, therefore, that the cost which is passed on to the fare paying passenger is reasonable. We have been told by the Minister for Aviation that the charges that we are concerned with in this Bill will increase the total costs borne by airlines by about one per cent. So there should be a very marginal impact on air fares around Australia. Mr Deputy Speaker, if you will permit me, I will make some general comments. I believe they are relevant to this legislation because it is concerned with costs. Those costs are ultimately borne by people who have to pay air fares around Australia. Mrs KELLY (Canberra) (5.2)-1 have listened with interest to the honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom) speaking in support of the Air Navigation (Charges) Amendment Bill. We find from the appropriation in the Budget Papers for the Department of Aviation that a Tasmanian travelling from a port in Tasmania to Melbourne is not charged according to a user pays principle but according to a subsidy principle. The amount of money provided in the Budget for the subsidisation of this leg is $4.4m. This is an incredible increase. Expenditure in this area in the last financial year was $1. 7m.
Mr Groom-What is that for?
Mrs KELLY-That amount of money is the air fare subsidy for travellers on route between Melbourne and Tasmania. The Government does not just work on the basis of the user pays principle in Australia generally, it works on the principle that if one is coming from Tasmania one does not have to make the same contribution to air fares as one would if one were flying from any other capital city or the national capital. Let us take the national capital. People who fly on the CanberraSydney route are the ones who have really been bearing the brunt of the burden increases in air fares over the last 12 months. Since April last year we have seen a massive increase of 58 per cent in the air fare between Canberra and Sydney. Who gets the benefits? The honourable member for Braddon knows about the user pays principle. He says: 'That is fine, providing it all goes back into aviation services and the people get benefits'. Again, I ask: Who are getting the benefits? We look around Australia and we look at the announcements in this Budget about the upgrading of airport terminals and tarmacs throughout Australia. Who are getting the benefits? It is not just those in the greatest need. A number of airports throughout Tasmania are being upgraded. There is to be an increase in the facilities at Hobart Airport in particular. Yet the Canberra terminal has a 50 per cent greater number of passengers and the facilities at Canberra airport are still not to be upgraded.
The Opposition does not oppose the Bill before the House but a few points need to be made about

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1021

the effect of these increased changes on the industry as a whole. The industry has suffered badly in recent years as a result of inadequate policies on fares, routes, capital works projects and the structure of the industry as a whole. Despite all the intervention by the Government, the result has inevitably been to the detriment of the consumer-higher fares, fewer services and less comfort at airport terminals. The situation in Canberra clearly illustrates this problem. All honourable members of this House will be aware of this because they all use the Canberra Airport. They are all well aware of the incredible need to upgrade facilities at that terminal. First of all, as I have said, Canberra travellers have been forced to pay a massive 58 per cent fare increase on the Canberra-Sydney leg since April last year. Even after the recommendation of the Independent Air Fares Committee that fares on this route be reduced by 7t per cent, it is likely that projected fare increases in the pipeline will wipe out this small measure of relief in the near future. Naturally, passenger numbers have declined dramatically. Passenger numbers through Canberra have dropped from 951,000 in 1980 to 827,000 in September 1981. Figures available from the Department of Aviation to June this year show that there has been a further decline of 5.7 per cent. This is an appalling situation which has had a very serious effect on the local economy and which, in particular, constitutes a major barrier to the development of the tourist industry in Canberra.
There is no doubt that the massive air fare increases have caused a serious distortion of traditionalload factors on this route. Just to emphasise this, I refer to the reply received by the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris) to a question on notice to the Minister for Aviation (Mr Fife). The question dealt with the number of Trans Australia Airlines and Ansett Airlines of Australia flights on the CanberraSydney leg and the Canberra-Melbourne leg that had been cancelled. The figures supplied in the answer are very disturbing. They show that between 28 August 1981 and 26 March 1982, 180 Ansett flights between Canberra and Sydney, representing 7 per cent of total flights on that leg, were cancelled. The number of TAA flights cancelled on the same leg amount to 18 per cent.
This has a very damaging effect on the number of people using this air link. If people cannot be sure that the aeroplane on which they are booked will actually take offI think many people in this chamber have found when they have turned up at an airport that their service has been cancelledthey are discouraged from using this service. A

great number of these flights were, in fact, cancelled because of the low load factor. The same thing applied on the Canberra-Melbourne leg. Be-
tween 28 August 1981 and 26 March 1982, 5.8 per cent of Ansett flights between Canberra and Melbourne were cancelled and the figure for TAA was just over 12 per cent. This does nothing at all to encourage the people to use the air service out of Canberra.
While it is true that the air navigation charges are a relatively small component of air fares, nevertheless the imposition of a further 20 per cent increase in air navigation charges, coming on top of a 12t per cent increase last year, will definitely put further pressures on air fares. Additionally, the Government's policy on airport development works appears to be highly discriminatory. Last week the Minister announced a large number of major development projects at airports throughout Australia, but there was no mention of Canberra. It would appear that Canberra air travellers are expected to cop the massive fare in-
creases, over-crowded and inadequate terminal facilities, an inconvenient schedule of flights and fewer services and yet at the same time pay more and more in charges and fees for the operation of the airport. I remind the Minister that as far back as the 1980 Budget a commitment was given to spend $16m on a new terminal, tarmac and taxiway at Canberra Airport to be completed by 1985. Apparently this promise has been allowed to lapse during the term of the current Minister for the Capital Territory (Mr Hodgman) although the Hobart project has got the go ahead, despite the fact that Hobart had only about half the number of air passengers as Canberra. I hope the Minister concerned will be able to inform us when the promise to upgrade Canberra's airport will be kept. Canberra travellers and local transport and tourist operators are sick and tired of continually paying large increases in charges while getting no improvement in facilities and services.
The general aviation industry already experienced considerable difficulties as a result of Government decisions in recent years to increase the cost of avgas and airport charges. Combined with the serious problems faced by all business in Canberra in recent years as a result of the Government's Canberra bashing policies, those measures mean that the general aviation industry here is in serious trouble. The latest round of increases in air navigation charges will only exacerbate the problems faced by these businesses. The problems I have outlined regarding Canberra's situation exist in general terms throughout Australia. Australia is a very large country where

1022 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

an efficient air network is a necessity rather than a luxury. It is important that all Australians should have the opportunity to see their own country, especially their national capital, as well as travel
here for business and family re-asons. At present, however, the structure of the industry prevents many Australians enjoying such travel. The major barrier is the enormous cost of air travel. The ordinary family person these days can no more think of buying an air ticket than flying to the moon. Some possible remedies for improving the situation are available and some of them have been mentioned in the debate today by both the
honourable member for Shortland and the honourable member for Braddon. One of these is the no-frills service, which I think needs to be
explored a lot further.
One other suggestion, which was put forward in the report of the Independent Air Fares Committee, was for a system to reflect the different costs associated with peak and off-peak flights, not only as between days of the week but also as to time of the day and time of the year. We need to have a much more flexible approach to looking at discounting and all the diferent types of ways of making air travel much more accessible to all people in Australia.
In relation to increases in navigation charges, while the principle of cost recovery in the aviation industry is not disputed, nevertheless it remains highly questionable whether increased charges should be levied without a corresponding fair share of improvements in infrastructure. The honourable member for Braddon says the increase in air navigation charges should be directed at an increase in aviation services. I have attempted to argue here today that that should be based on needs. It has been clearly demonstrated that there is a need to upgrade the facilities at Canberra Airport. We need a commitment from this Government that it will live up to its 1980 promise and follow that through. That promise has not been kept by this Government. It is not just a promise made to the people who live in Canberra; it is a promise that should be kept to the people of Australia because Canberra after all is the national capital and the gateway to Australia.
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Aviation) (5.15)-in reply-I wish to take two or three minutes of the time of the House to respond to a couple of points which were made during this debate. In the first instance I remind the House that during my second reading speech I drew attention to the fact that the total revenue from air navigation charges in 1982-83 is estimated to amount to $119.3m compared with $101.8m from this source in 1981-82. I also advised the House that

the 20 per cent increase in charges for domestic trunk and regional airlines is estimated to increase total airline costs-I emphasise the word 'cost'-by around only 1 per cent on the basis of
the information that has been published so far. The honourable member for Braddon (Mr Groom), and I think also the honourable member for Shortland (Mr Morris), drew attention to the cost recovery rates that are at present operating. The estimates for 1982-83 show that there will be full cost recovery for the international services. In 1982-83 the domestic trunk airlines will still provide only 90 per cent of the costs; the regional air-
lines, 25 per cent; and general aviation is meeting only 15 per cent of the costs. I mention those figures again to draw attention to the fact that the Government is still not requiring the industry generally to meet all of the costs. Such a burden could not be carried by the industry and the travelling
public at this time.
I am aware of a recent newspaper report re-
lating to a call by the General Aviation Association (Australia) for an inquiry into these costs. However, up to date I have not received any formal approach, but no doubt it is on the way. Because I have not received a formal approach from the Association I am not acquainted with the pre-
cise nature and scope of the inquiry which the association has in mind. I have noticed in the published reports the concerns that have been expressed by the General Aviation Association about the costs which the general aviation sector is being asked to bear. I think it is relevant to mention that the Aviation Industry Advisory Council has, in recent years, reviewed and reached broad agreement on principles for allocating aviation infrastructure costs amongst the various sectors in the industry. Of course, the General Aviation Association is represented on this body. Moreover, I should inform honourable members that my colleague, the Minister for Transport and Construction (Mr Hunt), and I are completing arrangements for a study by the Bureau of Transport Economics of the methods of calculating the overall level of cost to be attributed to the aviation industry for cost recovery purposes. I hope that this study will be of considerable assistance in providing a better understanding of the present cost recovery practices as they affect the industry. My predecessor with responsibilty for this portfolio, the present Minister for Transport and Construction, undertook to allow the Aviation Industry Advisory Council-that is the body I have just referred to-to scrutinise the departmental expenditures. This undertaking given by my predecessor is being put into effect. I believe that it also will assist the industry in understanding the cost and

Air Navigation (Charges) Bill

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1023

how those costs are met by both revenue on the one hand and charges on the other.
Another point I would like to touch on very briefly is the comment made by the honourable member for Shortland in relation to discounting by airlines. I did not hear him clearly but I think that he either said or inferred that the Government had brought pressure to bear on Trans Australia Airlines in relation to discounting. I want to make it abundantly clear that at no time have I indicated to TAA that it should desist from whatever commercial practice it desires within the law. That obviously applies to discounting. The House needs to be reminded that there is an Independent Air Fares Committee-the concept of that Committee has the support of both sides of this House-which determines fares. It also has a responsibility in relation to discounting. The Independent Air Fares Committee Act makes specific provision for the approval of discount fares at the request of an operator. In considering an application the Independent Air Fares Committeeis required to be satisfied that the fare will improve profitability and be unlikely to result in an increase in the trunk operator's economy fares, and that the fare conditions are reasonable and non-discriminatory. I give that undertaking. I give an undertaking also that I will not be interfering in the commercial activites of TAA in the way that I understood the honourable member to imply.
The only other point I wish to make in the time that is available to me is that the Commonwealth Government recognises that a large number of airports throughout Australia need to be upgraded. A large number have already been included in a program. Cairns, Townsville, Brisbane, Adelaide, Perth, Hobart, and a large number of other regional cities just to mention a few, are included' in the four-year program to which the honourable member of Braddon has referred. I am aware that others are still to be included in the program.
Mrs Kelly-When?
Mr FIFE-Priority has to be determined. Let me respond to the honourable member for Canberra. The program that this Government has approved and announced in the context of this Budget is the largest and most ambitious airport upgrading program in the history of Australia.
Mrs Kelly-But when is Canberra coming into it?
Mr FIFE-The honourable member knows that approval has been given in principle for a new terminal in Canberra. Indeed, she reminded the House of this fact during her speech on this

Bill. I am unable to say at this stage when it will be programmed in the same way as I am unable to say when a number of other projects around Australia will be programmed. In 1982-83 a record sum in real terms will be spent on airport development in Australia. I see that the honourable member for Lilley (Mrs Darling) is in the House and is taking note of what I am saying. She will be interested to know that a large part of the money allocated to the four-year program is being spent in Queensland, and in particular in Brisbane. These projects are very costly and obviously have to be spread over a period.
I thank those honourable members who have contributed to this debate. I again give an undertaking to the House and to the people of Australia that the Commonwealth Government will continue to be sensitive to the needs of the aviation industry and, in particular, to the travelling public.
Question resolved in the affirmative.
Bill read a second time.

Mr KENT (Hotham) (5.24)-During the first part of my speech which I made in this House yesterday I drew the attention of the House to the similarities of this year's Fraser Budget with that of 1977. The 1982 Budget, like the Budget of 1977, is based on rubbery and shonkey estimates. It offers illusory instead of real tax cuts. I predicted also that the Budget will disintegrate in a few months time. However, I still welcome some of the concessions that the Government made in this Budget, no matter how reluctantly they were made by our mean Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser). We fought this Government for years until it grudgingly agreed to increase the family allowance, and to give other long overdue assistance.

1024 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No. J)

I welcome these measures even though the Government acted too late and gave too little. The fact that this Government gave grudgingly is illustrated by the fact that the benefits offered~ little as they may be~start in November and, in one case, in May next year while impositions such as the tax slug is immediate. It is not just a case of the Fraser Government taking away with its right hand what it gives with its left. But the hand that slugs is moving fast while the motion of the other hand is somewhat tardy to say the least. The electors of Hotham, as indeed everywhere else, have lost their faith in this Government. To put it another way, the Fraser Government has lost its credibility with the Australian voters. It is no
wonder that this is so as the Government cannot fool all of the people all of the time. The Treasurer (Mr Howard) cannot get away with offering illusory tax cuts while budgeting to collect more tax revenue than that which was collected the year before. Of course, the electors are asking where the money is coming from and who will pay for the small electoral bribe that the Prime Minister is flashing around. We do not have to look too far for an answer. I quote now from the Melbourne Age editorial from which I quoted yesterday. It states:
The Treasurer has summed up his package of surreptitious take and conspicuous give as a 'family Budget'. It could be more aptly and accurately described as a voter's Budget. Its concessions are aimed at middle Australia: the family man with a wife at home and two children at school who is paying off his home, owns a few shares and doesn't drink or smoke. He is, of course, not the only one to benefit, but he will do best. Or appear to do so. Mr Howard gives the example of such a family man on average weekly earnings and calculates that, after allowing for the effect of indirect taxes (including those on beer and tobacco), he would be better off by about $17 a week. What Mr Howard didn't mention is that inflation will claw back most of this apparent generosity. As for the poor, the jobless and the homeless, most of them can expect to remain poor, jobless and homeless.
Indeed, while the Treasurer was delivering his Budget Speech last week and extolling its virtues the economy around him was collapsing due to the Fraser Government's economic mismanagement.
As I have said before, due to this Government's policies we are entering into the worst period of economic recession since the Great Depression. For seven years the Fraser Government has been promising that recovery was just around the corner. A couple of years ago our Prime Minister said~1 am glad that he is in the House now~that interest rates would fall by 2 per cent and would keep falling. There has been nothing of the sort. Our interest rates are the highest in the world and are still rising. The Prime Minister promised that his Government would bring down inflation. In fact our inflation rate is rising and is higher than

that of our trading partners. The Prime Minister announced that there would be jobs for all who wanted to work, yet this Prime Minister presides over the highest unemployment in our post-war history. There is more to come~~

in his Budget Speech. As a matter of fact he was all gloom and doom. He could not hide any longer the failure of his Government to manage the economy. Of course the Treasurer and the Prime Minister are desperately trying to find some excuses. The Treasurer must have remembered the story of an American President who had a sign on his desk that said: 'The buck stops here'. 'Good', thought little Johnny Howard, the Treasurer, 'I will pass the buck up there'. So he is now blaming the American economy for the Fraser Government's failure. But even that misfired; the American interest rates fell in the last few weeks while ours soared. When our interest rates went up, Mr Howard said that the rise followed rises in the United States interest rates. Why is it that, when United States interest rates fall, ours do not follow? I do not think Mr Howard can pass the buck on this one; he has to accept the blame himself.
While I am talking of interest rates, let me mention that the Government has desperately tried to confuse Australian home buyers. It scrapped its home savings grants scheme, introduced another one instead with a means test and introduced a tax interest rebate scheme which will not benefit the poor or the pensioners who are struggling to pay off their homes. Whilst this scheme may slightly assist some home buyers, the Melbourne Age warns in the following terms:
However, the beneficiaries of this election bait should beware: It may be a prelude to further relaxation of controls over housing interest rates, leaving them worse off than before.
It is obvious that the Government has lost the little remaining credibility that it had. Only yesterday, the Government was exposed for harbouring tax evaders. It refused for years to prosecute the tax bludgers while at the same time it harassed invalid pensioners and other social security beneficiaries. This lenience towards tax dodgers resulted in a loss of $10 billion to $15 billion. This is in stark contrast to the Government's eagerness to prosecute pensioners. A total of 3,697 social benefit recipients were mercilessly prosecuted in the last two years whilst there was not a single prosecution initiated by the Treasurer against his tax avoiding mates.
Let me turn briefly to one other aspect of the Budget. It fails to assist our manufacturing industry to extricate itself from the crisis that this Government's policies caused. Some time before the Budget, there was an announcement of concessions to business and industry. But honourable members should keep in mind that the concessions will not cost a single penny this financial year. When the benefits which are due start

operating next financial year, having regard to the Government's track record, they will most likely be withdrawn. In contrast to that, the Treasurer introduced a new tax on cheque transactions to start this financial year. That will have an immediate detrimental effect on business and industry as well as slug the individual cheque account users. I wonder whether this tax was introduced by the Treasurer to deter his colleague Mr Sinclair from signing too many cheques and thus reduce the risk of another scandal for this scandal-prone Government.

down by the Treasurer (Mr Howard) a few days ago has been welcomed by--

Mr MALCOLM FRASER-The Budget brought down by the Treasurer a few days ago has been welcomed right around Australia by many different groups because it has been directed to the specific and real needs of Australians as they face the problems ahead in 1982-83. The tax cuts announced by the Treasurer are substantial and significant. It is worth noting that the Australian Taxpayers Association, which is an independent body, has sent out a document to its members which largely substantiates the Government's view that the cuts as they relate to Australian families from 1 November onwards are substantial and significant. The home interest relief goes right to the heart of the matter that is very important to a very large number of Australian families. We all know the difficulties that have been brought to the fore as a result of rising interst rates overseas and the impact of them on Australia, and the measure of support and relief that the Government has given will be of help to many hundreds of thousands of Australian families.
The additions to the housing finance and welfare housing will be particularly welcomed not only because of the additional people who will be assisted through the course of this year as a consequence but also because it will add to activity and to employment in a depressed period. The level of welfare housing is up by 27 per cent. Family allowances for the first two childen have been very significantly increased by 50 per cent, bringing over the last two years a total increase of 50 per cent in the family allowance system which this Government pioneered. On top of that, this Government has come forward with a further major first in the social security and social welfare area, the family income supplement, to offer special and particular support to families with

children, lowest paid working families, who might otherwise be better off on the welfare system than working. Every member of this Parliament will be able to go out and say that single income families with children no matter what the circumstances, will be better off than they would be if that same family were on welfare. That is a very substantial improvement on the earlier system. A number of families will be $10, $20, $30 or $40 a week better off as a result of this Budget, dependent of course upon the number of children that they may have.
The spouse rebate has been increased where there are children in the family. I think it is particularly important that the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme allowance for tertiary students and the secondary allowance to cover low income families to try to persuade them to keep their children at school for the last two years of secondary schooling have been increased substantially. The means test for the secondary allowance has been increased by 44 per cent, making it the same as the TEAS allowance. As a result a much larger number of low-income families will be covered by the allowance. I think all honourable members know that it is those young Australians who leave school at the minimum age or as soon as they get past the compulsory school age who are the most at risk in a difficult world because they leave school often with a sense of frustration and without any particular academic or manual skills. To persuade these young Australians to stay in school or to stay in training is an important objective of the Government and is one that I am sure is supported by an overwhelming majority of Australian families.
The employment and training programs are related to this group also. Much larger sums are being provided this year. Over 250,000 Australians will be assisted through those programs in 1982-83. While pensioners will experience benefits from the general reductions in taxation or the lift in the threshold and the reduction in the tax rate from 32c to 30c, the special pensioner rebate is also being introduced to give further benefits to pensioners with some income of their own. In addition, the income test free area has been significantly improved and the income allowable before a pensioner loses the pensioner health card has been significantly expanded. These improvements are being widely welcomed.
A development of a different kind is the Australian Bicentennial Road Development Program. It is an imaginative program, which over the period to 1988, will give this country a road network of which it can be truly proud. That and other changes we are introducing will lead to

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1027

more efficient transport and more efficient competition between road and rail. The program also will be of special benefit to local government in this difficult period through increased funds for local roads and increased expenditure on arterial roads. Significant funds also will be available under the program for urban transport proposals and urban arterial roads. That program will come to be appreciated in the years ahead by an increasing number of Australians as they see the benefits of the work done.
Against that background, the speech of the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) was somewhat disappointing. Towards the end of it he said:
What Australia needs is a new outlook altogether.
However, if one reads through the speech one will find that only one new out1ook~it is as old as the Australian Labor Party itse1f~really seemed to come through; that is, that large additional wage increases are appropriate at the present time. The Leader of the Opposition attacked the Government quite vigorously for trying to achieve this year wage settlements significantly less than the rate of inflation. He indicated that that was a quite unreasonable thing to do. Against a background in which Australian industry is losing competitiveness~not insignificantly because of the wage increases of last year and not insignificantly because of the shorter working hours of last year~and against a background of very substantial real wage increases of several per cent over the last two years, quite plainly the views of the Leader of the Opposition on that subject would only lead to even higher unemployment and greater hardship amongst the people he is purporting to help.
There were some curious ideas from the Leader of the Opposition. He said that he thought his front bench had ideas to offer. Not only has the left jeopardised the unity and solidarity of the Caucus by its offer to Mr Hawke, but in responding to pressures from Messrs Carmichael and Halfpenny, the left (in their support of Mr Hayden) have left the Party open to charges of outside inHuence.

The honourable member for Blaxland was only recognising the reality of something that we have always known existed. The honourable member for Reid (Mr Uren) was also active over the same period. There was some doubt about which side he would ultimately be on, and then he stated:
The re-election of Bill Hayden was an achievement of the left collective.
It would be interesting to know what is the honourable member for Reid's definition of the left collective, but I have no doubt that that was well noted. The Leader of the Opposition also ought to have included the activities of some private members of his party in his praise of their active interest in certain events over the last few weeks and months. The honourable member for Prospect (Dr Klugman) said very plainly:
But once Hayden was endorsed by Communist Party officials outside I think it's impossible for us to win with him as leader.

Your followers are asleep.
The Leader of the Opposition responded with this brilliant remark:
One scarcely notices the difference.

Mr Hayden~ You and I know that you are much safer when you are asleep.
Mr MALCOLM FRASER~You are both very vulnerable and that makes no difference. It is perfectly plain that the Australian Labor Party has no alternative policy. Sitting suspended from 5.58 to 8 p.m.
TEMPORARY ASSISTANCE TO IRON AND STEEL INDUSTRY
Report and Ministerial Statement
Mr FIFE (Farrer-Minister for Aviation and Acting Minister for Industry and Commerce)- Pursuant to section 30B (2) of the Industries Assistance Commission Act 1973 and on behalf of the Minister for Industry and Commerce I present a report prepared by the Temporary Assistance Authority on certain flat steel products, pipes and tubes of iron or steel. I seek leave to make a statement relating to the report.
Leave granted.
Mr FIFE-The Temporary Assistance Authority report recommends that a 10 per cent temporary duty be imposed on certain pipes and tubes and that the developing country preference accorded imports of the goods under reference from the Republic of Korea be removed. The report also recommends that an import watch be placed on all goods under reference except certain pipes and tubes. The Government has given long and careful consideration to the question of temporary assistance to those sectors of the iron and steel industry covered by the report. We have decided that the Authority's recommendations would not provide adequate short term assistance during the period until the Government's decision on the Industries Assistance Commission's long term report on the industry. The Government felt that temporary assistance in excess of that recommended by the TAA was necessary in view of the seriously depressed situation in the industry and the impact of import pressures when global demand is also at a depressed level and serious world overcapacity exists.
A system of tariff quotas will be introduced to allow individual importers to import at their 1981-82 levels at the current duty rates. Imports above the quota level will be subject to an additional temporary duty of 150 per cent. Assistance by way of tariff quotas was chosen because it preserves a degree of flexibility of choice for users. At the same time the Government recognised the effect of additional protection, and of any resultant steel price increases, on costs within the community generally, and in particular on the competitiveness of user industries. It also did not wish to discourage any restructuring the industry might take to improve its long term viability. The tariff

quota levels have been set with a view to providing the industry with a breathing space until the Government has considered the longer term future of the industry after receipt of the lAC report next year. At the same time care has been taken to minimise additional costs to users and consumers.
In a meeting with the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) and other senior Cabinet Ministers yesterday afternoon Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd stressed the decline currently appearing in the domestic steel market. The company requested that the Government in reaching its decision make every effort to distribute the burden of any future decline in the market equitably between local industry and importers. The Government carefully considered the company's request but did not consider that the information presented by BHP warranted any change to its earlier decision.
The Government recognised that due to the cyclical downturn, the longer .term industry restructuring and the regional concentration of the industry, some workers leaving the industry may face adjustment difficulties.
The Commonwealth Employment Service has already mounted a special effort in the Port Kembla-Wollongong area using existing manpower programs. Given the special knowledge of the needs of the industry's workers which the companies and unions in the industry have, the Government will be inviting them to bring forward jointly proposals for a program of adjustment measures which might more effectively utilise existing Government manpower programs in the area of retraining, relocation and employment services. Details of the Customs tariff changes arising from the Government's decision will be announced in the Parliament when administrative arrangements to implement them have been completed.
Mr HURFORD (Adelaide)-by leave-My speech will be short because the Government Whip has very kindly agreed not only that should I be able to say a few words on this important subject, the future of the Australian steel industry, but also that the honourable member for Cunningham (Mr West) should be allowed to say a few words. I know that the honourable member for Grey (Mr Wallis), who is in the House at present and whose electorate covers Whyalla, would also like to say a few words. I really have not had time to organise that. Whether he will be able to speak will have to be left to the good grace

Iron and Steel Industry

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1031

of the Government Whip. Although the Australian Labor Party welcomes some temporary assistance for the ailing Australian steel industry I have to say that we regret the many depressing features of the Government's decision which was announced tonight in the statement by the Minister for Aviation (Mr Fife).
Giving quotas at last year's level, which is the basis of what the Minister has had to say, at a time when we are suffering such a great depression in this country-mainly because of the monetarist policies that have been followed by this Government-will mean a further drop, a damaging cut, in Australian steel output. The result of that will be a further drop in the number of jobs available in the Australian steel industry. I hardly need to tell this House what that means to hundreds and hundreds of Australian families living in Wollongong, Port Kembla, the Whyalla district and Newcastle. I hardly need to add that that drop in jobs and the effect it will have on the Australian families in those regions will only add to the misery that has already been created by the drop in output. For instance, I refer to Kwinana where 600 jobs have gone.
Much of the Government's statement tonight which we have just heard from the Minister is taken up with giving details of the availability of government employment services. But that availability of service and those details relate to the Government making plans for unemployment: I want to draw a strong contrast between this Government's attitude to making plans for unemployment and a Labor government's attitudethe Labor Opposition's attitude at this time-to making plans for employment. It is not unemployment for which we should be organising; we should be making plans for employment in the steel industry in such a way that we will make Australian steel competitive with imports. Labor supports any adjustment programs for unemployment-miserable though they arethat this Government announces. But what we want more than that is Government leadership in planning the future of this industry. Those plans were singularly absent from the message the Minister had to give this House tonight. We have only to contemplate the indecision in relation to this matter over the last week or two to realise just how lacking in plans this Government is.
Firstly, we know that the Temporary Assistance Authority reported some weeks ago. On top of that we learn that the Government came to a decision yesterday and that that decision was announced in the party room. It had the courtesy to call in the management of BHP. I notice that it did not call in any union representatives, who are

equally involved in this decision. Having listened to the management of BHP, Government members decided that the decision they had come to was not satisfactory so they got out of their beds a little earlier this morning and met at 8.30 in the Cabinet room to reconsider their decision. In fact, they discarded the representations of BHP and came up with the decision which we have learnt of tonight.
What is needed is not just joint participation on the part of unions, companies and government in planning unemployment relief but also collective action involving all three-unions, management and government-to map out a long term future for the Australian steel industry, to build a strong and public commitment to the future of steel production in Australia, to harness the confidence that such a commitment would create and to find a major reinvestment and re-equipment program for Australian steel. We in the Australian Labor Party recognise that this country needs steel to be produced at competitive prices because if it is produced at higher prices there are problems for the downstream industries. We do not resile from that in one way. We want to see government leadership to make sure that there is tripartite organisation, to ensure that steel is produced at a reasonably competitive price and also to achieve productivity in this country. We acknowledge that under such a plan the steel industry would look different from the way it does now but it would be in a much better shape to combat more effectively the current intense competition that comes from overseas. It would deal with such pressures from a position of strength and not-as the lack of positive policies from this Government makes certain-a position of serious and growing weakness. As the Whip is behind me I must conclude. I thank the Government not only for the opportunity for me to say a few words but also for allowing the honourable member for Cunningham to add to what I have had to say.

The difficulty with this decision is that which always attaches to any quota system. We have new importers with material on the water who will not have a quota unless some allowance is made for them. I trust that these quotas will be freely negotiable, but nothing has been said about that. I urge the Government to ensure that they are freely negotiable. It is not only the importer who might be worried but also the person who will be using the imported steel. Steel is a variable quantity. Some steels do not substitute for other steel. It will be necessary, even within a 12-month period, to cater for new demand. For that reason alone, the quotas must be negotiable. This decision is a watershed in government understanding. I wish it were also a watershed in Opposition understanding. The Government has realised that by imposing high levels of protection it will not preserve jobs but actually destroy them.
Mr WEST (Cunningham)-by leaveUnfortunately, this decision will be cold comfort for the steel industry in Australia. Obviously, the original recommendation from the Temporary Assistance Authority that a temporary duty of 10 per cent be imposed on certain finished steel products was insufficient. It is not surprising that the Government realised that it could not meet the
problem simply by accepting that recommendation. Steel imported into Australia ranges in price up to $400 a tonne. That is up to $100 a tonne cheaper than the local product. Tariffs of up to 25 per cent would have been required to have any effect. It is quite obvious why that option was disregarded.
The decision to peg imports of finished steel products into Australia at 1981-82 levels, with all imports over that quota being subjected to the prohibitive duty of 150 per cent is not good enough either. What the Government has effectively done is to peg steel imports at their highest historical levels. In 1981-82-the year that the Government will use as a bench mark-steel imports to this country in raw steel equivalent amounted to one million tonnes. In 1978-79 the figure was 580,000 tonnes. John Lysaght (Australia) Ltd has a rolling mill which manufactures finished steel plate and galvanised iron products. Imports of 320,000 tonnes per year were directly competing with that company's products in the first six months of 1982. If the request of Broken Hill Proprietary Co. Ltd had been accepted, imports would have been pegged at 175,000 tonnes.
There are a number of problems in the steel industry. We accept that. They relate to the domestic recession for which this Government has to take responsibility, the world downturn in steel,

the level of imports and the rate of technology that is being introduced into the steel industry. The BHP steel division at Port Kembla, for instance, has moved its rail bank to Whyalla. It has closed No. 2 open hearth as it has expanded its basic oxygen steel making process by implementing an extra steel making vessel there. Of course it is introducing that type of technology.
Imports are a very important component of the present crisis and to freeze them at the historically high levels of 1981-1982 is just not good enough. In this situation of rocketing unemployment the company is not just threatening but making direct plans and actually implementing them to run its labour force down by 10,000. BHP has already run down its labour force in Port Kembla by 1,500 this year. It expects that figure to increase to 3,000 by early next year. Lysaghts in Port Kembla will close for one week in several weeks' time during the school holidays. It has told this Government-the Minister for Industry and Commerce (Sir Phillip Lynch) knows this-that if the import situation cannot be properly controlled, in the three plants, one at Westernport, Victoria and the two at Port Kembla will face a labour run-down of up to 1,400 jobs in the first three months of next year. I am told 1,200 of those threatened jobs are at Port Kembla. I wonder what the Minister for Industry and Commerce thinks since Westernport happens to be in his electorate. This would be quite amusing if it were not so tragic. I refer to a statement at the conclusion of the speech by the Acting Minister for Industry and Commerce (Mr Fife). He said:
The Commonwealth Employment Service has already mounted a special effort in the Port Kembla-Wollongong area using existing manpower programs.
Those existing manpower programs are quite inadequate to handle the present 13 per cent unemployment rate and the run-down in the steel industry which is increasing. As I have said unemployment in that area, as it is in Fremantle, Whyalla and Newcastle is growing faster than honourable members opposite could ever believe. So to talk about the Government's existing manpower programs, when faced with a run-down of 10,000 to 15,000 jobs in the steel industry, plus the multiplier effect proves that the Government is living in cloud cuckoo land. I conclude by saying that it is the Opposition that has the real plans for the restructuring of the steel industry. They include setting up a steel industry council, a task force to investigate the problems of the industry with representatives of the unions, the companies and government upon it, and making any assistance offered to the steel industry dependent on a guarantee that the steel industry companies avoid
