APPROPRIATION BILL (No.1) 1982-83
Second Reading
(Budget Debate)
Debate resumed.
Mr WILLIS (Gellibrand) (8.34)-Prior to the suspension of the sitting the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) made his contribution to this Budget debate. The Prime Minister paraded his normal farrago of falsities and fancies, most of which were directed at attacking the Opposition rather than defending his Government's Budget, which shows just how much conviction he has about the relevance of that Budget to the nation's need at present. Of course, the Prime Minister is in a very difficult situation. He is a man who came to the leadership of this nation proclaiming that he had the answers to Australia's economic needs and that he could produce growth and jobs. Of course, we have seen a very sad situation in the Australian economy over the period in which he has been Prime Minister. On I August 1980 the Prime Minister said:
The justification for our claim to Government is our capacity to secure sustainable economic growth.
I ask the House to take particular note of those words. I will repeat them. He said:
The justification for our claim to Government is the capacity to procure sustainable economic growth.
What has he just done? A week ago he had his Treasurer (Mr Howard) bring into this House a Budget which provides for no economic growth at all. On the basis of that Budget the Prime Minister has forfeited his claim to government. If he claims government on the basis of economic growth and he produces Budgets which provide for no economic growth, he has forfeited his claim to the leadership of this nation and his party's right to be the government of this nation. That is the basis on which the Opposition attacks this Budget. This Budget is totally inappropriate to the present needs of this nation, as have been all previous Fraser Government Budgets.
What has happended to the Australian economy in the period in which the Fraser Government has been in office is a terribly sad story. At present there is no growth in Australia. What is happening in other countries? The Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development has forecast that growth in the OECD region as a whole-that is, the Western developed countries-in 1982-83 will be 2t per cent. We will have zero growth. So, compared to the rest of the world, Australia is way behind. Let us look at what has happened under the Fraser Government between 1975 and 1980. Over that period the rate of growth of real income per head in Australia

was 1.2 per cent. For the OECD region as a whole the growth rate was 2.7 per cent. In other words, the real incomes of Australians grew in the first five years of office of the Fraser Government at less than half the rate that real incomes grew in the OECD region as a whole. That is a pathetic record. By way of contrast, let us look at what happened when the Labor Government was in control of this nation. In Australia between 1972 and 1975 real income per head of population increased by 1.9 per cent and the increase for the OECD region as a whole was 1.2 per cent. Real incomes in Australia relative to those in the rest of the developed world increased quite substantially in the period of Labor's control of this country; but in the first five years of the Fraser Government's control of this country they ran at less than half the rate of real income growth elsewhere. That is why Australia is becoming a relatively poor nation.
Australia is sliding down the list of living standards in the Western world. Year after year, under this rotten Government, our nation is becoming poorer and poorer. This is something that I am sure the Australian people will be insensed about when they become fully aware of it. It is an alarming fact that we are sliding rapidly down that income scale. In 1950 Australia was the fifth richest country in the world in terms of income per head. In 1979 it was the sixteenth richest of the Western countries, not including the oil rich countries such as Kuwait and Saudi Arabia. I am talking just on comparable Western countries. We slipped from fifth to sixteenth in that 29-year period. During that 29-year period we had a Labor government for a miserable three years, so honourable members opposite cannot blame us. In our three years of government incomes increased faster than they did in the rest of the world. It has been in the period of conservative governments-the 26 years that they have inflicted themselves on the Australian peoplethat we have become a poorer and poorer nation. The slide is going on as indicated by the growth figures that I have given for 1982-83. We will become much poorer relative to the rest of the world in 1982-83. The slide will continue as long as this Government controls the Australian nation and deadens its economy with its contractionary policies.
If we continue on this path, by the year 2000 countries such as Singapore and Hong Kong will be richer than Australia. We will become relatively poor in our own region. That will be a hell of a shock to the Australian people. But that is the

\036 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

path we will be on-markedly so-unless different policies apply, unless we have a new government which can promote economic growth, increase incomes per head and provide jobs. This Government can do none of those things.
The Government claims that this Budget is a family Budget, that it is a fair and workable response to the very difficult problems that Australia faces at present, that it is an anti-inflationary Budget and that it is a Budget to achieve voluntary wage moderation. All of those
claims are false. It is none of those things. Let me go through each of those claims. The first is that it is a family Budget. The Government claims that there are massive benefits for families in this Budget. That is a totally false claim. The fact is that the impact of the Budget makes most families no better off or even slightly worse off than last year and those that are better off are only slightly
so.
The Treasurer claimed in his Budget Speech that a typical family, which he described as being one with a dependent spouse and two children despite the fact that only 20 per cent of taxpayers claim the spouse rebate, nevertheless taking his example, a taxpayer on a $300 income and paying a mortgage of $25,000 at an interest rate of 13t per cent will be $16.84 better off. He got that in
various ways. I will not go through all of the arithmetic. Basically he did it by ignoring the fact that the Government abolished tax indexation. Of course, without tax indexation the proportion of our income that we pay in tax automatically increases. The Government abolished tax indexation which it once proudly trumpeted as being a great reform in this country. It did this so that it could come in with a Budget which gave tax cuts and say, 'There, we are giving you big tax cuts', whereas before it was almost an automatic process. The Government is simply trying to deceive the Australian people into believing that there are l~rge tax cuts when in fact there are no such large tax cuts.
The income tax cuts in this Budget are only slightly more than what would have been there with full income tax indexation. That is the real situation. Tax indexation would have cost about $1,500m, so the proportion of income paid in tax did not increase. The income tax cuts in the Budget are $1,570m, not counting the mortgage interest tax rebate, the dividend rebate or the age pensioner rebate which of course do not go to all taxpayers. If they are included the amount comes to $1,768m-a bit over $1,500m certainly. That difference is swamped by the increases in indirect taxes. The $1,046m worth of increased indirect taxes means that there are no tax cuts. There is

indeed an increase in the proportion of income going in tax as a result of this Budget. So we see that the so-called tax cuts which are going to make families better off are in fact a mirage. Even when one includes the increased family allowances, which are rightly to be taken into account in considering the position of families then, as I mentioned, most families are no better off. At this stage I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard some tables relating to the effect of the Budget on living standards.
Leave granted.

Mr WILLIS-There are four examples in these tables showing the effect of the Budget on different households. Let us take as the first example, the Howard family. this is the family which, as I mentioned, the Treasurer boasted was getting a $16.84 increase. When one takes account of the fact that there is no tax indexation, that the tax cuts apply from I November and that the increase in family allowances does not apply until I November, one sees that a family, such as the Treasurer describes, for the full year 1982-83 as against the previous year, has real tax cuts of only $2.03. That is, the tax cuts that it has in income compared to what it would have had with tax indexation is only $2.03.
When one allows for not only the mortgage interest rebate and the increase in family allowances but also the effect of increased indirect taxes of $4.40 a week one finds on our calculations, which is dividing the increase in income in direct tax revenue by the 4,600,000 Australian households, that that family instead of being $16.84 a week better off, as the Treasurer falsely proclaims, is only $3.86 a week better off. If they did not have a mortgage and rented their home they would be only 48c a week better off because

they would not get the mortgage rebate. That is the real situation for the year 1982-83 as compared to the previous year 1981-82. The figure of $16.84 is a mirage. It is a figure which is arrived at on the bases I have mentioned and by looking at the situation on one day, namely, 1 November 1982 rather than looking at the situation for those families over the whole of this year compared to the whole of last year.
Other family groups have been mentioned by the Treasurer. Example two looks at a two income family with a total weekly income of $400. On our calculations that family is only $1.79 a week better off this year as against last year because of this Budget. If they rented their home or already owned it and were not paying it off they would be $1.59 a week worse off. The third example is for a single taxpayer on an income of $300 a week with no children or home loan. That taxpayer is 20c a week worse off. Example four reflects the Howard family of a taxpayer with a dependent spouse, two children and an income of $300 a week. Let us look at the situation now compared to two years ago. One sees that that family is $3.04 worse off, or if they rented their house, $7.02 a week worse off. So much for this family Budget. This is no family Budget which gives great hand-outs to families. This is a Budget which gives nothing to the majority of families. Let us remember that two-thirds of Australian families are not buying their own home, they either own the home or they are renting the home. So two-thirds of families will not get the mortgage interest rebate. When one takes that fact into account the majority of Australian families will be worse off. I believe that that puts the situation of the so-called family Budget in some perspective. I also seek leave to incorporate in Hansard two tables relating to the position of taxpayers in Australia over the last seven years.
Leave granted.

-To enable comparisons to be made between 1975-76 and all years, these figures treat the difference in family allowance each year and child endowment in 1975-76 as a negative income item to account for the change from tax deductions for dependent children in 1975-76.

Mr WILLIS-I thank the House. I will not go into the details of these tables because of the lack
of time. They simply show that the single taxpayer or a taxpayer with a dependent spouse and two children this year will be paying a higher level of tax than last year. As compared with 1975-76 all taxpayers are paying a much higher proportion of their incomes in tax except for those on very
high incomes. That table certainly puts into perspective the whole issue of tax cuts. It was also an important claim of this Budget that it was a fair
and workable response to the very difficult problems the Australian nation faces at present. In our view it is a highly inadequate response. In the midst of the worst recession since the 1930s the Government has brought in another contractionary Budget that is designed not to create any jobs. It is a deliberate part of this Budget not to create any jobs at a time when unemployment is at the highest level it has been since the 1930s. As a result the forecast is no employment growth. So we are going to get an increase in unemployment officially from 6t per cent or thereabouts to over 8 per cent as a result of the increase in the work force. The rise in the deficit does not mean that this is an expansionary Budget which in some way

responds to the needs of the nation to have a more expansionary policy.
The increase in the deficit which is in this Budget is a result almost totally of the recession which this Government's policies have forced upon the Australian economy. Last weekend the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) said that the Government was looking at about a $1 billion increase in the deficit as a direct consequence of economic conditions. He was saying that because the economy was recessed, we will get a much lower rate of growth of taxes and we will have to payout a lot more on unemployment benefit. Because of those two factors we were automatically having an increase in our deficit, because of the recession that these policies have brought on this country, of $1 billion. By how much did the deficit go up? It went up by $1.1 billion. So almost all of the deficit increase was attributable to the recession and not to any change in government policy. This is only a very slightly less contractionary Budget than the one which we had last year-a Budget which forced us into deeper recession. This Budget, clearly on the face of the Budget forecast itself, will force us even deeper into recession. We have to ask: How long will we go on with these crazy policies which are pushing us

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1039

deeper and deeper into recession and to higher and higher levels of unemployment? There has been no change in policy. As a result of that fact we have no tax cuts. The tax cuts mentioned in the Budget total $1 ,812m. The tax increases in the Budget total $1,916m. Taken overall there are no tax cuts and there is very little increase in real expenditure. The 13.9 per cent increase in expenditure is taken up by maintaining the real level of outlays per head of the population and the automatic increase in payouts and unemployment benefits because of the high level of unemployment benefit recipients.
This is clearly a Budget which does not try to do much about the economy. It still has a domestic surplus. As a result of this we have the most contractionary policy applying in the Organisation for Economic Co-operation and Development, in any Western country, at present. That is what this Government is inflicting upon us.
The Government claims that this is an anti-inflationary Budget. It has made such claims many times before. That statement is totally false, as it has been on previous occasions. This is a Budget which increases indirect taxes by over $1,OOOm. On the basis of the Treasurer's own figures given to this House in March last year, a $500m increase in indirect taxes equals a one per cent increase in the consumer price index. So this Budget will add 2 per cent to the consumer price index. It is a hell of a strange way to fight inflation by increasing prices directly by 2 per cent.
The Government also claims that this is a Budget to achieve voluntary wage moderation. The Budget does no such thing. As I have already indicated, there are little or no benefits to families, there is no employment growth and the Budget has no anti-inflation effect. Indeed, it does the opposite; it increases prices through increased indirect taxes which will automatically increase wage claims. For all of those reasons it is not a Budget to bring voluntary wage moderation. It also has none of the supplementary measures that are needed if the government is really serious about getting voluntary wage moderation. Two of those supplementary measures which are crucially important are: Firstly, that restraint is applied to all sections of the community. The Government should not just say to the wage earners in this country: 'You are the ones who have to restrain your income claims when everyone else can go free and do what he likes.' That is no way to get wage restraint or anti-inflationary policies operating because the unions will rightly ask:'Why should we be the bunnies? Why should we be the ones who restrain our activities when everyone else can do what he likes?'. If this

Government is really serious it has to have restraints on other sections of the community.
Secondly, we have to have a centralised wages system. This Government is going the other way. It is trying to destroy the centralised wages system. It has been fairly successful. But without a centralised wages system how can the trade union movement have a co-ordinated response to government initiatives, if it is going to be operating on a totally decentralised basis? Of course this policy really amounts to a policy of making the market work more effectively on wage claims. It is a policy which is designed to reduce wages involuntarily by the bludgeon of unemployment. This Budget is designed to create more unemployment, to reduce income claims of wage and salary earners and, in that ways to try to keep inflation down. Despite all of that, the inflation rate is still forecast at around lOi per cent-a higher rate than we had last year. What a hopeless Budget strategy all that amounts to.
The appropriate kind of strategy is one in which we seek to achieve growth in this economy by a more expansionary budgetary and monetary policy. We do not seek to use that policy simultaneously as a means of restraining inflation. If one tries to do both all that one finishes up with is the contractionary policies which have destroyed this nation in the last seven years and made Australia a relatively poorer nation year by year than previously. Those kinds of policies are simply passe now. They are not working. The policies which will work are policies which seek to employ prices and incomes policies to restrain inflation, married to a more expansionary budgetary and monetary policy to promote growth. That is the kind of strategy which will work. It is the kind of strategy the Australian Labor Party is developing, and will develop more fully, with which to lead Australia in the very near future.

Quite to the contrary; he has acknowledged that it has. He stated that there were no tax cuts yet he qualified this by stating that they amounted to $1 ,800m. He

stated that there were no benefits for families but he ignored the fact that there is an increase in the family allowance, and interest rate subsidy for those who are paying off their own homes and a special allocation for those who are paying rents. He then spoke about job creation but failed to acknowledge that in the Bicentennial Road Development Program and in the defence budget, about which I wish to talk in a moment, there is a significant contribution.
This Budget ensures that the people of Australia are given a chance, in spite of contracting world trade opportunities and conditions that apply in other parts of the world, without the inflationary impost of those nonsensical policies which the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden) tried to suggest were hidden somewhere in the woods, and about which we were going to hear some time in the never never. Mr Charles-Oh, rubbish!
Mr SINCLAIR-We have provided a Budget which demonstrably has not in any sense been rebutted by the idiotic comments of the honourable member for Isaacs. However, I want

predominantly to identify to honourable members in this House the contents and the parameters of the $4,622m that has been allocated in this Budget for defence expenditure in 1982-83. This represents an increase of 11.8 per cent on the sum provided in 1981-82. It is expected to be about 2.9 per cent of gross domestic product, in line with the target set by the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) of 3 per cent by 1984-85. Except for the $llOm included in the Budget for prospective wage and salary increases, the defence proportion is largely in April prices. It does not include provision for all price increases yet to be met in 1982-83. Provision for these, of course, will be addressed in the context of the 1982-83 Additional Estimates.
Since 1975-76, thanks largely to my predecessor, the honourable member for Moreton, the former Minister for Defence, and the present Leader of the House (Sir James Killen)-he is presiding with some distinction over the deliberations of this place-defence outlays have risen by 148 per cent taking this year's estimates into account, a remarkable increase over those absolutely deplorable figures during the days of the cutback during the Whitlam Labor years. Of course, this is a much higher rate than the rate of infla-
tion. Inflation in the same period, as measured by that glorious economic statistic, the implicit GDP non-farm price deflator, is estimated to rise by about 92 per cent. The defence vote has risen by 148 per cent against 92 per cent.
A further indication of the priority which the Government has given to defence is reflected in its increasing claim on national resources. Defence spending, as a proportion of the GDP, has risen from 2.6 per cent in 1975-76 to an expected 2.9 per cent in 1982-83. Last week I tabled the interim report of the Defence Review Committee. The final report of the Committee is expected later this year. Its interim recommendations have been acted on by the Government to rationalise overall defence organisation and to strengthen defence management. The new porfolio of Defence Support gives added focus to the industry, production, research and purchasing activities associated with defence. My colleague the Minister for Defence Support (Mr Viner), who is also Minister Assisting the Minister for Defence, works in close co-operation with me and within my policy guidance is able to give his undivided attention to those important activities.
Given the industrial difficulties that prevailed in that area over recent years I believe it is a very worthwhile development. Indeed that is one of the problems I see facing those involved in trying to maintain an adequate standard of defence in

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1041

this country. The unfortunate activities of unions at Williamstown is particular have prejudiced even the follow-on destroyer program. That, I think, is a very sad indictment on the preparedness of those who claim they are seeking jobs to provide the sort of work output that is absolutely essential if contracts are to be let in that area. Our two departments are working together to coordinate all defence related activities of the Commonwealth in a single defence program.
This year priority will continue to be given to raising the level of defence expenditure devoted to investment in capital equipment and capital facilities. The 1982-83 defence budget increases the proportion of defence expenditure devoted to capital investment from 16.9 per cent last year to 21.1 per cent this year, with an estimated expenditure for these categories of$987m. This includes $773m for capital equipment and some $214m for capital facilities. On the equipment front, the picture is one of substancial expenditures to fulfil commitments and to fund a number of new projects, including two minehunters, eight mortar locating radars and semi-trailer transporters for armoured vehicles.
Honourable members will recall that my predecessor on 29 April this year advised the House that in 198 I-82 the defence budget received significant relief from reductions in planned payments on purchases under the united States Department of Defense foreign military sales arrangements.
Mr Donald Cameron-Who was your predecessor?
Mr SINCLAIR-A very distinguished gentleman, the present and continuing honourable member for Moreton, mind you, at times he was a jackaroo and one is not sure whether he does not recall those days with even greater favour. Some further relief is expected on this account in 1982-83 and this is reflected in the figures I have just cited.
The sum of $183m of the facilities allocation will be devoted to new construction, providing work for large numbers of local building contractors, tradesmen and labourers in locations all over Australia. There will be substantial construction in the north and north-west of the country, the defence needs of which I was able to assess first hand during an extensive tour of those areas in June. This year will see work on airfields at Derby South and Learmonth, Western Australia, on fuel storage at Tindal in the Northern Territory, and the beginning of the program at Darwin airport. In addition to the construction activity generated by defence expenditure on facilities there are

many ways in which the funds appropriated for defence directly support local communities. I would that the honourable member for Gellibrand stayed in the chamber because he complained that this Budget did not provide any contribution to work generation. I can assure him that the extension of the vote in defence is a very important contributor to work activity in our community.
With small business there is the significance of defence contracts with local firms. Importantly there is also the household expenditure by members of the Defence Force and their dependants. There is the expenditure by defence establishments on food, fuel and other consumables in the areas where they are located. There is too the expenditure by units on local services such as the repair and maintenance of buildings and equipment. In total more than $3.5 billion of the funds appropriated for defence purposes this year will be spent on goods and services in Australia. That is in spite of the fact that we have a major forward commitment in re-equipping the Services with capital equipment, much of which is necessarily still purchased overseas.
We are continuing to maintain restraint on manpower and running costs so that capital investment can be given priority. In 1981-82 expenditure on service and civilian pay and allowances and Defence Force Retirement and Death Benefits was 54 per cent of the total expenditure on the defence function. This expenditure included $230m for the part year effect of additional costs arising from recommendations of the Committee of Reference for Defence Force Pay, which most of us know as the Coldham Committee. In 1982-83 the full year effect of the Committee recommendations will be $300m but overall manpower costs will reduce to about 51 per cent of total defence expenditure.
The Defence Force will continue to be a major force in the labour market. There will be over 7,700 vacancies to be filled over the next twelve months, in the overwhelming majority of cases by young people. The proportion of expenditure devoted to running costs will also decline, in this case to an estimated 26.8 per cent of total expenditure in 1982-83. This in part reflects economies arising from the retirement of aged equipment, more specialisation in flying training and the better utilisation of fuel reserves. I seek leave to incorporate in Hansard a paper setting out details of the major projects and activities which collectively determine the distribution of expenditure in the defence program just outlined.
Leave granted.

The document read as follows-
MAJOR ELEMENTS OF DEFENCE EXPENDITURE 1982-83
Presented to Parliament by
THE RT HaN. IAN SINCLAIR, M.P.
Minister for Defence
The following are the major items of expenditure for 1982-83, and the initiatives which will be taken during the year.
FFG 2. A review of the estimated project cost of acquiring four guided missile frigates from the United States has shown no real cost increase. The estimated project cost at January 1981 prices was $I063m, comprising $879m for the ships and $184m for helicopters. At January 1982 prices the estimated project cost for the four ships is $923m. This difference of $44m in the ship costs reflects changes in exchange rates and inflation only. The proposed expenditure on these ships in 1982-83 is$12Im.
Phalanx 3. Orders have been placed for two additional Phalanx weapon systems for the RAN at a total project cost of about $15m. The weapon provides a fully automatic close-in defence against sea-skimming missiles of the Exocet type. The guided missile frigates HMAS Darwin and HMAS Sydney will come already fitted with Phalanx.
FFG helicopters
4. Acquisition of helicopters for the guided missile frigates is proceeding separately. A short list of contenders was recently announced. The manufacturers of these contenders will be funded to develop options for the design and development of sensor and weapon systems, and to examine the opportunities for Australian industry participation in the project as a whole. The possibility of acquiring a common aircraft to meet the Navy's guided missile frigates and other military roles will be examined.
Light helicopters 5. A contract was recently awarded to the French company Aerospatiale for eighteen light helicopters for the Air Force and the Navy. The estimated total project cost is about $23m and the helicopters will be delivered in 1983 and 1984. Some of the Navy helicopters may be embarked in the guided missile frigates as an interim arrangement.
New tactical fighter 6. $217m has been provided in 1982-83 for progress payments on the new F/ A-18 fighter. This project will continue to demand significant resources in the years ahead.
7. Many contracts involving Australian industry in parts manufacture, assembly and test of the F/ A-18 are already in place. The flow of technical data into factories has started. Construction of facilities needed by industry to participate in the program has commenced. Much of the required machinery and plant has been ordered. Many key technologies will be introduced.
8. Much remains to be accomplished in this enormous undertaking. The task will be difficult and demanding and will test Australian industry. The cost will be high, but the rewards from this investment in self reliance in an area of advanced technology are substantial.
F-lll
9. The enhancement of the capabilities of the F-III force continues. $40m is provided this year for the PAVETACK

target acquisition and tracking system which will enable the F-Ills to use precision guided munitions.
P-3C 10. A contract for ten new P-3C Orion aircraft has now been signed with Lockheed. The project cost was reduced by about $60m to $362m by Lockheed agreeing to buy back the older P-3Bs. The new aircraft will give us much enhanced antisubmarine warfare and surveillance capability through to the turn of the century and, with ships, submarines and other aircraft, add up to a formidable maritime defence capability. $33m has been provided for in 1982-83.
Mirage II. It is planned that $22m will be spent this year on the Mirage aircraft refurbishment program. This includes the acquisition of new and refurbished wings and of advanced short range air-to-air missiles.
Patrol boats 12. Construction of the Fremantle-class patrol boats at Cairns is proceeding on schedule. The sixth vessel of the class was commissioned as HMAS Whyalla in July. It is planned that $19m will be spent in 1982-83 on patrol boat acquisition, with a further three vessels planned for delivery during the year.
Destroyer construction and modernization
13. If productivity develops satisfactorily destroyers of the FFG type will be built at Williamstown Naval Dockyard. Some $18m is to be spent this year on equipment items which have long lead times for delivery. Another $46m has been provided for modernization of Navy's existing ships-the Riverclass escorts and the guided missile destroyers.
AOR-I
14. The underway replenishment ship HMAS SUCCESS, which will replace HMAS SUPPLY, is continuing construction at Vickers Cockatoo Dockyard. $18m is to be spent on her this year.
Minehunting catamarans
15. The development of an Australian-designed minehunter is proceeding. Contract negotiations have started with Carrington Slipways Pty Ltd of Tomago, NSW for the construction of the first two vessels.
16. With these craft, Australia will be able to develop the complex skills needed to counter mines laid in areas of greatest concern, i.e. harbours and their approaches. Provision has been made for expenditure amounting to $12m this financial year.
Minesweepers
17. The Government has decided that minesweepers should be examined that could complement the minehunters. Proposals will be sought in this financial year.
Bridge simulator
18. A contract has been awarded for the supply and installation of a ship's bridge simulator which will be used to train naval officers in ship handling and fleet manoeuvres. Total cost of the simulator and its associated equipment, to be installed at HMAS WATSON in Sydney, will be about $13m.
Army transport 19. Expenditure to re-equip the Army with modern
wheeled vehicles for field use continues. In 1982-83, $32m is to be spent on the medium trucks which are being assembled in Australia, with substantial wider benefits for industry.

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1043

Tenders were recently called for an initial order of light vehicles to begin replacement of the existing Land-Rover fleet. Up to 3000 might be ordered.
Communications
20. Tenders have been invited for the first phases of the Defence Integrated Secure Communications Network (DJSCON), which involve the design of the complete system and its installation in the eastern states. Australian industry will become increasingly involved in this project with some 40 per cent of the principal work of this phase being performed here. Through this project there will be a significant transfer of knowledge and skills in modern computer-controlled digital communication technology from overseas. It is expected that some $8m will be spent on DJSCON this year.
21. Recently, the signing of a contract for $7m to provide new High Frequency and Very High Frequency combat radios for the Army was announced. This is the third phase of project RAVEN, which will re-equip Army with modern radio equipment. The quantity of radios to be acquired has yet to be decided, but up to 11,000 could be produced over the next 10 years at an estimated total project cost of some $200m, depending on the final number of radios ordered.
22. Tenders are also to be invited for the next phase of a project to provide the Army with a Tactical Field Trunk Communication System. This phase of project PARAKEET, as it is known, is for the design and development of the system and it is planned that a contract will be awarded later this financial year.
Training Aircraft
23. It was also announced recently that the Government has decided to proceed with the design and development of a new basic training aircraft. A contract has been placed with the Australian Aircraft Consortium Pty Ltd which has been formed conjointly by the Commonwealth Aircraft Corporation Ltd, Hawker de Havilland Australia Pty Ltd and the Commonwealth Aircraft Factories. Test flying will start in 1985 and production aircraft are expected to enter service in 1988. Expenditure in 1982-83 is $14m of a total estimated project cost of $155m.
Mortar Locating Radars
24. Eight new mortar locating radars will be acquired for entry into service in 1985 so that Army can update its capability in this important field. The estimated project cost for this equipment is $42.7m. $5.5m has been provided for expenditure this year.
Other Acquisitions
25. Other acquisitions to be proceeded with this financial year include semi-trailer transporters for armoured fighting vehicles, engine protection equipment for F-III s, and water purification equipment for the Army.
Definition Studies
26. A number of major definition studies will be fundedfor the new destroyers, for an automated tactical command and control information system, for improved electronic detection systems for the P-3C aircraft, and for airfield radar at the Army Aviation Centre at Oakey, Queensland.
27. Contracts are being let with three Australian companies for funded studies to examine the broad options for Project WALER, which looks for Australian involvement in the development and production of a family of light armoured fighting vehicles to enter service in the mid-I 990s. This is an important project in the context of the Government's policy of increasing self-reliance in Defence.

Manpower
28. Over the 3 years to June 1982, regular military strength has increased by almost 3,000. In the interests of containing rising manpower costs and reducing the proportion of Defence funds allocated to them, this growth will not continue into 1982-83. Indeed, some manpower economies will be possible with the retirement of HMAS MELBOURNE and the RAAF's ageing Canberra aircraft.
29. Navy and Air Force Reserves will increase and the Army Reserve will maintain its strength at 30,000. No growth is provided for in Defence civilian manpower.
Facilities
30. The 1982-83 Budget provides also for continued development of our defence infrastructure, with substantial expenditure on the training and other facilities necessary to support the Defence Force, as well as on those facilities required for direct support of military operations.
31. The support of naval operations will be enhanced by the extensive modernisation of Garden Island Fleet Base and Dockyard, Sydney, where works totalling over $65m have now been authorised and are well under way, and by a large housing program for Navy's new base in the west, HMAS STIRLING, in preparation for the home porting of ships there from 1984.
32. $3.4m will be spent in 1982-83 on replacement of cranes at Cockatoo Island dockyard and continuing development of Williamstown dockyard.
33. Naval operational training will be enhanced by a $1.1 m building at HMAS WATSON, Sydney, for training in bridge duties. At Jervis Bay, work will begin on a $1.9m facility for training in fire fighting and damage control at sea. The importance of such training has been highlighted by the recent Falklands war.
34. Major works to develop the Army's three Task Force bases at Holsworthy, Enoggera and Townsville will continue in 1982-83. Work is also planned to begin on a $17m redevelopment of a base for Army's 16th Air Defence Regiment at Woodside, South Australia, and on a $14m redevelopment of the Armoured Centre at Puckapunyal, Victoria.
35. In March 1983 tenders will be called for a new building to house the Physics Division at the Materials Research Laboratories, Maribyrnong. The building, to cost $2.6m, will provide modern facilities for the Division's laser research.
36. At Bonegilla, near Albury-Wodonga, a new $40m Army Apprentices School will be opened early in 1983. Training facilities for higher Army command will be improved by the development of a new $2.5m complex at the Army Staff College, Queenscliff. Following the Government's initiative to increase the Army Reserve, additional training facilities, costing a total of about $5m, will be provided at Wacol in Queensland, Warradale in South Australia, and Karrakatta in Western Australia.
37. Significant steps towards further improvement of defence infrastructure in north and north-west Australia will be taken with the commencement this year of works on a new $54m airfield at Derby South, Western Australia, which will be capable of handling all types of RAAF aircraft, and with the completion of works to improve the airfield at Learmonth, Exmouth Gulf. Work will also start on a new fuel storage facility which had been planned at the RAAF airfield at Tindal, near Katherine, Northern Territory. The possibility is also being examined of developing Tindal as a base for the new F/ A-18 fighter aircraft, instead of Darwin. A decision on this question should be made in the near future.
38. Expenditure on the structural bonding workshop being constructed at the Government Aircraft Factories will be $9.7m this year.

1044 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

Housing
39. Reflecting the Government's concern with the welfare of servicemen, improvements to the standards of new houses for servicemen were announced earlier this year. A large program of new housing is already under way, and this year it is planned to start the construction and acquisition of a further 600 houses. These will cost about $35m, of which $11 m will be spent in 1982-83.
Activities
40. The resources allocated to day-to-day activities will continue to be tightly controlled so as to make more room in an expanding program for capital items, our investment in the future.
41. Some economies have been achieved through specific action such as the phasing out of the Canberras and HMAS MELBOURNE's aircraft and the specialisation of Mirage pilots in either air-to-air or air-to-ground training. These moves have brought about a small reduction in overall flying hours. But within the smaller total it has also enabled the activity levels of some elements, including pilot training, to be increased.
42. The allocation to navy for fuel purchases has been reduced in 1982-83. This does not imply a reduction in activity levels. Rather, some reserves of fuel held in low usage areas will be utilized, making it possible to increase steaming time as well as to introduce new vessels to service.
43. Planning will be put in train this year for exercise KANGAROO 83, the fourth in a series of large scale ANZUS exercises conducted biennially. Exercising in 1982-83 will be maintained at around the same level as last year, KANGAROO 81 apart. A number of multinational exercises, together with a wide range of single service and joint exercises are being planned.
44. Some specific activities for which provision is made in the Budget allocations are the introduction to service of HMAS Sydney, the stationing in Darwin of three Fremantleclass patrol boats by the end of the year, foreign port visits by RAN vessels and the maintenance of a RAN presence in the north west Indian Ocean.
45. Army will complete its restructuring of the Ist Infantry Division and continue with the reforming of the 2nd Infantry Division. There will be further refinement of the capabilities of the Operational Deployment Force.
46. Provision has also been made to maintain P-3 Orion surveillance in the Indian Ocean at the level to which it was raised in 1980. The Orion detachment at Butterworth will also be maintained. The long term demands which these activities will make are also provided for; activity levels in the training organisation will be increased so that a sufficient pool of qualified aircrew will continue to be available.
Defence Co-operation
47. The Government considers that there is great mutual benefit to be had in Defence Co-operation with neighbouring countries. In 1982-83 Defence Co-operation Programs will involve expenditure of $43m, an increase of $3.3m over 1981-82.
48. Highest priority will continue to be given to Australia's nearest neighbours, Papua New Guinea and Indonesia, with estimated expenditure totalling $17.5m and $IO.2m respectively. Other major recipients are Malaysia ($4.4m), Thailand ($1.9m), the Philippines ($l.3m), Singapore ($l.2m) and nations of the South West Pacific ($5.2m).
Mr SINCLAIR~I am sure that the nation will be gratified to learn that, in spite of generally worsening economic conditions, the steady

progress towards the Government's declared defence goals is being maintained. In the light of decisions announced earlier it is appropriate that I report to the House that the British Government has decided not to make HMS Invincible available to Australia. On 1 June last, in light of British military action to recover the Falkland Islands and the losses suffered by the Royal Navy, the Prime Minister wrote to the Prime Minister of the United Kingdom, Mrs Margaret Thatcher. He volunteered to her that should her Government wish to re-examine the sale, Australia would not hold her Government to the earlier obligation.
The British Government intimated that it wished to discuss the implications of the Prime Minister's letter with us. Accordingly, together with the Secretary to the Department of Defence, the Chief of Naval Staff and the Chief of Naval Materiel, I visited the United Kingdom from 7 July to 15 July. There I met with the Right Honourable John Nott, the Secretary of State for Defence, the Prime Minister, Mrs Margaret Thatcher, and senior officers and officials of the Ministry of Defence. They made it clear to me that as a result of the Falklands conflict there had been a major revision of British defence planning. As a result it had been decided to retain all three Invincible class carriers for the Royal Navy. This would give their fleet a two carrier capability at all times.
It had been our strong belief that when HMS Ark Royal was commissioned, probably in 1986, one of the three Invincible class ships should be available for the Royal Australian Navy. Having in mind the financial constraints which had motivated the earlier British decision to sell HMS Invincible. this seemed a reasonable proposition. However, we were advised quite firmly that only HMS Hermes could presently be offered to us.
The needs that led to the Government's decision to buy HMS Invincible remain. In particular there is the necessity to ensure an adequate anti-submarine capability given the long maritime sea routes between Australia and her principal trading partners. The Government is therefore now re-examining a range of options with a report to be made as soon as possible, and certainly before the end of 1982.
The re-examination will involve a number of ship options, including the possibilities of a new ship of the Invincible class; one of the Iwo Jima class; one of the Garibaldi class, possibly a sea control ship; a conventional carrier of 35,000 to 40,000 tonnes, which of course would be able to carry the FAl8, British and United States proposals to build a simple carrier to merchant ship

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1045

standards, and smaller carriers, including the conversion of existing container and other merchant ships. Honourable members will recall the Atlantic Conveyor. which was sunk in the Falklands conflict, and its conversion by the United Kingdom. The re-examination of the ship options is also taking into account a full appraisal of current and future short take off and vertical landing aircraft developments, and the need and cost of acquiring anti-submarine warfare helicopters. As honourable members would know, particular examination is being made of the performance of the Harriers in the Falklands campaign.
Depending upon the outcome of all these considerations it may be necessary to review the need for interim solutions such as re-commissioning HMAS Melbourne, perhaps even acquiring a foreign carrier such as HMS Hermes, or converting a merchant ship. The commitment of funds to defence and their direction is a product of the analysis of the strategic situation Australia faces. Importantly it is also a product of a continued appraisal of the political and economic circumstances of Australia vis-a-vis the nations in our region.
In this respect the Vietnamese invasion of Cambodia, the continued support it gives to the Heng Samrin regime and the stationing of at least 250,000 regular Vietnamese troops in Cambodia is a matter of concern to Australia and its neighbours. Allied to this is the influence of the Soviet on Vietnam, the deterioration of Vietnamese agriculture and the increasing economic subordination of that country to the Soviet. All are destabilising influences in South East Asia.
In frequency and capability Soviet aircraft and naval movements through Cam Ranh Bay and Danang in Vietnam are identifiable new factors in that country's military presence in the Pacific. These are not the product of any change in the strategic situation of the area but the direct result of Soviet initiatives. With the Soviet record in Afghanistan, Poland, Angola and so many other countries there rightly needs to be apprehension as to future Soviet intentions in our region.
To appraise the position in South East Asia, the Chief of Defence Force Staff, the Secretary to the Department of Defence and I visited a number of member countries of the Association of South East Asia Nations last month. While in no respect can ASEAN be regarded as a defence association there is no doubt that its increasing status internationally and the respect with which its views and attitudes are regarded by other countries of the region are vital elements in any perception of Australia's future security.

Recent ASEAN initiatives towards Cambodia reflect both a shared concern at the continued Vietnamese presence there as well as a reaction to the earlier Vietnamese invasion. They also in part reflect more concern at increasing Soviet influence on the Vietnamese Government. In the defence area Australia's role in helping individual ASEAN members to enhance their independent defence capability through our Defence Cooperation Program is greatly appreciated. This program with its two way benefits is emerging as an increasingly significant part of our national defence effort, encompassing 12 countries in the ASEAN and South West Pacific area. The knowledge, skills, and resources of the Australian Defence Force and our defence related industries have helped significantly in increasing the capability of each of these nations to resist external aggression.
In Singapore the work by the Royal Australian Navy and Royal Australian Air Force advisors serving with the Singapore Air Force has contributed importantly to lifting the standards of air and ground crew. Since this Government decided in 1980 to increase the Philippines Defence Cooperation Program, there has been a significant lift in the number of Filipinos involved in Australian training programs. The Philippines Air Force has been assisted by Australian technicians in developing helicopter engine repair facilities. There, too, a demonstration was given of the Disappearing, Automatic, Retaliatory TargetDART-which is manufactured in the electorate of the Minister for Aviation (Mr Fife). That equipment has been provided to the Filipino Government under DCP. The equipment, together with spares and training provided in association, has been acknowledged as improving the operational capability of the Philippine soldiers.
In Thailand, the front line ASEAN country, our defence assistance has also been greatly appreciated. There we have provided 20 Australian Nomad aircraft to the Royal Thai Air Force.
In Malaysia, we have announced a program of assistance to a major new military project, the Malaysian Army Combat Training Centre at Gemas. Many honourable members will know Gemas as the site of the first major battle in which Australian troops fought while aiding in the defence of Malaya in 1941-42. The most important element of our assistance in Malaysia is the continued RAAF presence at Butterworth. The training and exercise undertaken in conjunction with Malaysia and Singapore and the contribution made by the RAAF were acknowledged both by

1046 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

the Prime Minister of Malaysia and by the Minister for Defence in Singapore.
The redeployment of No. 75 Squadron of the RAAF has already been announced. It will continue on schedule. However, it is possible that there may be some delay in the withdrawal of some units to allow for the build-up of replacement personnel from the Royal Malaysian Air Force. Discussions are to take place with the Government of Malaysia during 1984 with respect to the future of the second Mirage squadron and its possible replacement. I confirmed with the Malaysian Prime Minister that the redeployment of the first squadron is important to enable the RAAF to build up the skills necessary to operate the new FA 18 tactical fighter, the first squadron of which is expected to be in service with the RAAF in 1986.
Announcements with respect to Butterworth and the future operations of RAAF aircraft there will be made after the 1984 discussions with the Government of Malaysia on that country's future requirements. It is also the intention of the Government at least to maintain periodic deployments of RAAF fighter aircraft at Tengah air base in Singapore. P3Cs of Nos 10 and II Squadrons of long range maritime patrol aircraft based in Edinburgh, South Australia, are also periodically deployed in Butterworth on a three month rotation basis.
In any assessment of defence expenditure in the Budget, three matters need to be in mind. First, the Government has not sought to replace equipment affected by cuts announced by my predecessor. To have done so would have reduced future flexibility for decisions to be taken following presentation of the report on the carrier, aircraft and equipment requirements for the RAN to which I have referred earlier. Secondly, in recent years the expenditure has often fallen short of the budgeted defence program. Some account has been taken of this in the level of commitment in planned payments on purchases under the United States Department of Defence Foreign Military Sales arrangement. Thirdly, any appraisal of Defence expenditure must be made against a background consideration of Australia's strategic position and future Force development. A full analysis of this, I have not attempted.
However, later in the session, I will be making a statement covering Australia's strategic position, the future defence program and defence management. I commend the Budget to the House.

Mr SCHOLES (Corio) (9.14)-The Minister for Defence (Mr Sinclair) started his speech by making certain references to how beneficial the Budget will be to Australians. I return to the Budget. The Minister talked about job creation in the defence areas. The authorised strength of the Defence Force is reduced by over 700 personnel. That is stated in Budget Paper No.1. The HMAS Melbourne fixed wing aircraft with the Royal Australian Navy and the Canberra squadrons are deleted from authorised strength. Throughout the Budget there are significant cuts in the amounts of money available, such as $10m for the operation of naval aircraft. In overall terms the Budget reduces the funds available for expenditure in the defence area, other than in the manpower area, by a significant amount. In all areas other than manpower the amount of funds in real terms available in the Budget is lower than that provided in these estimates last year. The Government has engaged in a little book juggling, which is now becoming a regular means of justifying itself. The Government, having significantly reduced or underspent last year in a number of

areas including capital equipment, now claims significant increases by using last year's underspent amounts as the basis for projecting increases this year.
I will run through some of the figures in relation to the Budget. These are the real figures. Last year manpower expenditure was estimated to take up 49.2 per cent of the defence expenditures; it actually took up 55.8 per cent. This year the estimate is 54.2 per cent-an increase of 5 per cent in the share of defence expenditure for the manpower element. That reduces the funds available for expenditure in the defence areas on other than wages to around 45 per cent of the defence budget. This is down from the estimate last year of 48.3 per cent of the defence budget. An 11.8 per cent increase in actual terms over the total Budget is, in fact, a 6.5 per cent increase in the defence vote-less than half the inflation rate. This includes all funds available for training, new equipment, facilities, support stores, maintenance and administration. This represents an actual increase of 6.5 per cent against a projected inflation rate in the general community of in excess of 10 per cent. Taking into account the fact that defence costs increase at a greater rate than those in the general community, the increase in defence funds available for non-wages will be less than half of the increase which will take place this year because of inflation.
The capital equipment appropriation in this Budget is down from 16 per cent to 14.9 per cent. Of the 16 per cent allocated in the Budget for capital equipment last year, only 11.69 per cent was spent. In fact, less than 80 per cent of the capital equipment vote last year was actually spent. That is a luxury for a country which is desperately short of modern equipment in its defence forces. In not one year in the entire period in which this Government has been in office has the capital equipment vote actually been spent. The expenditure has been in the high 90 per cent bracket on a number of occasions; on this occasion it is below 80 per cent. That is hardly the performance of a government which has a commitment to the real defence of Australia. In earlier defence statements we had commitments to such Australian projects as the Mulloka and lindalee projects and mentions of a number of other programs which were urgent to our defence requirements. We hear no mention of them now although the Minister has said that later he will make another statement.

Mr BIRNEY (Phillip) (9.34)-Along with millions of fellow Australians I welcome the magnanimous measures outlined in the Budget brought down in this House last week. This Budget will provide relief, assistance and incentive within a responsible economic policy. Australians from all walks of life must face the stark reality that we are living in a world in which economic activity is still extremely subdued and in which conditions of recession persist in a number of major industrial countries. The international recession is with us and is hitting with an everimpacting force. Now, more than ever, we need to take the community into our confidence about economic conditions in Australia. Whilst the present state of this recession can almost be laid squarely at the door of the world economy, the excessive growth of wages in Australia has made matters worse. Australian industries, instead of becoming more competitive, seem to be hell bent on pricing themselves out of business. There can be no doubt about the serious flow-on of these effects on Australian families and on the unemployment prospects of many.
We are, of course, unable to insulate ourselves completely from adverse economic developments in the rest of the world. The economy of the United States of America remains the powerhouse of the Western industrialised world. The economic reality is that strength or weakness in the United States economy has ramifications in national economies all around the world. For example, if interest rates are high in the United States of America and other countries, as they have been, it is nonsense to believe that they can be kept low in Australia. The fact is that we in Australia have lost the competitive edge which we had a year ago on many world markets. The explosion in wages over recent years has seen to that-sadly, to the detriment of so many Australians.

1050 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

In a conscientious endeavour to slow wage increases, sales tax changes were made in the Budget. The Government bore in mind the proposal of the President of the Australian Council of Trade Unions, Mr Dolan, that unions would be prepared to moderate wage claims if income tax cuts were granted. This is precisely what the Budget has done. The tax-free threshold now climbs from $4,195 to $4,595 at a cost to revenue of almost $800m. The Government has hearkened to the cry from all over Australia as a result of a well-orchestrated campaign for reduction in direct tax and, as a consequence, the standard rate of tax of 32c in the dollar was reduced to 30c and the point at which a taxpayer commenced to pay 46c of each additional dollar was increased from $17,894 to $19,500. The full cost of taxation cuts in a full year is a mighty $2,567m. It is a matter of extreme regret that Mr Dolan, together with the Leader of the Opposition (Mr Hayden), did not come out openly and state the apparent fact that without increases in sales tax it would have been impossible to adopt the measures of which I speak and which will benefit millions of Australians.
It is patently obvious to even the most casual passer-by that Mr Dolan must have been speaking with tongue in cheek when he described the Budget as horrible. Was this Budget horrible to the men and women who were prepared to lay down their lives for the nation, when the Government brought in so many initiatives which were to help in their financial security and that of their families? Amongst the initiatives of which I speak is the payment of the disability pension at the 100 per cent general rate for veterans who received a pension for pulmonary tuberculosis before 2 November 1978 or who, before that date, lodged a claim which was successful, and which is to be restored from 6 January 1983. From II November 1982 any disability pension received by a veteran will be totally disregarded as income when assessing the level of service pension and eligibility for fringe benefits or increased pensions for amputations, loss of vision and many other benefits too numerous to mention.
Is this Budget horrible to home buyers who will have their mortgage interest rates effectively cut-many by more than 2 per cent-as a result of the announced housing assistance measures which are part of a major $726m housing package, including new interest rate subsidies, first home deposit assistance grants and higher direct housing assistance to those in need? Does Mr Dolan think this Budget is horrible to all home buyers who will be eligible for the new rebate which will cover all taxpayers buying the home they occupy? Do he and the Leader of the Opposition really believe

that this Budget is regarded as horrible by the 440,000 aged pensioners with small incomes who will benefit substantially as a result of pension changes?
The Government, ever mindful of the needs of our senior citizens, has brought down magnificent measures which have been welcomed by pensioner organisations. It has decided to lift by a third the amount of extra weekly income that people can earn without affecting their full rate of pension. The changes to apply from November this year will increase the allowable limit from $20 to $30 a week for single pensioners and from $34.50 to $50 for married couples. Does the Australian Labor Party regard this initiative as horrible? It is incredible that the Labor Party would so regard these initiatives when an estimated 430,700 pensioners will get higher pensions as a result. About 16,000 who are not entitled to a pension will become eligible for small part pensions.
I and many millions of fellow Australians applaud measures in the Budget that recognise, with deep and abiding compassion, the real needs of pensioners. These are reflected in the provision of a tax rebate of up to $250 a year. Allied with the Government's increase in the tax free threshold to $4,595 a year, or about $88 a week, the new rebate permits a pensioner to have a total income, including pension, of $5,429 a year. I am delighted that many of my representations on behalf of my pensioner constituents have proved so successful. Never let it be forgotten that the Fraser Government was the first government since Federation to bring in automatic indexation of age pensions. This initiative will be reflected in substantial pension rises in November. The single age pension will increase by $3 a week to $77.25 and a married couple's pension will increase by $5.20 a week to $128.80. When fringe benefits are threatened a tremendous amount of worry and concern is felt amongst our senior citizens and the Government's intention to make the income test more generous in this area will alleviate that concern. In this regard, the current limits of $40 a week for a single pensioner and $68 for a married couple will rise to $54 and $90.
There are other wonderful benefits to our senior citizens too numerous to mention here. The total package is an outstanding example of the care, concern and compassion that the Government generates towards our senior citizens. I have come to the conclusion, without a great deal of deliberation, that the only ones to whom this Budget is horrible are members of the Labor Party. They have seen that the Government's compassion and economic wisdom, as mirrored in the Budget,

Appropriation Bill (No.1)

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1051

have made their chances of electoral success fade like a dream. On Budget night, as the Treasurer (Mr Howard) announced his wide-ranging measures, it was interesting to look at the faces of Opposition members. It was quite incredible to see the startled looks on their faces. Almost to a member their mouths dropped open in unbelievable dismay. They were bedazzled by its generosity. They rushed to the conclusion that because this Budget gave so many benefits to such wide-ranging and far flung sections of the electorate this was, to use their own phrase and not that of the Government, an election Budget.
This Budget is deemed by the Labor Party to be an election Budget, whatever that might mean. If it means that because of its framework people are contented, that good will has been exhibited to all and that it is a Budget to which the endorsement of the people is given in approval then so be it; it is an election Budget. The Opposition calls it an election Budget because its ramifications reach out and benefit many millions of Australians. In short, if it is an election Budget it is thus because it reflects the wishes of the people. It will remain an election Budget with their continued endorsement this time next year, or whenever. It is a matter of common sense that the great benefits to all Australians contained in the Budget, irrespective of what is said here tonight, have the obvious endorsement and imprimatur of Labor Opposition members for the simple reason that they regard it as an election Budget. Whilst a few months ago they were eager and itching for an early election this Budget has frozen any such desires. They know that if an election were fought on the merits or demerits of this Budget they would be annihilated. The Labor Party members are playing every piece of legislation with a straight bat, frightened to leave their creases in case they get stumped. It is an incredible about face but such is the case. The Budget has wrought havoc in their ranks. Whereas before they were ecstatic, now they are crestfallen and running scared of an early election. Such has been the impact of the Budget.
I believe in giving credit where it is due and one of the truly great initiatives of the Fraser Government was the introduction of family allowances, indicative of its awareness of the additional costs that all parents undertake in bringing up children. No less than two million Australian families will receive a long overdue further recognition. The rates of family allowances for the first and second child will be increased by 50 per cent effective from November 1982. The rate for the"first child will be increased from $15 a month to $22.80 a month and the rate for the second child from $21.70 a month to $32.55 a month. A family with

two or more children will therefore gain by $18.45 a month. The cost to revenue will be $375m in a full year. The increase in family allowances as I said, has been long overdue and it will be warmly welcomed.
No one should ever again call into question the sincerity and compassion of this Government towards the people of Australia. The Government, in its deliberations, has indeed heeded the needs of the sick, the aged and the infirm. This is reflected in the fact that total expenditure on social welfare is estimated to increase by $1,774m or 15.4 per cent to a colossal, staggering $13,272m in 1982-83. This amount includes assistance to the families of which I have just spoken and families on low incomes. The Government has decided to introduce from May 1983 a tax free family income supplement of $10 a week for each dependent child of a low income family not in receipt of a social security type pension or benefit.
Over the years this Government has been the subject of severe criticism from individuals and organisations who have called into question its commitment to the provision of day care services for children. This Government cares for the welfare of Australian families and, as part of its commitment, the Government has allocated $70m for expenditure on child care services for 1982-83, an increase of $22.7m or, put simply, almost 50 per cent on last year's expenditure of $47.36m. What is most gratifying is that, in addition, the States and the Northern Territory will receive $33.09m as a contribution to pre-school education under the Children's Services Program. The Government will continue to support existing services and, where necessary, increase grants to maintain their viability. It is of importance to note that an amount of $6.86m has been specifically earmarked for this purpose. In this area alone many other financial initiatives have been announced but are too numerous to mention in the short time available to me.
At the expense of reiteration, can this Government ever be accused of being blinkered and oblivious to the needs of so many of the undeprivileged in the community? The answer is a resounding no, and this answer is already reverberating throughout the nation. Its assistance to the handicapped and the provision of accommodation for the aged and the disabled stand out like beacons that negate any such unfounded propositions. It has stamped itself indelibly as a humane government that does care. In the latter area alone additional capital funds ar~ to be made available for aged or disabled persons' accommodation and indeed over the next two years $170m will be provided for the program, with $83m being

I052 REPRESENTATIVES 26 August 1982

Adjournment

allocated in 1982-83. In addition, $37m and $14m will be spent over the next two years on capital facilities under the Handicapped Persons Assistance Program and on senior citizens centres.
A particular interest to many of the students in my electorate of Phillip will be the substantial increases announced in the Budget to all student assistance schemes. They have been made quite plain in the Budget and will benefit all those students, some 12,000 or more, who otherwise would not have received such financial help. This will kill off the many false and malicious representations floated in my electorate by the Labor Party over a lengthy period to the effect that this Government had chartered a course whereby, amongst other things, fees would be reintroduced in universities and the Tertiary Education Assistance Scheme allowances would be decimated.
This is a Budget that has something for all. I am not one who will join with Labor's prophets of gloom, doom and despair who would have us take ourselves over the brink and into the self-fulfilling forecasts of economic stagnation. Ours is a Liberal government, a party which believes strongly in private enterprise and initiative and in the ability of individuals to meet challenges and to seize opportunities and then turn them to advantage. That is what this Budget is all about.

Mr HOLDING (Melbourne Ports) (9.54)~1 wish to raise on the adjournment a matter which I think touches very seriously on the future of the defence of this nation. Today the House had before it a very important report from the all-party Jomt Committee on Foreign Affairs and Defence which dealt with the issues which were considered by that Committee in relation to a request for the purchase of an aircraft carrier. I do not propose to take the time of the House going over the recommendations of that report except to say that the House ought to note that the recommendations were unanimous. At the same as that report was being presented representations were also being put to ~he Minister for Defence Support (Mr Viner) by a widely-based delegation representing all the union, white collar and blue

collar, in the aircraft industry. The delegation raised questions on the future of the aerospace in-
dustry and its role in the future defence organisation of this nation. That arises as a result of what I believe was yet again one of those short-sighted decisions taken by the razor gang~the decision to cut back in the production of Nomad aircraft.
The importance of that decision, taken and announced virtually overnight, to the industry as a
whole is such that 43 workers will be retrenched from the Government Aircraft Factories at Fishermens Bend in Victoria on October 1982, with a total of 370 jobs to go by mid-1984 if the Government adheres to its present policy. Australia's other two main aircraft manufacturing plants, Hawker de Havilland Australia Pty Ltd and the Commonwealth Aircraft Corporation Ltd, also manufacture major components of the Nomad and will be adversely affected by present policy with about 100 smaller companies involved in parts manufacture and supply. The problem with these retrenchments is that we are retrenching skilled tradesmen, skilled white collar workers, upon whose technical capacity the future of Australia's role in the aerospace industry must depend.
Of course, one of the points that has been made by Government and Opposition spokesmen was that Australia, in terms of defence policy, must be self-reliant. It is perfectly true that one can buy modern and sophisticated fighter and bomber aircraft, but as was shown by events in Iran one can have the most up-to-date and modern aircraft in the world but if one finds oneself short of the capacity to maintain and keep them flying then they have a very short life indeed. The cessation of the Nomad production will threaten Australia's capability to manufacture a whole aircraft. The Nomad, whatever its deficiencies and strengths, is an aircraft totally designed and manufactured in Australia, and is a total line production. Simply to cease that without making any adequate arrangements for the continuance of the art, for the continuance of the capacity to maintain a skilled work force, and to retrench men and say 'Well now, you go off and find jobs elsewhere and we will hope that at a later point, perhaps two years from now when the FI8 comes on line, we will have the capacity', is to be extremely optimistic. It is important that we maintain these skills in this area. To abandon those skills and to abandon a work force that has taken years of training to produce, carries very serious defence implications. The delegation was strengthened by one of the recommendations contained in the report of the Katter committee which said that the Government should proceed forthwith with the purchase

The Aboriginal people in Queensland do not want a confrontation. I have attended some of the meetings of the black protest committee in Brisbane. So if there is any violence at the Commonwealth Games in Brisbane-it would be a tragedy for all Australians and all the Aboriginal people of Australia if that were so-a good deal of the fault must be laid at the feet of this Government for not facing up to its responsibilities.
