Mr FREE (Macquarie) (10.15)-1 wish to raise tonight a matter which has been drawn to my attention by prisoner trainees serving sentences at Her Majesty's training centre at Emu Plains in the electorate which I represent. It is a rather interesting situation. It is interesting because it involves persons serving sentences under Commonwealth law, as indeed may some of the officials of the Western Australian branch of the Liberal Party in the near future for tax avoidance. Therefore this may interest previous speakers. As I understand it, since early this year a program has operated in New South Wales prisons called the Work Release 2 Program, which has allowed people imprisoned under State law who have established a good record in prison to obtain a release before the parole period. They have been able to go out of prison and be assimilated into society under State law. It applies only to those

people who have been imprisoned for State offences. People in the same institution who have been imprisoned for Commonwealth offences are not eligible for early release under this system. That has created a great many problems because, as I understand it, there are State and Commonwealth prisoners in this institution who have been imprisoned for broadly the same offences but a trainee who is there for a Commonwealth offence is not eligible for this early licence whereas one who is there for a State offence is. This has created a great many problems with morale. In fact, I had a letter from one of these trainees. I would like to read part of it so that it is in Hansard. I will not identify the man. He states:
I am currently a Commonwealth prisoner at Emu Plains Training Centre, serving a sentence of three and a half years with a non parole period of eighteen months. During the ten months of my imprisonment, I spent two and a half months at Long Bay Gaol, where I worked in the hospital, after which I moved to Emu Plains. Since my arrival here, I worked in the kitchen, beginning as the butcher, which is my trade, and progressing on to head cook-my current work situation.
He has had a good record so far in prison. The letter continues:
Recently I lodged an application to be considered for inclusion in the works release II programme, which is designed for low risk prisoners with a stable family life. A parole report was prepared by means of a parole officer doing a report on my home life, and work reports were submitted by the overseer of my work location. Both reports were satisfactory, my work and conduct reports being excellent. Consequently, the Board decided, on the 16th day of June 1982, that I was to be accepted to this scheme. I contacted my wife and children, and told them. I would be coming home. Now, two weeks after my acceptance the State Government has decided to annul this scheme and release those on the programme on licence.
He is not quite correct there. What happened was that they discovered he was a Commonwealth prisoner. He continued:
This has been a very traumatic experience for my family. My wife is undergoing extreme financial difficulty, and now a terrible disappoint for my children who were expecting me home. Because I am a Commonwealth prisoner, I am not eligible for a special licence, so I must remain in prison.
Myself, along with seven other Commonwealth prisoners in this institution, feel that there is a large amount of discrimination against us in respect of licences. The eight Commonwealth prisoners in this camp, all hold responsible positions. It is very frustrating for us to see our state counterparts, whether drug or multiple offenders being released after serving three months only, with lesser work and conduct and work reports, and also without very good prospects upon their being released.
I would very much appreciate it if you could present my case to the Attorney-General, and make him aware of our predicament in N.S.W. gaols.
I presented that case to the Attorney-General (Senator Durack) on 7 July. All I have had back is an acknowledgment. I know the Attorney General has had his problems in recent days. I

Mr O'KEEFE (Paterson) (10.19)-The worst drought for 40 years has caused great problems in New South Wales. Tomorrow a total of 51 pastures protection board districts out of 58 will be declared drought areas. This must be a record. It has been reassuring today to hear the Prime Minister (Mr Malcolm Fraser) mention in this House that arrangements are being made for the various State Ministers of agriculture to meet with the Minister for Primary Industry, Mr Nixon, to work out and ascertain ways and means of alleviating the situation for those people who are affected by this very severe condition. Small businesses in the country areas are in great distress. Business in these towns, particularly in the wheat belt, is at a standstill. Farmers are unable to meet their accounts. This situation is being felt very much by business concerns. The numbers of stock, both sheep and cattle, coming forward to the saleyards are very high indeed. Farmers and graziers cannot hold their stock on their properties owing to the severe drought conditions and have to market it. This is a very serious situation.
Not only are pastures bare but also water is scarce. Our dams are at an all-time low. Domestic water on properties is scarce and if this drought continues this will present a real crisis. Indeed drinking water and house water on many country properties has now been exhausted. The owners of these properties are using water from their shearing shed tanks. How long will this water last? Water for stock is scarce. Farm dams are drying up. In many instances stock is dying, particularly cattle. Sheep are able to live longer than cattle because they will nibble the short grass whereas cattle require long grass. Numbers of both sheep and cattle are down. While travelling around the countryside I have observed that thousands of lambs are being born at the present time. Nature has a great way of replenishing stock. Unfortunately many of these lambs, twins and triplets, which are arriving all over the countryside are being lost because the mothers do not have sufficient milk to feed them. The ewes are just walking away and leaving these lovely little lambs in the paddock. What a loss to this country; what a loss to the agricultural industry.

Wheaten hay and oaten hay is practically non-existent at present. Lucerne hay has become very pricey and is available only in certain areas of New South Wales. It will cost primary producers millions of dollars to buy fodder to keep stock alive in the hope that the drought will soon end. There is no sign of this taking place. Warm days and blue skies are the order of the day.
Very little wheat has been sown in the north-west and this is the hard wheat growing area of this country. About 20 per cent of the normal wheat crop has been sown. Some of the wheat has come through but it is wilting under these dry conditions. Some wheat has been sown dry and, of course, the grain is still in the ground. Other wheat has been sown in moist conditions and the grain will rot as it does not hav sufficient moisture on the top to bring it through. It is a desperate situation. In addition, summer crops such as sunflower and sorghum will not be sown unless we get rains. Generally these crops supplement the farmers who are unable to sow wheat. At the present time conditions are such that the summer crops will not be sown.
Massive assistance is required from governments and the banks. We,hope that the banks will come to the party and help the primary producers in this situation. There will be a very serious effect on the export income of this nation if we do not get wheat, meat, wool or livestock for export. I hope that the powers that be will do all that they possibly can to help the farmers, those who are on the land, and those small businesses in the country towns that are feeling the effects of these drastic conditions.

Postal Rates
(Question No. 4304)
Mr Holding asked the Minister for Communications, upon notice, on 20 April 1982:
(I) On what date, recently, did Australia Post raise certain of its postal rates.
(2) What rates were increased, and what was the (a) monetaryand (b) percentage increase in each case.
(3) On what date did Australia Post provide post offices with details of the postal rate increases for public distribution.
(4) Was there any delay between the increase in postal rates and the availability of details to the public; if so, why.
Mr N. A. BrownThe answer to the honourable member's question, based on advice from the Australian Postal Commission, is as follows:
(I) 22 March 1982.
(2) Charges for ten services increased. Details follow:

(3) Limited supplies of leaflets for public distribution, setting out details of the more important increases, were provided progressively to post offices from 15 March 1982.

(4) Although the leaflets were forwarded progressively over the period 10 to 18 March to State administrations for distribution to all post offices, because of administrative delays, some post offices did not receive them until after the increases took effect. Post office staff were provided with details of the increases, however, to enable them to provide customers with appropriate information.
Operation of Visual Display Units: Department of Home Affairs and Environment
(Question No. 4428)
Mr Les McMahon asked the Minister for Home Affairs and Environment, upon notice, on 21 April 1982:
In respect of the Minister's Department and statutory authorities within his jurisdiction, (a) what steps have been taken to make operators of visual display units and screen based equipment aware of the need for a sufficient break from operating this equipment, having regard to the nature of the task and the need for involvement in substantially different activities during the break, as per Public Service Board memorandum 81/418 entitled 'Operation of Visual Display Units and other Screen Based Equipment'; (b) what percentage of their working day (i) are operators expected to spend on operating duties and (ii) do operators spend on other duties and (c) how many keystrokes per 7 hour 21 minute day (i) are operators expected to complete and (ii) do operators actually complete.
Mr McVeigh-The answer to the honourable member's question is as follows:
(a) All supervisors are aware of the need for operators of visual display units and screen based equipment to take rest breaks to prevent excessive fatigue and ensure that all operators are aware of this requirement.
(b) (i) Because of the large number of applications of visual display units and screen based equipment and the different duties of the operators there is a wide variation in the time spent on operating duties. Across the whole range of operators it varies from 20 per cent, where the operators perform other duties up to lOOper cent for word processor operators.
(ii) see (i) above.
(c) (i) Because of the wide variation of tasks involved within my Department and each statutory authority expected workload is generally determined in terms of output rather than keystrokes per 7 hour 21 minute day.
(iii) In most instances no record is maintained. Where records have been maintained the wide variation in the results suggests that keystrokes per day may not be a reliable indication of workload.
Service Pensioners
(Question No. 4449)
Mr Scholes asked the Minister representing the Minister for Veterans' Affairs, upon notice, on 22 April 1982:
What is the estimated cost of extending the entitlement for a service pension to all Australian ex-servicemen and women by extending eligibility to (a) all who volunteered for overseas service and (b) all who served overseas, but not in designated war zones.

Answers to Questions

26 August 1982 REPRESENTATIVES 1059

Mr Thomson-The Minister for Veterans' Affairs has provided the following answer to the honourable member's question:
(a) There is no precise information available about the numbers of veterans who volunteered for overseas service.

On the limited information available it is estimated that the additional cost of service pension would be in the order of $8Imayear.
(b) Reliable information about the numbers of such veterans is not available. An estimate has been made that the increased expenditure would be in the order of $1 Om a year. These estimates do not include medical treatment costs.
